CSC
The Manufacturer and the
University
The Red Page
Robert Kennedy Duncan, Professor of In-
dustrial Research and of Industrial Chem-
istry at an American university, is the most
fertile of the writers who are translating to
the world the meaning of the revolutionary
discoveries and inventions of to-day. The
New Knowledge and The Chemistry of Com-
merce flooded the world of intelligent readers
with the wonderful white mjgic of the great
magician chemists of this age; and though
Some Chemical Problems of To-day (Har-
per) is merely a collection of disparate es-
says, it is a stimulating and not too techni-
cal work.
In “The Prizes of Chemistry,” Duncan
gives an astonishingly varied list of discov-
eries yet urgently waiting to be made which
will certainly afford a fortune to the man
who makes them. The number of things
that this modern world wants and can’t get
and is willing to pay handsomely for is im-
mense. And the number of things that
science does not know practically anything
about, such as leather and glue, indicates
the avenues of investigation that would cer-
tainly prove profitable to any young chemist
seeking a life-work.
In the chapter entitled, “The Whither-
ward of Matter,” the author argues in favor
of the new theory that “the elements of
matter are not eternal, but temporal, that
there exists in every form of matter the
process of its own decay, and this suspicion
is gradually becoming crystallised into be-
lief, into a new philosophy.” The solution
of the problem probably hinges on the
question of the disappearance of the alpha
rays from radioative elements. For, fly-
ing out from through matter, at a certain
distance, at the very culmination of their
powers to affect the detectors —photographic
plate, phosphorescent screen, etc.—they dis-
appear.
The explanation is that below a certain
speed we have no means to detect these par-
ticles, and “were ordinary substances, mat-
ter of every kind, emitting these particles
at any velocity below this very considerable
pace, they would be wholly beyond the power
of present-day apparatus to detect.”
There is therefore a strong presumption
that ordinary matter is continuously emit-
ting these particles, that, in consequence, it
is undergoing degradation, and that we are
m ignorance of it only through the limita-
tions of our apparatus. Finally, it leads
us to think that the only essential differ-
ence between radio substances and ordinary
substances lies in the velocity of the par-
ticles they eject.
And as, through the radio-activity of the
materials of the earth, there is continuall
being evolved an amount of heat far in ex
cess of the earth’s loss of heat by radiation
and to keep its temperature constant, Dun
can begins to suspect “a fatally determined
acceleration of this disintegrating dissolu
tion of matter,” which can only end in th
glorious burst-up prophesied in the Bible.
Jt
Duncan calls attention to a new theory o
the beginning of things, the Planetesima
Hypothesis of Prof. T. C. Chamberlain, o
the Chicago University, which has begun to
take the place of Laplace’s Nebular Hypo
thesis, still taught, we believe, in Australian
schools. This theory, upon which most o: !
us have been brought up, supposes that the
solar system begins with a nebula, which
slowly cooled and contracted, parting
with rings of matter, which ultima
tely, and in order, broke and collected in
to spheres, forming our globe and the other
•planets in turn, each to cool and in the end
bear life. The new theory begins with
collision between two stars—the “partia
impact”. of Prof. Bickerton of Maoriland—
giving rise to a spiral nebula —the commones :
type of nebulae in the sidereal system. The
catastrophe would .fling out large masses o::
fragments, which from collisions as they
revolved would gradually collect aboui;
the larger fragments; and with every in-
crease by accretion the larger lumps would
have a stronger attraction for the smaller
fragments. These platetary nuclei woulc
become planets. But these planets began as
small lumps of cold and solid matter, not
globes of flaming gas. But how did these
chilly planets get warm? Partly by the
pelting action of the colliding fragments
but most by the immense compressive
forces of the earth’s own gravity de-
veloping as it grew ever larger and larger,
until it culminated in an era of vulcanism.
Not that the earth ever became an intensely
heated body. The objections to Laplace’s
theory answered by this new hypothesis
are too many for detailed enumeration here;
but the new theory is hereby commended to
those who lose sleep at nights worrying over
what happened before anything happened
at all.
Duncan points out a remarkable similarity
between the elements contained in living
matter and those contained in sea water —-
in which wet place it is believed life on
this globe began.
Sea-water and air comprise the substance
of man; but there is more than this to it;
there is a relation between sea-water and
living matter in the actual relative propor-
tions of the elements that comprise each.
A comparison between sea-water and the
serum of the mammal shows an astounding
identity in the percentage of sodium, cal-
cium, potassium, though sea-water contains
a greater percentage of magnesium. The
correspondence is remarkable. But chemists
advance proof that when the oceans were
first formed from puddles of condensed water-
vapor, the percentage of magnesium dis-
solved therein was exceedingly small—as it
is in the serum of the mammal to-day.
The conclusion seems almost inevitable
that we have actually in our veins, fixed by
heredity, the water of that early pre-Cam-
brian ocean in which the remote ancestors
of man first assumed a closed circulatory
system. But this is not all. The inorganic
constituents of the solid tissues of our
bodies, the ash of muscle, and such like,
vary widely from the constituents of the
liquid blood-plasma that we have been con-
sidering. One remarkable characteristic of
living tissue is the great preponderance of
its potassium content over its sodium.
Now, there is a great deal of evidence going
to show that in the earliest sea there ex-
isted also this potassium preponderance....
We see, then, that not only did the air and
water at the beginning of things contain in
contiguity the elements of living matter,
but that these elements did actually unite
to form this living matter.
Most fascinating is the chapter dealing
with the trend of chemical invention —a
record of miracles; while the story of the
revolutionising of camphor reads like a
drama.
The most fruitful portion of the book,
however, is a detailed statement of the sys-
tem of industrial fellowships obtaining in
certain universities of the United States—a
system that cries out to be set in motion in
Australia. In America, thanks chiefly to
the efforts of the author, the manufacturing
companies co-operate with the universities in
a scheme for the adequate solution of im-
portant manufacturing problems. The ar-
rangement benefits the manufacturers, the
chemist, and the university. The American
manufacturer —also the Australian manu-
facturer—is inefficient “sometimes, it is safe
to state, to the extent of 50 per cent, of the
value of his product.” He —unlike the Aus-
tralian—is confessedly inefficient, and desires
help. He gets it by founding a fellowship
for the study of the problems bothering him.
Take a few actual examples: Here is one
firm which gave a fellowship on enamel-
lined tanks —£260 per annum, for two years,
with a bonus on results. Six months be-
fore the expiration of this fellowship, the
manufacturers took the two chemists into
the corporation, because they had succeeded
in constructing an enamel which lies beauti-
fully on steel, which is of extraordinary re-
sistivity, and which affords good promise of
being adapted to large-scale application.
Also, they have helped to develop a sub-
stance which is not glass or enamel at all,
and which seems remarkably well adapted
for use as a coating for steel tanks. Another
chemist, holding a fellowship on the chemical
treatment of wood, at ,a salary of £3OO,
within a year had devised and developed a
process for the improvement of the finish
on wood, which, while brilliant and resistant
to all ordinary reagents, is 43 times harder
than varnish. He already has patents pend-
ing—for the firms offering these fellowships
always allow their chemists to profit in .a
fair degree from any of the discoveries made
in their service. Other fellowships include
such diverse subjects as: The chemistry of
bread, the utilisation of the constituents of
buttermilk, the utilities of borax, the preser-
vation of orange juice, and one, of £2400 a
year, with 49 per cent, interest in the re-
sults of the work, on the abatement of the
smoke nuisance. The results from these
inquiries are available to the public three
years after the termination of the fellow-
ship, though patents can be taken out at
any time. The fellows give certain lec-
tures in the university.
The university which does not devote it-
self to research, both pure and applied, is a
dead limb on the tree of our civilisation, for
without research it can neither teach nor
can it be of service.
There must be innumerable problems,
almost peculiar to Australian manufacture
and industry, that the establishment of such
fellowships would incalculably benefit. Can
our manufacturers afford to stay outside
such a profit-making arrangement? And are
our universities somnolent enough not to see
the advantage that would accrue to them by
encouraging the perplexed manufacturer to
come to them for expert help? And how
many young trained chemists, with a desire
for research, have we at present with nothing
to look forward to but a barren professor-
ship?
+
A neat little description of Maoriland is
New Zealand, by Robert Stout and J. Logan
Stout (Cambridge University Press). The
authors state that there are more Baptists
than Jews in God’s Own Pawned Country,
but “there is no Australian colony that has
so great a proportion of people of Scotch
descent.” As there are no Australian
“colonies” left, this is quite likely.
When War Comes to Us
A book containing the fruits of the study
of naval strategy by the world-renowned
author of The Influence of Sea Power On
History cannot help being of remarkable
value —and of a value inestimable to Aus-
tralia, whose situation and naval inferiority
lay us open for the practical application
of the principles first formulated by Cap-
tain A. T. Mahan (now Admiral of the
U.S. Navy), and adopted by the navies of
the world. Naval Strategy (Sampson, Low,
per Sydney Book Club) deserves the closest
study by every patriotic Australian, if we
are to avoid the fatal consequences of popu-
lar panic in the time of danger. It is a
work that can be read by the layman with
ease of comprehension, for the author suc-
ceeds in making the art of warfare, naval
as well as military, a matter of a few broad
principles, astonishing in their simplicity.
It is in the application of these fundamen-
tals that the ability of our naval commander
will be found —or found wanting. Mahan
ranges for his illustrations over the whole
of history, military and naval, from the
Athenian expedition against Syracuse, 415
b.c., to the Russo-Anthropoid war, of which
an intensely interesting analysis is made.
And though the naval defence of Austral-
asia is but mentioned casually, the reader
can deduce from a study of the principles
of naval strategy the parlous position in
which we stand, now and in the immediate
future. For Mahan, being a foreigner, has
no hesitation in stating the facts about the
British Empire, unpleasant as they are, in
the domain that is Mahan’s special and in-
spired study. Thus, he rather despairs of
Gre%t Britain’s ability to hold her premier
navai position against Gennany.
Great Britain has abandoned the isolation
of 20 years ago, stands joined to the Dual
Alliance, and it becomes a Triple Entente.
To the United States this means that Great
Britain... .cannot at present count for as
much as she did in naval questions through-
out the world. Great Britain does indeed
for the moment hold Germany so far in check
that the German Empire also can do no more
than look after its European interests ; but
should a naval disaster befall Great Britain,
leaving Germany master of the naval situa-
tion, the world would see again a predomi-
nant fleet backed by a predominant army,
and that in the hands, not of a State satiated
with colonial possessions, but of one whose
late entry into world conditions leaves her
without any such possessions at all of any
great value.... Although the colonial ambi-
tions of Germany are held in abeyance for
the moment, the wish cannot but exist to ex-
pand her territory by foreign acquisitions, to
establish external bases for the support of
commercial or political interests, to build up
such kindred communities as now help to
constitute the British Empire To-day
central Europe, that is, Austria and Ger-
many, form a substantially united body,
wielding a military power against which, on
the land, no combination in Europe can
stand. The Balance of Power no longer
exists The important point to us is the
growing power, of the German Empire, in
which the efficiency of the State as an or-
ganic body is so greatly superior to that of
Great Britain, and may prove to be to that
of the United States. The two English-
speaking countries have wealth vastly
superio'r, each separately, to that of Ger-
many, much more if acting together. But
in neither is the efficiency of the Government
for handling the resources comparable to that
of Germany; and there is no apparent chance
or recognised inducement for them to work
together, as Germany and Austria now
work in Europe. The consequence is that
Germany may deal with each in succession
much more effectively than either is now
willing to consider; Europe being powerless
to affect the issue so long as Austria stands
by Germany, as she thoroughly understands
that she. has every motive to do. . . .If social
and political conditions in Great Britain
develop as they now promise, the British
Navy will probably decline in relative
strength, so that it will not venture to with-
stand the German on any broad lines of
Policy,, but only in the narrowest sense of
immediate British interests. Even this
condition may. disappear, for it seems as if
the national life of Great Brtain were wan-
ing at the same time that that of Germany
is waxing.
Jt
Wars in which nobody gets hurt, Mahan
explains, do not come within his purview.
Disagreeable things will happen—and they
will probably happen to Australia.
In a military sense, as affecting ultimate
national, safety and victory, it will not
matter if one coast suffer raid, blockade,
bombardment, or capture, if meanwhile the
enemy s fleet is destroyed. With such de-
struction every other loss is retrievable.
So., until our own fleet is able effectively
to dispute the paramountcy of the Pacific,
which is at present a Japanese pond, we
shall have to see Sydney and Melbourne
bombarded, or let off with a huge ransom,
while the British fleet seeks out and de-
feats the enemy—that is, of course, if the
British fleet can afford to let go of its
mother’s apron-strings.
A crushing defeat of the fleet means a dis-
location at once of the whole system of
colonial or other dependencies, quite irre-
spective of the position where the defeat oc-
curs. Such a defeat of the British Navy
by the German in the North Sea would lay
open all English colonies to attack and
render both them and the mother country
unable to combine effort in mutual support.
The fall of any coast position would become
then only a question of time and of the
enemy’s exertions, unless the British navy
should be restored... .On the other hand, so
long as the British fleet can maintain and
assert superiority in the North Sea and
around the British Isles, the entire Im-
perial system stands secure.
This is not to say that a powerful,
although inferior, navy may not by success-
ful _ evasion and subsequent surprise seize
positions in a distant part of the world, and
there, so to say, entrench itself. The ques-
tion then would arise whether the superior
naval State would he willing to endure the
protracted conquest necessary to expel the
intruder.
Suppose the powerful, though inferior,
navy were Japan’s, and we that distant
part of the world, the question for us is
whether' the superior naval state, Britain,
however willing she might be, could come
hither for the purpose of expelling the in-
truder.
Australia, Maoriland, South Africa and
Canada.. . .each feels that, like Minorca and
Malta in their day (which were taken from
Britain by the French respectively in 1756
and 1798) it cannot itself alone cope at sea
with several possible enemies of Great Bri-
tain. ...and while it is probable that entire
subjugation, that is, conquest and perma-
nent tenure, would be too onerous to attempt,
the cession of a particular harhor or district
might he exacted, or other commercial or
naval advantages, as the price of peace....
The British colonies are thus exposed to be
attacked, to the harassment of war, in order
to obtain concessions. Under several pos-
sible contingencies an enemy’s division not
only may reach their shore before a British
pursuit, hut the British may feel it not
wholly expedient to pursue , lest the detach-
ment so weaken the home fleet as to render
doubtful the security of the British Islands
themselves.
The italics in the foregoing passages are
our own. Their emphasis is commended to
any Peace (or Geese) Societies there may
be left in this locality five minutes after the
first shell smashes the central railway sta-
tion and dislocates a few over-crowded
trams.
In emphasising the necessity of a fleet
being concentrated, Mahan fears that na-
tional panic would insist upon our forces
being subdivided.
If the defence of ports, many in number,
be attributed to the navy, experience shows
that the navy will be subdivided among
them to an extent that will paralyse its
efficiency... .Of what use is a navy, if it is
to be thus whittled away? But a popular
outcry will drown the voice of military ex-
perience.
Mahan instances the panic on the eastern
coasts of the United States at the outbreak
of the Spanish war; resulting in keeping a
valuable fleet for defensive instead of offen-
sive work. This panic was voiced by the
national legislature. When war comes to
Australia we shall certainly find Melbourne
and Sydney and all the intermediate surfing
resorts clamoring each for a battleship or
two,,, and the local press will howl for tying
up a cruiser in front of its own doorstep,
while a destroyer will be taken to pieces
and carted up to the Cotter. For, as
Mahan acutely remarks:—-
Panic, unreasonable apprehension, when
war begins, will be found in the same per-
sons who in peace resist reasonable pre-
paration. “It is better,”. said a British Ad-
miral of long ago, “to be frightened now,
while we have time to prepare, than next
summer, when the French fleet enters the
Channel.”... .Every proposal to use a navy
as an instrument of pure passive defence is
found faulty upon particular examination.
And, anyway, there isn’t enough water
in the Cotter to buoy a submarine mine.
Said the man who knows the ways of
women subscribers to a lending library: “A
woman chooses a novel by the first sentence.
Here are some opening words that invari-
ably make the average novel-reader remark
sweetly: ‘Thanks; I’ll iake this one’
“Then, there is no hope for met ”
Her silence was his answer.
“Mother , I am ruined!”
“And I, love you,” he stammered.
“That,” said she, “I do not believe.”

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Six Delightful Girls.
I am in love—no boast, my dears,
With six delightful girls,
In bumpers full I toast my dears —
Red rubies, gems and pearls!
To wed with one and lose the rest
Would surely break my heart.
Undoubtedly I’d choose the rest.
And let the one depart.
I am perplexed-polygamy
Seems fit and proper now,
The cold world calls it bigamy....
There’d be a howling row !
You’ve puzzled since the Fall, my dears.
And we went wooing you;
I cannot wed you all, my dears,
Alas, what may I do?
I drove with Enid yester eve,
By Luna’s ’trancing lanes ;
Her taunting lips confess’t her Eve,
When Cupid tugged the reins;
Yet, while we kissed, another girl’s
Ripe lips were haunting me:
I doubt the absent other girls
Are half as coy as she.
When blessed with one I’m jealous of
Five charming absentees,
Though her desires I’m zealous of
And her light whimsies please,
I wish for wizard’s power, my dears,
But Heaven grants me none;
In less than half ah hour, my dears,
I’d weld you all in one!
How dear were Bribe’s laughing lips,
Allied with Enid’s eyes;
How tranced were Madge’s chaffing lips
Allied with Freda’s sighs;
How darkly-fair would Mary’s seem
If blent with Mimi’s curls;
Were I as wise as fairies seem
I’d make you one, my girls!
I’d blend the warmth of Bribe’s kiss
With Mary’s saintliness.
At last I’d know how Hebes kiss,
And Spartan maids caress!
Where one girl’s sweet perfections end,
Another’s charms begin-
What bliss, did my deflections end
Above one perfect chin !
It baffles me! To choose the one
Would lose me five sweet wives,
So sadly I refuse the one.
Goodhap in other lives —
When I give up the ghost, I mean —
I’ll wed the six dear girls
Merged into one —-the host, I mean,
Of these delightful girls!
N.S.W.
W.M. WHITNEY.
The Sun Over the Fore-yard.
The sun mounts o’er the fore-yard.
Rouse, boys ! Rouse !
“Splice we the main-brace” now in glad carouse.
And drink to Beauty’s eyes from this bright can—
The tar’s own loving-cup—to bouse ami Ik,. „
Meridian sunshine, truly; passing hot;
The liquor’s gone. . ..yet. . . .still survives the pot;
How blest we all before we swigged the lot.
While expectation (tip-toe);,'[smiled and sighed
For what was coming, and; What now is not.
So passes noon upon the sun-smit main.
I would it now were only noon again !
When grog descending on the soul like rain
Brings dreams of Moll and Bet and Poll and Sue,
With just another driblet left to drain. ,
And yet the odor’s left (to fly too soon).
The refrain of the cup in afternoon,
The fading sweetness of a passing boon
In memory cherished; treasured in the soul
Like the last radiance of a setting moon.
And, still from day to day, while lasts dear life,
In lazy calm or elemental strife,
Or ’cross the Line with Neptune and his wife,
Or south out-past Kerguelen’s mists and ice,
The air’s with memories-meridian rife.
Yes ! Time must come ; our day is dying out,
When men shall tell, aboard strange craft, no doubt,
Of ships that “wore;” or went “in stays” about,
And took the sun at noon, and served out grog,
What time all maids were fair ; all hearts were stout.
Meridian’s round again ! I’m glad ’tis true !
There’s grog in show’rs descending <on the blue ;
With yet some kindly drops for me and you ,
To drink oncfc -more to Moll -and Bet and Suri,
Who hail us criming, as the anchor’s cast
And wave as we unmoor.... Adieu ! Adieu !
VICTORIA.
G. G. McC.
Where, the Curlews Call.
Where the pale-plumed pampas sway
Silver' in a/wan moon’s wake.
Hear the curlews crying low,
In the.-reeds that fringe the lake.
Where the shadows thickest fall
Hear a rustling, then a call.
Like unto a painted scene,
How the gaunt pines stirless, lean,
Silhouette''! ‘gainst the night
* Black’ as >jet. The Waning light
Of. a disappearing moon,
Silvers river and lagoon.
Sphinxlike in its mystery
Lies the Mallee’s great Scrub-Sea,
Rippling in moon-hollowed waves.
Till the far Dream-water laves
Shores of cities strange, it -seems,
As I lie here weaving dreams.
Gentle night around me throws ,
Her soft cloak, moon-edged, while blows
O’er the river a faint breeze
From the rippling Mallee-seas;
Like a painting by Corrit
Yon lone sapling leaning low.
Where the pale, plumed pampas sway
Silver in a wan moon’s wake.
Hear a rustling, then the call
In the reeds that fringe the lake.
(Lone land, where the Curlews call
Still you hold my heart in thrall.)
VICTORIA.
LINDSAY RUSSELL.

THE EMPTY BREAKFAST TABLE.
There has been a general rise in the price of bread. The delay and disappointment in regard to the Argentine crop are the control-
ling causes, but the coal strike has hastened the decision to raise the price of The loaf. — Cable.
The natural consequence of England’s Free Breakfast Table.

By sending direct to Tiie Bulletin Office Twenty-
Six Shillings sterling you can have The Bulletin
posted regularly to you every week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communica-
tions to be addressed to William Macleod, Manag-
ing Director.
How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking
some care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the
form of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse,
Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, SOOO words, are specially accept-
able), Paragraphs, Letters or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS.
when the address is specified and stamps are en-
closed. But no MS. will be returned, or preserved,
unless these conditions are observed. When MS. is
forwarded without stamps .for return it will be un-
derstood that the copy is to be destroyed if not ac-
ceptable. Personal applications for return of MS .
cannot receive attention. The Bulletin desires,
further, those sending MS. to distinctly understand
that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea,
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i,e,, any pic-
ture which has to be re-drawn—-will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.
The Bulletin is forwarded direct to agents on
account of Messrs. Gordon and Gotch (wholesale
agents) to Victoria, West Australia, Queensland,
Tasmania and New Zealand; and on account of
Messrs. Atkinson and Co., and Rigby, Ltd., Adelaide
—at J//6 per doz. nett.
Copies of The Bulletin can be obtained in Lon-
don, after the arrival of each mail, from Messrs.
Gordon and Gotch, Ludgate Circus.

THURSDAY, MARCH 21, 1912.
A KAILWAY TO THE TERRITORY.
The silly multitude which believes that
evei’ything must be immediately brought
to Sydney, whether there is room for
half of it in Sydney or not, had a
day out last week, with the assistance of
State Minister Griffith, and the Tory
dailies, and other institutions. There was
a deputation, and the deputation made the
same noise that a monkey does when lie is
looking for a flea on his tail in a tree top.
It chattered at length about the necessity
for immediately building. a railway from
Sydney to Port Darwin, in oi-der to fetch
»the trade of Port Darwin ‘to Sydney, and
Minister Griffith cordially approved, and
the S. M. Herald backed up the idea, and
the Telegraph applauded, and other horrors
and businesses gave their moral support.
The proposition rested mainly on sentimen-
tal considerations. A line from Port Dar-
win to Sydney would be very much longer
than one from Port Darwin to Adelaide. It
wouldn’t be' so much a transcontinental line
as a coastal one. Instead of opening up
the Territory—which is Federal property
and must be developed with Federal money
—as much as possible, it would cut across a
corner of it only, and make all possible
speed to get out of it. It would avoid the
most promising mineral lands and hasten
into the intensely dry, low-lying and non-
mineral country of south-west Queensland —-
that region where an itinerant pedagogue
looks alter 137 children scattered over an
area of something like 63,000 square miles,
and where the rainfall gets down at times
to about 2in. per annum. And it would
end at an overcrowded port which is
further from Europe than any other im-
portant coastal city in the Commonwealth
except Brisbane. Still the sentiment or
conviction that everything should be dragged
instantly to Sydney was firm in the miuds
of the deputation and its supporters. For
Sydney is Australia’s biggest city, and it is
the doctrine of Toryism that the grease should
be poured over the fat hog; that every effort
should he made to bring all new trade and
population to the place where there is most
trade and population already; that the pion-
eering spirit is accursed ; and that any effort
to push business where there isn’t business
already is a ridiculous attempt to follow in
the footsteps of Columbus and Cook and
other fools.
* * * *
A side-issue of this business is the appar-
ent conviction of the Congestion party that
there is no future before Australia. Thus
it wants to drag everything to Sydney, under
a fixed assurance that there won’t be enough
people to go round, and that, if it doesn’t
grab every possible person for Sydney the
great Ma city will lose the only chance
there is between now and Judgment Day.
By the same process of reasoning the ‘Mvo-
cates of a railway from Sydney via Bourke
to Western Queensland and the Territory
devote themselves to amazing lies re the
alleged disadvantages of the straight line
to which the Commonwealth is committed —
the line from Port Augusta to Port Darwin.
Having a crazy creed to the effect that this
great continent will never, under any cir-
cumstances, be able to support more than
one railway from north to south, the Con-
gestionists of the eastern States are filled
with a desire to get possession of that lone
and solitary railway, or if they can’t get it
they want to prevent the hated land of Cen-
tral Australia getting it. Consequently
they devote themselves to trying to con-
vince the community that most of Australia
is such a rotten place as to be quite unde-
serving of a line. In the desperate struggle
for an argument they even assure the com-
munity that if the transcontinental highway
is built from Port Augusta to Port Darwin,
Rockhampton will be 4084 miles by train
from the great Northern Territory port,
whereas it would be only 1950 miles from
that place if the railway was built from Syd-
ney. This, after all, is much the same thing
as proving that Sydney is 1300 miles from
Parramatta, provided you go round by Mel-
bourne.
* * * -It
There is no proof that the whole
prospect and destiny of Australia lies in
building the shortest possible line from
Rockhampton to Port Darwin. But as-
suming that the connection is desirable,
there is no reason why the lines that ex-
tend westward from Brisbane and Rock-
hampton and Townsville should not be car-
ried further westward to connect with a
railway through the middle of Australia as
well as with one through the eastern
States. Nor is there any reason why
there should not be a railway from Mel-
bourne to the Gulf of Carpentaria, connect-
ing with all the eastern ports on the way,
as well as one through the middle of Aus-
ti’alia. To those people who regard Aus-
tralia as not quite dead it seems feasible
that there should also, one day, be a rail-
way connecting Albany or Esperance Bay
with the Far North, and that the Townsville-
Cloncurry line should be extended west till
it strikes the coast of Westralia. The silly
idea that Australia has nothing ahead of it—
that it will never support more than one
track north and soutli, and that if “we”
don’t secure the end of that track now “we”
will be eternally lost —is retarding the pro-
gress of the country all the time. The
Australian Government is pledged to build
the l’ailway up the centre of the continent.
That was the agreement upon which the
Territory was handed over to it. The
eastern States, quite naturally, want con-
nection with the No-tli. And on the foolish
assumption that there can never to all
eternity be more than a single connecti■m,
their advocates proceed to heap insane de-
nunciations upon the scheme to which the
Government is pledged. The South Aus-
tralians have a simple and obvious answer ;
they handed over the Territory on certain
conditions, and if the conditions r-e ob-
served the agreement will be null and void,
and they will be entitled to resume posses-
sion. Then the eastern States may clnmor
in vain for their railway. Meanwhile the
Australian Government, not being able to do
anything without aggravating somebody,
does nothing at all. From its own point of
view it is right. It can’t build the central
line without losing votes, unless the Con-
gestion crowd recovers the senses it never
had. It can’t build any other line without
losing votes. While it doesn’t build anything
it doesn’t necessarily lose any votes in the
immediate present. 1 In time, of course, its
neglect to do anything may infuriate every-
body, and lose votes all round. But that is
a matter for the future.
* * * *
The battle of the routes turns on the
question .'whether Australia should try to
maintain the old pioneering spirit, or
whether it should abandon it and settle
down to be a shop and office jobber in a
town. The advocates of the line throtxgh
;the centre of Australia want to live up to
the character which the heirs of a nearly-
empty continent should possess. They
want to carry the railway through the great
mineral belt of the Territory, and dig up
another Bendigo and another Kalgoorlie.
They expect to make great cities of the
termini at Port Darwin and Port Augusta,
and thus drag population and progress fur-
ther out into what are now the lonesome
places of Australia. They recognise that
if the iron highway up the middle of the
country is built, the lines that now stretch
inland from the east coast must be carried
westward to join it, and then half of Aus-
tralia will be opened to settlement and
civilisation. Of course Australia isn’t the
best-watered and most fertile country on
earth, and its centre isn’t as good as the
centre of Europe, but it is the only country
we have, and we must needs make the best
of it. On the other hand, the advocates of
the Sydney-Port Darwin line hold that we
should keep on concentrating population and
trade where there is most population and
trade already. They want the line through
Central Australia abandoned for good and
all, which means the definite abandonment
of Central Australia. They don’t regard
Port Darwin as a possible great independent
centre, and a place from which population
may spread out to the vacant spaces beyond,
but as a mere feeder of Sydney. They
nominate Sydney as the place which ought to
be first connected with * the Territory, and
as the natural outlet for the Territory’s
trade, because, having already more trade
than any other place in the Commonwealth,
it is least in need of new trade. The row is
mostly between the pioneers and the im-
porters—between the men who like to sleep
beneath the stars and the men who like to
live in terrace houses in Paddington, and to
go to their jobs per penny tram in the
morning, • wearing bowler liats and moored
to small black bags. And the advantage
of numbers, and votes, is with the importing
people of the bowler hats and the small
black bags.
A RE-ORGANISED SUFFRAGETTE
MOVEMENT.
“llenry Horsecollar” writes :
1 am weary to death of the ways of these London
suffragettes. They are so monotonous, with their
vvindow-breakings and their shoutings and their occa-
sional throwing of stones—so monotonous and so
humorless. It is all a waste of good possibilities.
I take it that the suffragettes are anxious to draw
attention to their case; that they have no scruple
about the destruction of private property so long as
they draw attention; yet that they don’t want to go
to gaol if it can be avoided. Also they have abund-
ance of self-sacrificing helpers and abundance of
money. In such a case they should abandon the old,
well-trodden paths where the policeman is always
looking for them, and seek new roads and devices.
They should never do the same thing twice —or if
they do it twice they should do it at long intervals.
They should never break out twice consecutively at
the same place. They should keep the police
moving, and the public amused and filled
with wild curiosity as to the next hap-
pening. It should be their aim to contribute to
the gaiety of nations, and then their doings would be
chronicled far and wide, whereas a policy of mere
window-smash threatens to become a weariness.
They require a male leader—a Chief Jester —and a
corps of male auxiliaries, and then they could get
to work in real earnest.
* * * *
Consider the possibilities of one simple protest!
They might send round agents to hire for a short or
long term almost every empty house in London.
The sudden rush of business would gladden the
house agents and make the landlords rejoice. In
the poor neighborhoods they could pay a small cash
deposit; in the better-class ones they might give a
bogus cheque ion account. On the eventful night
every suffragette would he camped . in an empty
house. Then at 11 p.m. precisely, they would
turn on every gas jet and water tap (having previ-
ously plugged up the sinks) and go home. Next
day the lunatic asylums would be full of insane
house agents, and the plumber Vote would go solid
for woman’s suffrage, because the profession would
be so darned glad at all the work it had secured.
Of course an infuriated and disguised policeman
would be stationed for a week afterwards outside
every house agent’s place, and he would arrest house-
hunters wholesale on suspicion. But that would
only make the next move easier.
* * . * *
If the suffragettes must have window smashing
04 their programme, it should be done anonymously,
at night, with the pea-shooter. A pea-shooter com-
mands a good wide radius, and if a multitude of
upstairs front offices were hired, and all the suffra-
gettes pea-shooted simultaneously, they might break
half the most expensive windows in London inside 10
minutes, while the police were away attending to 16
bogus fire alarms. Where offices Weren’t available,
suffragettes might 1 get access to ordinary business
premises by hiring themselves out under the name of
Jenkins— a respectable old female with a chronic
toothache —-to 1 do office-cleaning at. specially low
rates. This move would probably do a great deal to
secure the glazing vote. i But probably the best
result would be arrived a't by an effort to meet the
servant girl difficulty. Supposing large numbers of
attractive young suffragettes who could .cook—girls
from the country preferred and with first-class refer-
ences-—threw .themselves -headlong into the domestic.
Bridget market of London, they could easily obtain
access to a great many of the best houses. With
patience and perseverance a time would , doubtless
come when the domestic staffs of Ministers and . other
prominent men consisted mostly, of. these enthusiasts.
And if each of these Ministers and other. Importants
awoke soipe morning to find, all his. clothes gone, his
house front dyed in garish colors and placarded with
Woman Suffrage posters, his chimneys stopped up,
the premises consequently full of smoke, this male
flunkey. intoxicated or missing or locked up, and the
establishment infested with strange cats, he would
become an object of public ridicule. The spectacle
of a Prirr>e Minister fleeing into the street in his
wife’s bonnet, and clamoring amid a great escape
of cats for. the police, would be a subject for the
world’s mirth. Of course the domestic staffs would
be gone, 1 after distributing 10 waggon-loads of
clothing to the poor. ;
■ After that event the foundations of Bull's home
life would be broken up, for the Prime Minister or
other notable would always suspect his new Mary-
anne of being another political conspirator. And
if, when he was giving a dinner party
to many other ' people of social tonnage, he .
found a horse’s leg, with the shoe attached,
in the soup, ; or discovered a complete boiled
cat on his piaffe; and then one of his domestic jewels
suddenly broke a bottle of sulphuretted hydrogen on
the floor, and fled, locking the door behind her, he
would know that his worst fears were realised. And
if, to avoid a repetition of this trouble, he
took to dining at restaurants he would identify
the suffragette waitress by the hot soup she acci-
dentally spilt on him. But there would be no reason
to adhere too exclusively to the domestic side of the
case. Assuming that there could be found a few
hundred ladies of the “Votes for Women” persua-
sion, who are prepared to take risks —and appar-
ently that would be an easy matter —-it would be a
pleasing- novelty if, for instance, every important
train in the United Kingdom was stopped at the
same moment by a lady pushing for all she, was worth
on the communicator. With the block system there
would be no risk, but some thousands of railway
officials would curse simultaneously, and it would
dawn on Bull that he was up against . a well-
organised carflpaign. The dawn would become
broad daylight if the philanthropic ladies
who ran the new Dogs’ Home suddenly
turned all their live-stock, 4000 strong, into the
street, each dog being dyed blue, and then left the
premises by the back door without paying the rent;
and if 40 heavily-laden lorries broke down simul-
taneously in four of London’s main thoroughfares—
-10 in each thoroughfare. And supposing, just at
that moment, the 100 prize-takers in the suffragettes’
free beer-drinking competition emerged from some
premises close by, the police would reckon that they
had their hands full and say it was their busy day.
These things might be arranged to happen on the
anniversary of Mrs. Pankhurst’s arrest, so that they
would amount to something like a celebration.
A campaign of joyous frivolity such.. as the one
faintly outlined would naturally divide itself into
two sections. The general movement, including the
blue dogs and the free beer-drinking competition and
the insane house agents would be intended to impress
the nation at large. The special movement would
be directed against influential opponents of woman’s
suffrage. As far as possible things would be dong
quietly and unobtrusively. If it was possible to
arrange that, at a given moment, every anti-suffrage
member of both Houses who happened to be out of
doors would have his hat driven bard down over his
eyes, this hat smash would be achieved by a single
individual in each case, and she would endeavor to
he missing before the victim got out of the ruins of
the tile, and turned round. Orders for 800 different
loads of coal to be sent to Ministerial residences on
the same day would be distributed by post, and
would leave no trace behind them. The great object
of the movement would be self-effacement. It
would aim at being a secret society and an unknown
power, and it would endeavor to be everywhere, and
to always do something new. It would be a per-
fectly drilled force; if it even said “Boo” to a Lon-
don policeman it would endeavor to say “Boo” to
every London policeman at the same moment. There
ought to be hopes for a cheery organisation of that
sort. The present disorderly arrangement strikes me
as having no hopes at all.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
All Is Lost but Congestion.
Now and then the financial column of the
daily paper, in its passion for accuracy,
makes a sad hash of the loose eloquence of
the leading article. That was the sort of
thing which happened to Sydney Telegraph
last Friday. It got on to the railway con-
gestion anguish, and the truck shortage, nnd
the engine deficit, and the lamentable
struggles of Johnson’s railway's, and jtfhe
miseries of the wheat grower who, becaiise
of the railway deadlock, finds the mouldy
tail end of his last year’s crop stifi ,on
hand when the head and front of this year’s
crop is coming in. Then, in it= passion
for accuracy as before mentioned, the finan-
cial column reduced the situation for the
last four years to figures
1908. 1909. 1910. 1911.
Engines .. 696 798 872 *903
Carriages .. 1,259 1,325 1,420 I#2o
Trucks .. 12,540 13,649 14,527 15$80
Passengers .. 47,487,030 52,051,556 63,044,271 60,919,*628
Goods (tons) 10,175,389 9,298,929 8,393,038 10,355,*565
These are really interesting figures. Ac-
cording to all ordinary arithmetic they abso-
lutely acquit Railway Commissioner John-
son of any suspicion of lack of foresight in
providing rolling-stock to meet every reason-
able emergency. The supply of new passen-
ger carriages has kept pace with the in-
crease of passengers. The stock of trucks
lias increased enormously, while the quan-
tity of goods that these trucks had to carry
was just about the same in 1911 as in 1908.
In fact, the stock of trucks was increased
in 1909, and again in 1910, while the goods
to be carried were growing less and less, and
the new trucks were, in most cases, much
larger than the old ones. And the stock
of engines was increased even faster than
the increase of traffic seemed to
likewise the new engines were far mpx*e
powerful than the old ones. Yet it is well
known that the N.S.W. railways are in des-
perate straits for want of engines, and that
the difficulty of getting the wheat crop to
market has grown and grown until it has
become more like an impossibility than a
difficulty. Where 12,540 trucks carried a
given quantity of goods with comparative
ease in 1908, 15,680 trucks of greater
capacity, and hauled by more powerful en-
gines, are hopelessly bogged with much the
same quantity in 1911. In its misguided
desire for accuracy the Telegraph’s finan-
cial column supplied the explanation. Dur-
ing the last 10 years, it said —
The average distance which that tonnage of goods
has been carried has increased by nearly 34 p.c.
It is quite a simple explanation. One
Government and another exerted itself to
“promote settlement.” It had to do that
sort of thing in order to live. It pushed
railways further out, and pushed settlers
further out, so the boots and tea and sun-
dries they obtained from ; Sydney had to
travel further, and the wheat and pumpkins
and jumbucks they sent to Sydney had also
to travel further. Now 10 million tons of
goods travelling: (say) 66 miles is one thing,
and the same lot travelling 100 miles is
quite another. As the distance increased
cost increases. Also, more trucks and ;enf
gines are required, for the further they have
to travel the longer they are in getting
back for a new load. And the longer they
are on the road the more do they block the
way of other trucks and engines, and that
makds more delay and reduces the efficiency
of the rolling stock. No doubt this trouble
could be largely obviated by letting goods
for export take the shortest road to the
nearest deep-water port.’ But the insane
policy of Congestion demands that they should
all come to Sydney, no matter how vast
and needless and ruinous the distance fnay
be. The private traders and private land-
lords of Sydney, whose humble slave and
flunkey and maid-of-all-work the McGowen
alleged.“ Labor” Ministry is, demand it So
these curious results have arisen:—
(1) The expansion, of settlement has, by increasing
the distance that goods have to be carried, added
so much to the difficulty of getting to'the only
market the farmer is allowed to use, that now 15,680
trucks can’t carry his-goods neatly so well as 12,540
trucks did four years ago. : "... ' ;
(2) The difficulty-of getting his goods, to market
over the extra distances that the extra 3140 trucks
have to struggle with, has so hampered the producer
that, despite increased \ population -and - increased
settlement, the goods carried on the railways in 1911
were almost exactly -the same as in 1908, : :
(3) Therefore all there -is to show for four years
fuss and progress and lancL settlement is a lot more
worry, a lot more congestion, a lot more expense in
the way of freight, a lot more difficulty jn. turning
the crop into money, a wild whirl of correspondence,
3140 more trucks, and a maddened Johnson.
Regarded as the. fruit of all this energetic
land settlement it isn’t very much, but at
all events the blessed policy of dragging
everything to Sydney has been preserved.
All is lost but Congestion, still, while Con-
gestion remains, there is something to go on
with.'

A Small Upheaval in the Quill-Driving
Trade.
A letter from Melbourne:—
The quietest triumph of unionism that ever hap-
pened in Melbourne was the recent revolt of bank-
clerks. Fifty of the black-coated toilers at the-
Bank of Victoria notified the management, through
their league-leader, that they were determined to-
throw down their tools and knock off work on the
last day of February unless their minimum wage
was increased by 10 “bob” a week —or language to
that effect. The demand was put in the form of an
ultimatum, to be answered by the Board of directors
before the date mentioned, and the Board answered
it in the polite affirmative without unnecessary
delay. The increase of salary was granted all
round, because it was demanded all round. Union-
ism succeeded where individualism would probably
have been rewarded with the order of the sack. The-
idea of one bank-clerk presenting an ultimatum to
the management on his own is too pathetically
humorous Ur words. Such things have been done
in the past, and the result was disastrous to the in-


dividual rebel. The difference between an impudent
demand and a reasonable request is a mere matter
of unionism. When the force of this distinction had
been so strikingly proved by the malcontents at
the Victoria, other clerks at other money stores
started to assert themselves, and within a week the
working exes, of the associated banks had risen
quite considerably. At the present time, a large
number of highly superiah young men, whose parents
and guardians had taught them to regard trades
unionism as a dangerous combination of vulgah
workers, are looking forward to the day yhen a well-
organised strike on a large scale will obtain for
clerical labor its rights, or something approaching
its rights. The “Clerks’ Union” (so-called), which
is the merest pretence of a practical organisation
thus far, should include, of course, all the sweated
employees of banks, lawyers, insurance companies
and the like. Capital of the kind that wears gold-
rimmed glasses on its nose has for ages past been
sinfully profiting by the snobbish respectability of
the slaves of tradition who labored for it. The
Labor party in politics, so far from advancing the
interests of the better educated quill-drivers, has
given them only scorn for scorn. Now that bank-
clerks are offering wharf-laborers the sincerest form
of flattery by imitating their method of arriving at
a fair deal, the old gulf between the unionist wage-
earner and the beggarly “salary”-earner is about to
be properly bridged. Melbourne, of all the big cities
in the Commonwealth, is the paradise of the sweat-
ing employer of “respectable” labor. At the time of
the big bank smash in the aftermath of the boom,
the salaries of even the valuable and indispensable
clerks of many reputable firms were reduced 30 p.c.,
and in some eases the reduction has been continued
to this day. There are clerks in the employ of well-
known professional men who have served them for
30 years without attaining to a reasonable living
wage. They have no union to aid them. They
suffer for the want of the co-operative spirit which
they were taught in their youth to regard as the
main evil of Democracy. Having at last emerged
from their state of political darkness, the careworn
quill-drivers may be expected to arise and demand a
small slice of the rich man’s cake in place of the
crumbs that fall from his table.
The Commissioner from Hull.
Hull is a dingy seaport on the north-eastern
coast of England—a muddy rising place with
a future before it and an insanitary past
behind it. And it has sent out a special
commissioner to tell Australia, in veiled
language, what kind of a fool it is in its
export arrangements. This country is the
greatest wool exporter on earth, yet all, or
practically all, of the steamship lines which
run between here and Britain have their ter-
minus in London, and the wool Australia
sells to Britain is dumped ashore there.
London isn’t a wool-manufacturing centre,
and has hardly any more use for this kind
of cargo than the tropical savage has for an
overcoat. In this business it is a purely
useless middleman. But the sheep’s hair
is landed there through force of habit, and
then it is put on trains and carried at suner-
fluous expense to the north of England and
the south of Scotland, where the woollen fac-
tories are mostly located. In like manner
the Australian butter, meat, apples, etc.,
which go to England, are all dashed ashore on
the banks of the Thames, though a good half
of them would be put on land much closer to
their ultimate destination if they were
despatched to some port further north, such
as Hull or Liverpool. It is reckoned that
if the Australian goods which are consumed
in the north of England were landed there,
instead of being humped to the expensive
port of London, and shoved into the most
glutted city on earth, the saving on fruit
would he fully Bd. a case, on butter about
|d. per lb., and on wool and meat much the
same. The point is one well worthy of con-
sideration, for, when a country sends away
55,000,0001 b. of butter a year, a saving of fd.
per lb. on a fairly large proportion of it
represents, the interest on a very respect-
able public debt. Hull is less than 200
miles from London as the crow bolts, for
English distances are short. Still on the evi-
dence, it appears that Australia could make
a' decent pyramid of money by sending por-
tion of its exports there by sea, and thus
saving needless railway haulage. What it
could make by saving needless railway
haulage at this end would be a much larger
matter. Apparently the idea that all N. S.
Wales or Victorian exports for the United
Kingdom must go via Sydney or Melbourne
at this side and via London at the other is
eating up the value of the wool and the cow
produce at both ends. In fact, it looks as
if the sheep and the cow spent one-third of
their lives paying for needless train ex-
penses. The Commissioner from Hull has
received a cordial reception from the daily
papers in Australia. What kind of a re-
ception would they give to a similar com-
missioner from Portland or Port Stephens
or Twofold Bay?
4
The Great Obstructionist.
The citizen of Sydney rages ceaselessly at
a number of grievances—at the rotten tram-
service with which he is afflicted, at the ab-
sence of a bridge or tunnel to connect the
North Shore with the city side of the har-
bor, and so on —but not one in 20 of him
ever sits down and figures out why such a
state of affairs isn’t swiftly ended. If a
fair proportion of the inhabitants of Syd-
ney had the time to spare from cricket
rows, sectarian discussions, surf-bathing and
the like to consider the political situation
in a rational and independent spirit, there
would presently ascend a howl for the ob-
literation of Party Government that would
be heard for many miles. And a similar
howl would arise on the same subject from
the country districts, always assuming that
the outbackers suddenly acquired the gift
of arriving at conclusions on political affairs
without regard to the guidance of the daily
papers. In truth, Party Government is the
largest lion in the path of progress in the
entire menagerie of State. It is the world’s
champion obstructionist, the most effective
system yet devised to prevent needed things
being done. Every day and every night the
State Assembly is filled to overflowing with
shouts of abuse, purposeless recriminations
and puerile insults. The press devotes
columns to the question of whether Brown,
M.L.A., did or did not declare in 1909 (as
alleged by Jobson, M.L.A.) for lessened
payment to tram-conductors or what not.
The uproar is a purely party concern, the
sole object of the Brown crowd being to
discredit the Jobson crowd, and vice versa.
The State which pays the fomenters of
these idiotic disturbances has not the re-
motest interest in them. It would be much
better for the State if the WADE-ites on the
one hand and the McGowEN-ites on the
other, instead of each eternally trying to
prove that the other ought to be dead,
settled down and attended seriously to the
State’s business. One of the reasons why
the Government of the day prefers im-
measurably to keep up an endless campaign
of bickering and abuse, rather than buckle
to and set about one or other of the big
things that clamor for attention, lies in the
fact that under the Party Government sys-
tem the big things are nearly all as danger-
ous as vague threats and vituperation are
safe. ' A North Shore bridge, for example,
is an extremely risky proposition for a
Government to tackle from the Party tac-
tics standpoint. The Administration which
started it would have to be prepared for the
unloosing of a flood of opposition orators
on the country districts, every one of whom
would argue that in this regard the country
was being shamelessly fleeced for the benefit
of the city. Only the other day O. G.
Wade —himself a pro-Bridger of a sort, and
the representative of a North Shore consti-
tuency—brayed loudly to the above effect
before a wayback audience. The same cry
will be raised, for a moral certainty, against
whatever Government dares to start an un-
derground or other civilised tram service.
The infamous delay in setting about the
work of decentralisation is almost entirely
a question of Party tactics. No Govern-
ment wants to lose Sydney votes by trans-
ferring Sydney trade, or trade that might
have been Sydney trade, to a number of at
present uninhabited and remote ports. This
is a situation where patriotism pulls one
way and self-interest another. Self-interest
lias been winning, hands down, for years
past, and there is no reason to think it will
do any worse in the future. The remedy
for the evil is in the hands of the people
who are groaning, not to say blaspheming,
under it. If they don’t rise up in protest
against an anachronism that is at the root
of half their troubles it is hardly worth their
while to protest at all.
Scattering the Seeds of Death.
From an old town on the rim of Sydney:—
Four years ago, in this N.S.W. town of about
20,000 souls, an attempt was made to awaken in-
terest on the part of the Department of
Health in the question of making' phthisis a noti-
fiable disease everywhere in the State. The ques-
tion arose through the distribution, by means of a
public auction sale, of the disease-soaked impedi-
menta from a house in which a poor body had lain
for months dying of consumption. Strenuous efforts
were made by the local health officer, pinioned and
leg-roped though he was, to stop the sale. The Board
of Health was appealed to. It replied pathetically
that “Nothing could be done, as in the district
phthisis was not a notifiable- disease.” Apparently
nothing has since been done, either, seeing that a
second sale is projected of similar goods, under
similar circumstances. The family whose disease
germs are now to be scattered has been decimated
by consumption. One poor fellow died six years
ago. His brother followed two years back. The
mother was buried at Christmas, and at her grave
the officiating clergyman referred to her devotedness
to her dying children till she fell a victim to their
dread complaint. Incidentally, three State children
have been “sheltered” in this house —a small,
tumble-down place, crying aloud for a fire-stick, with
the bedroom of two of those State children next to
that of the dying woman. I am not desirous of
indulging in bathos or hysteria, and therefore simply
state the plain facts. I don’t want to indict any-
body or any Department; but I do want to waken
somebody up to see how careless of human life
Authority is ; and I want to expose our dreadful folly
in scattering the cruel disease with one hand, while
with the other we grope in our pockets for the
means to fight the enemy of our own making.
The water supply at Stawell (Vic.) comes
in 18 miles from the Grampians, and as the
cash for the scheme was, many years ago,
borrowed from the Government, which bor-
rowed it from someone else, and as Stawell
dodged out of parting up for most of the
cost, it was at one time proud of its asset.
But as it was quite convinced that the per-
manent and reproductive work would last
for ever, it neglected to make any provision
for renewing the works when they wore out;
wherefore the Board is now beginning to
realise that it is in danger of having to do
something that will make it beastly unpopu-
lar. Recently it arose and substituted some
new reticulation pipes for certain old
ones, yet the populace in the higher
portions of the town can now only have a
shower bath " by means of a seltzogene; and
when the populace howls for more moisture
it is explained that the ancient main from
the Grampians is becoming so frail that if
too much water is allowed in it blows a hole
from the inside. Pessimists are predict-
ing that some day a heat wave will melt the
tar that holds the poor worm together, and
then there will be worse trouble than ever.
One way and another Stawell begins to
suspect that even a permanent and repro-
ductive water-pipe won’t last for ever, and
though it managed to do so little payment
in the first case, it can’t see how it is going
to refrain from expenditure in the future.

The fight of the N.S.W. State Government
against the Bank of the People which the
Federal Labor Ministry is building goes on
gaily like an army with banners. In Vic-
toria the tiredest and most prostrate Pre-
mier Australia possesses has been roused
out of bed in the bad cause, and in various
other places money is being poured out with
a lavish shovel on new Savings Bank pre-
mises and other appurtenances of battle.
The position is not especially promising for
the Federal institution at its beginnings.
It will have the Federal banking business,
but apparently most of the State Govern-
ments will prefer, in the immensity of their
cussedness, to do their bit of trade with
Private Enterprise banks. It will get a
certain amount of private business, but that
is likely to grow slowly, for competition is
very keen. And its Savings Bank business
must start, apparently, from the very be-
ginning, amid the fierce opposition of the
State offices. Nothing save sheer lunacy
with straws in its hair can prevent the
Commonwealth Bank being a success, but
its growth promises to be a work of time —
a good deal of time. Just here it may be
mentioned that there is still opportunity
for the Commonwealth to consider the wis-
dom of accelerating things by buying up
some of the very big and very thin joint-
stock ones that lost most of
their flesh in« 1893 and the succeeding years,
though in each case the tall skeleton re-
mains as a gallows might after the dead
depositor was cut down and buried. Then
the Commonwealth could, at a very moder-
ate cost, begin with a ready-made business
connection which would cover every im-
portant towri in Australia, and by compari-
son the State Savings Banks would be mere
financial ants making an unimportant local
fuss. In some cases it would be almost too
unimportant l to be worth calling a fuss. It
wouldn’t even amount to a dust.

“D.” attends a meeting:—
The keynote of the Sydney Anti-Home Rule meeting
was supplied, so far as I was concerned, by a Wowser
divine whom I observed hurrying across George-
street in the direction of the Town Hall at about
8.30 p.m. on Thursday night last. He wore the
regulation umbrella and vast flat hoots, a black
frock suit, which looked as though it had been made
at home, and a James McGowen bowler—a weird
and horrible sartorial combination. He suggested
something archaic, to the extent of being Early Vic-
torian. He looked like one with a tremendous
capacity for purposeless exasperation, the sort of
citizen who is capable of working himself into a con-
dition of wild passion over the continued existence
of Beer, the Vatican and such-like ineradicable cir-
cumstances. He radiated futility and well-meaning
anger in regard to matters concerning which he had
but scant knowledge. The meeting was an accurate
expression of his personality. As I came in the
occupants of the back of the hall were flinging out
Irish bricklayers, hod carriers, etc. The Hibernians
went protestingly, with myriads of red-ribboned Anti-
Home Rulers twined round them like lob-worms. I
asked a whiskered ancient nearby what their offence
had been. “Dommed. fleerin’ and irreverent Papists,”
he answered, with a fanatic gleam in his eye. When
the last of the Irish had been jerked into the street,
the show proper proceeded on its way, and a drearier
concern I never struck outside a Bethel. The
speakers were all either parsons or local preachers,
and, as is the habit of their class, they fired off
volleys of doddering old platitudes with an audacious
air of having just got off a collection of bright
new thoughts.
The inner meaning or real strength of these func-
tions is apparently to advertise the wilting kirks and
chapels and their pastors. Not a single argument
was supplied during the entire evening against the
Home Rule proposals that hadn’t been ventilated
about 4,000,000 times before. If the orators had
read a couple of pages apiece out of the Koran to
the audience they would have done about as much
good or harm to the cause they were advocating.
However, they got a fairly good and dirt-cheap advt.
for their various Gospel-shops, and the modern parson
needs that as badly as the Mysterious Jansen or any
other public performer.

One Rodgers, a sturdy, combative citizen
of Horsham (Vic.), assured Premier Weary
last week that he was voicing “a deep-seated
determination” to insist on the making of a
railway from the Wimmera district to Port-
land, in order that the Wimmera produce
may have a natural port of shipment. If
there are many other local determinators
with the pugnacity of Rodgers, they may
push their point through the passive obstruc-
tiveness of John Weary, who is trying to
shift his responsibility oxr to the Chief En-
gineer for Railway Construction. I ort-
land is no longer a proposition that a btate
Frights Government can avoid, or trifle ’~ith.
It has got to be accepted, or rejected, in the
limelight of public criticism.
The shire council of Tarro (N.S.W.) has
got the first cat’s-paw of what is likely to
become a cyclone very shortly. All over
the State, since the introduction of local
government, the councils have systematically
neglected the main roads, these being not
much used by the ratepayers, whose traffic
perambulates along the by-ways from the
farms to the railway station. As a result
the main roads, which were mostly in good
order when local government came into ex-
istence, have got into a most deplorable
state. The shire council argument is that
money extracted from the ratepayers should
be spent in making roads for the conveni-
ence of the ratepayers. The Government’s
argument is that the shires are heavily en-
dowed, some of them to the extent of 15s.
in the £, from the consolidated revenue,
and that this cash is given them to be spent
on the great highways. But as the shires
have had the handling of the money they
have ignored the Government’s argument.
The shire of Tarro ignored it a little more
violently than some others, and the north-
ern road between Waratah and Hexham
has just about vanished. On this subject
an exasperated deputation of people blew
into Works Minister Griffith’s office last
week and howled with indignation, begging
the Minister to do something. The Minister
said he would, and said it good and hard.
Tarro had received 15s. in the £ endowment,
which amounted to £4OOO, principally for
that road, and had spent only £3OO on it.
He promised he would write to Tarro coun-
cil and tell that body that if it did not im-
mediately put the way in repair he would
do it himself and deduct the cost from the
council’s next subsidy. This means that
the butter is in the fire, and the long im-
pending struggle between the local bodies
and the central Government over the main
road problem has really started. It didn’t
start any too early either. Some of the
great arteries of wheeled commerce are
hardly distinguishable now from the adja-
cent cow-paddock.
According to that luckless cynic, Minister
Beery, the N.S.W. State Government is in
the position of the man who, in defiance of
Marquis of Queensberry rules, sat on his.
opponent when he gat him down, because
there had been much trouble iu getting him
down, and it wasn’t safe to let him up. By
which figure of speech he meant —so he ex-
plained, in effect —that the McGowen Gov-
ernment, having now got a majority, didn’t,
consider itself bound by the pledge which.
Holman gave, that it would dissolve Parlia-
ment as soon as the redistribution of seats;
could be effected. Having got Wade down,,
the party was going to sit on his head till
the last moment. To which it might be'
replied that some people are more particular
than other people as to what they sit on..
Furthermore, the statement attached am
amount of importance to Wade when he is;
up that was, to say the least of it, bad;
tactics. The facts of the case, as set forth;
long ago by the fatally-copious Holman,.
were that the services of Speaker Willis.
were secured on the pledge that the Assembly .
would dissolve and go to the country at the
earliest possible moment. Then, when a
by-election made the Government strong
enough to break its promise, it decided to.
hold on for all it was worth, and it wasn’t
ashamed of being something very like a lie'
and a shabby deception—so long as the decep-
tion paid. Beeby’s attitude was that of a.
man who reckoned that his party couldn’t
afford to go to the country, even for the'
sake of its honor, lest the country should!
prove to be darned tired of it. Out of its.
own jaw is the McGowen Ministry con-
victed.
The busiest court of appeal in the country
never gets a word of mention in the papers
—which may be a very good thing for that,
court of appeal, as a rule. In the first 13
months after Holman had become Attorney-
General in N. S. Wales no less than 345 ap-
plications drifted in to his office to privately
upset the publicly-arrived-at decisions of
various courts. A few of the applications were'
double-banked, but, even so, as many as.
254 prisoners were affected. Two-thirds of
these applications to undo behind closed!
doors what the courts had done in the open,
came through politicians —and that isn’t the-
best feature of an unsatisfactory business.
Innocence is alleged in many cases —im
which event the matter ought, of course, to
go to a court of criminal appeal; but in far
more cases the application to undo what",
the constituted court has done is based on-
thiugs like this (there are dozens of them
“Wife destitute and in ill-health”; ‘‘wife-
and mother dependent on him” ; wife and!
family in need”; “wife ill and unable to.
provide for herself and child”; “wife and’
children in necessitous circumstances.”’
This sort of application doesn’t, however,,
howl for a court of criminal appeal so much;
as it does for a reform of the prison system.
At present, on the stupid plea that tha
prisoner mustn’t compete with the free citi-
zen, he is kept at jobs in which there is no.
money for either himself or the State; and
instead of allowing or rather obliging him
to work for the support of his family, the
unfortunate wife and youngsters either starve
or are thrown on charity, or some friendly
politician is called in to help roll away the
stone from the prison door. It is a bad
habit to get politicians into, anyway.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The principle of a compulsory minimum wage is
a deadly weapon directed at the very existence of
the private capitalist.— London Standard.
Lost, lost, all lost, we can no longer stay,
The enemy is master of the field.
Before this awful weapon forged to slay
Our private capital we needs must yield ;
Wild Socialists uprear their banners red,
How shall Fat flourish if the Lean be fed?
Fat, the triumphant master of us all!
Shall any brave the throne whereon he
sits?
Shall madding statesmen break them from
his thrall?
Shall treason pierce the coal-black of his
pits?
Ho, by the splendid hunger of their sires,
Let Britons guard their ancient altar fires!
The beacon light that led our fathers far
Shone from the holy temple of broad Fat ;
The freeman saw it glowing as a star
What time he grovelled low upon the mat;
It was the Bxdton’s heritage to slave
For aught of alms his lordly master gave !
Shall it be stablished fast and fixed by law
That he who works shall have enough to
eat?
Oh, for the vision that our fathers saw
When Toil was dust beneath its Master's
feet!
Mad to its doom runs down the fatal age
When pampered workers earn a living wage.
The secret of our Britain’s ancient pride
Was hunger gnawing at a people’s heart;
This spread her honored flag o’er oceans wide
And made her mistress of the shop and
mart;
And how shall industry and trade survive
Unless starvation and the Devil drive?
The die is cast, ’tis vain to make a row.
Mad statesmen air their Socialistic creed,
Fat, with a martyr crown upon his brow,
Beholds with pain his murdered country
bleed ;
His Private Enterprises sink and fade
In that sad land where wages must be paid.
Lost, lost, all lost! With wailing long and
drear
.The Tory goes with ashes on his head ;
His sun is set, the darker night is near,
The splendid day he knew is past and
dead ;
But in a million homes the folk are gay
With dreams of breakfast — breakfast every
day !
N.S.W.
GEORGE STREET.
The Passenger’s Prayer.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The discipline among the officers and the Euro-
pean section of the Oceana’s crew was exemplary ; but
the lascars, huddled together, alternately wailed
and screamed, terrifying the otherwise courageous
womenfolk, who awaited their turn in the boats as
patiently as the males.
—Report of collision at sea.
O Lord, pray I have a white crew by when
steel ships meet with a crash,
And lapping, icy waters of dawn come pour-
ing in through the gash—
When our good boat has a sick’ning list and
the other’s lost in gloom,
And half-drowned men come clambering up
from the flooded engine-room.
May there be no blacks to shriek and wail
when women wait for the boats —
No fear-mad wretches to rush and fight for
the first frail thing that floats.
Let there be courage and skill and speed
when Death from the dark bites through
And sloping decks to the sea go down. Oh,
give me a white man crew!
Give me a white man crew, O Lord, when
the ship to her rest has gone,
And feeble boats with the punching sea are
wearily “fighting on” ;
A heart of hope and the will to fight are the
plain white seaman’s all;
But let him be there upon the thwart when
the lifeboats landward crawl;
I want to look in no grey-black face when
the seas are rising sheer
And cravens bend to a strong man’s work
because of the pistol near.
The boat with only the. sea to fight may still
to a landing win ;
But God help the boat that fights the sea
and Fear in a coal-black skin.
N.S.W.
E. FRANCIS.

THE DARK LAGER.
where peace-lovers had secured a great vic-
tory didn’t even fire a shot. In fact, it
hadn’t a shot to fire —so wonderful was the
general blessedness of repose. Poland had
no British Empire to fall back upon, so it
was able to give the peace business a fair
trial, and the trial had hardly started before
there was no more Poland. In its last
moments the country noticed a previously-
overlooked leakage in its theory—it dis-
covered that it is no good loving peace unless
the other fellow loves it also, just as it is
no good loving high wages unless you are
prepared to fight for them, for if you aren’t
prepared to fight you will be very lucky to
get 2s. for a day of 10 hours. Whether these
Maoriland people who are taking a “firm
stand” are really believers in disarmament
and non-resistance, and are willing to accept
the full hazard and responsibility of their
acts, or whether they are merely cowards
who expect the British army and navy to
defend them, and propose to fight by
deputy, it ia difficult to decide. Certainly
every honest movement towards complete
peace and disarmament inside the Bull
Empire should begin with an appeal to the
British Government not to interfere with
its bloated sea and land defence system if we
are attacked. Then we might be an inof-
fensive people, accepting all responsibility
for our own methods, and giving the peace
policy a fair trial—just as Poland did when
it urged Prussia and Russia and Austria to
do the square thing, and was answered in
several languages with a cry that sounded
like “Rats!”
