“S.” on the mystery of Victoria’s Toryism:
It is a singular fact that Victoria, the most com-
pact, and, for its size, the best settled State in the
Commonwealth is, with the exception of Tasmania,
the least democratic. As a rule density of population
means an increase in radical sentiment. Victoria is
the only State which has never had a Labor Govern-
ment. At the last general elections, with only a
monument of incapacity like John Weary to fight
against, Labor lost two members. At the present
time the party is in an utterly hopeless position.
Only once has the State really wakened up, and
that was at the last Federal elections, when the
party captured the squatting stronghold of Coranga-
tnite, and ran Deakin to a nose at Ballarat. Since
then, however, democracy has been in a state of
collapse. Victoria is the one State without a Work-
men’s Compensation Act (even slow old Bull Land
has had this principle in force for several years).
It is the only State where the Labor party, save in
very rare and spasmodic occasions, never tries to cap-
ture a seat in the Legislative Council. This House,
also, is the stodgiest and fattest in the Common-
wealth—except the Horror which appertains to
Tasmania. Victoria invites comparison with Tas-
mania in yet another aspect. They are both ex-
porting population hand over fist. Also, they are
capable of supplying between them more ruined old
towns and wilting districts than the rest of the
Commonwealth put together. Why, in the face of so
many horrible examples, should Toryism flourish on
Victorian soil? So far as the local press goes it is
no more Tory than the Sydney or Adelaide press.
The Education system is as good as anywhere else.
The inhabitants bear an outward semblance of in-
telligence for the most part. The tally of cretins,
imbeciles, degenerates, etc., is no larger than in
other States. What, then, is the answer to the
tragedy ? It is a point I would like to have solved.
♦
About thq Need for a Prodder-
There is a loud call in Melbourne, and in other
Australian cities, for a Prodder or Speeder-
Up, to persuade public persons and bodies to get a
move on. It is more than a year now since P’ran and
Malvern proved the success of their electric tram-
line, and pretty nearly every metropolitan council
has a district crying out for a similar convenience,
and knows it would pay, and has the power to build
it. Yet not one has done anything, except inquire,
confer, consider and so on. So with the electrifica-
tion of the suburban railways. The long-dead
Bent was a very live man when Merz reported on
that project. Now Merz is to report again, and
will probably report once more to his successor when
Weary is dead and buried—at least when he is
buried, for he couldn’t be deader than he is. Another
concern that' is asking for the Prodder’s attention
is the Harbor Trust—the Trust whose harbor mostly
consists of some tin sheds, a tortuous drain and one
dock. After about 20 years’ cogitation the Trust
tentatively opines that the “time has arrived” when
the dangerous wharves should be lighted by elec-
tricity, and an electric tram run along the wharfside
from Spencer-street to the Victoria dock —about 11
miles. The Trust has had the power to construct
such works all along, but it hadn’t the energy, and
it never will have, to all appearance. It admits
that the line ought to be built, but it won’t do the
work. At present it has an idea that the Railway
Commissioners ought to tackle the job—though
Heaven knows how they can come into the business.
Failing them, it will wait for the advent of the
Greater Melbourne Council, and that isn’t expected
before tWfe middle of the century. So with the
appliances for fire extinction in the city. Twenty
years ago Melbourne was told plainly that its city
mains were, for fire-extinction purposes, a farce
and a fraud. Larger mains must be laid and the
responsible parties are agreed on that point, but
they can’t come to terms as to the apportionment of
the cost, so nothing is done. For these and many
other undertakings a Prodder or Speeder-Up is
wanted ; in fact, about 20 are wanted, and even then
they would be overworked. And if they were ap-
pointed and set to work there would need be a Boss
Prodder to. speed them up. But all this is vanity.
In a State where the head of the Government is
the Champion Tired Man of the Commonwealth,
there is no place for Prddders.

The bears waiting to be admitted to the
iron-fenced performing enclosure were
packed close in their wheeled cage. But
they weren’t as badly crowded as Sydney
tram passengers, and no bear had on heavy
boots, or wore hatpins, or carried a damp
umbrella; neither was there a line of bears
on the footboard outside, each of them hang-
ing on by one paw and trying to retain
hold of a brown bag and the evening paper
with another, while groping for the fare
and being crawled over by an indignant
bear ticket-collector. Every bear was in-
side ; and although growls went up when a
long, soft-coated newcomer slid in from the
second cage, there was no real discomfort.
These comfortable animals, whom none of
the shoved and trampled tram passengers
present were entitled to pity, transpired at
Wirths’ Circus, Belmore Markets, Sydney,
on Saturday night. The broad-beamed ele-
phant, in his ready-made pants, is a humor-
ous encyclopaedia, especially when viewed
from the back as he waddles stolidly about
his steady bear-dragging job. He is reminis-
cent of a stout sailorman whose natural
bulk has been grossly exaggerated by the
divided skirts of the sea. Still, he can’t
compare with the trained bear who walks
on his hind legs, lolls a red, thirsty tongue,
and drinks solemnly from an up-ended bottle.
This Wirth season is distinctly the bears’
night out. The eleven Bastiens, a fine acro-
batic troupe with an extensive repertoire,
provide one of the new turns of the revised
show. Atoris and Le Van, a pair of aerial
bar practitioners, the Adelia Sisters and
George (wire entertainers), and Miss Dol-
inda (trapeze) are some of the mid-air
people. They share the space next the roof
wilh the Flying Jordans. Kitchen and Foy
and the Harry Nage Co. dispense most of
the humor left over by the bears. Other
talented performers and a drove of sleek,
well-trained animals are hereby dismissed
with a flick of the pen, there being no more
room available for a grand parade than
there was on the opening night in Sydney,
when even the belltoppers of the firm per-
spired in an heroic effort to make the vast
crowd as comfortable as the bears.
** * *
To wind up his season at the Palace
Theatre, William Anderson on Saturday
night produced still another dramatisation
of “His Natural Life.” Judged solely as
an effort at dramatisation, it is remarkable
for the audacious and frequently absurd
liberties which have been taken with the
author’s version; but judged as a mere
helping of melodrama to stay the ravening
appetite of those who must have melodrama
to follow the evening corned beef and pota-
toes, just as “morality” plays are the be-
setting weakness of the fairy who invites
you to Paris House at your own expense,
and just as unmusical comedy with its pink
fleshings is the crave of the covetous flapper
—but this sentence has become long and
unwieldy, and it is time to cut it off and
begin afresh. Judged then —as the writer
was trying to remark before his last remark
bolted off the course —as a dish of melo-
drama, it has to be recorded that on Satur-
day a packed house rushed at the play with
its mouth open and swallowed Anderson’s
goods whole. In face of that, criticism may
get back to its kennel, or bark its disappro-
val from a distance. Stage-manager Camp-
bell must be congratulated on his portrayal
of Gabbett, the owner of the wonderful
teeth, especially in the scene where he, the
Crow and John Rex draw lots to decide
which shall be the diner and which shall
be the bill of fare. There, mad with starva-
tion, slavering at the mouth in anticipation
of the victuals to come, stands —it might
almost be said howls —Clarke’s creation of
Gabbett. Next to Campbell’s work is that
of Tom Cannam as the Rev. Mr. Meekin.
Cannam has given some careful studies
throughout the season, but the holy Meekin
is his best. H. J. Bentley, as Rufus Dawes,
doesn’t strike the depth of misery of that
God-forgotten man. Walter Baker, as Rev.
John North, the manly and upstanding dip-
somaniac, is too little North and too much
Baker; and Frances Ross is wasted as the
colorless Sylvia Vicars. On the whole, how-
ever, the revival of the old convict story is
of more than ordinary interest, and it loses
very little in the telling at the Palace. R.
Dawes will be flogged on these premises for
one week longer.
In spite of numerous warnings from the
press, certain young ladies at the Adelphi
continue to learn typewriting from a bilious-
looking gentleman, and are, in consequence,
rapidly driven towards destruction on an
average of seven times a week, only to be
rescued when hanging by their last back-
hair to the edge of the precipice by a pugi-
listic parson and a chauffeur. The name of
this cruel deed is “The Night Side of Lon-
don” —London where the famous Lights
were lighted, and the Worst Woman
prowled about in her Babylonish garment,
and where so many other dramatic happen-
ings have happened in five acts or even more
at prices varying from 7s. 6d. down to a
“tanner.”

St. George and the Dragon, the two
gentlemen who adorn the back of a coin, are
fighting a two-round battle on the Lyric
screen every day and night this week, and
each time George wins on a knock-out.
Anyone who doesn’t know the yarn about
England’s patron saint had better go along
and see it, and anyone who does know it
won’t suffer by seeing it again. The pic-
ture itself, by the Milano Co., is worth
looking at. The other leading attraction at
the Lyric is “The Margrave’s Daughter,” a
story of the Court of the Margrave of
Hesse, somewhere about the year 1557.
“The Voiceless Message,” a well-screened and
well-acted picture, was Colonial l’s head-
line this week. In the new programme “A
Noble Enemy,” a U.S.A. battleship yarn,
will be the attraction. “His Last Cent,”
which the programme says is “taken from
life,” will lend a hand to fill Colonial No. 2
during the rest of the week.

Reluctant though the writer is to draw
invidious comparisons between the dead and
the quick, the sacred cause of Truth impels
him to remark that for culture, humanity,
taste and all-round allurement, Prince
Charles, at the National Amphitheatre,
fairly loses the late lamented George, his
forerunner at the house next door. Charles
approaches the dead limit in educated
chimpanzees; he is a super-monk, if ever
there was one. Prominent among his fellow-
artists are the Harlem Trio, Mr. C., and
O’Dell and Hart.

The violin—the faithful wailer that fol-
lows the tottering heroine through the snow
and attends at the bedside of the dying but
still garrulous stage infant —has hitherto
kept aloof from low comedy with a strong
odor of beer. Rinaldo, the wandering fid-
dler, now at the Tivoli, makes the serious
contrivance which is so addicted to gloomy
introspection in low keys and hysterics in
high ones, gurgle happily as if several pints
had been poured into the S-shaped mouths
in its polished face. It is good to know that
the violin can submerge its quivering nerves
if it wants to, and cease to be distressed
over snow wanderings and interminable
last speeces. Fred Poplar, the humorous
pursuer of limelight, Comedian Tom Paw-
son, the juggling Testros and Kitty Wager
are the chief remaining headliners of the
present bill.
The Bulletin gathers from the hoard-
ings, rumor, matinee devotees, and so on,
that “The Quaker Girl” is still at Her
Majesty’s (Sydney). T 9 verify the fact in
person it resolutely declines lest it thereby
blight one of its most gracious memories.
The central figure of the persistent old show
is certainly a Quaker Woman by this time,
mayhap (horrible thought!), a Quaker
beldame —and the writer’s predilection is
for Girls.

At Sydney Royal, Everywoman continues
to collogue interminably with Nobody-
a veracious touch this, the average female
being prepared to talk to a dog rather than
remain silent. Everybody applauds the
show from the front of the house, whereat
Nobody is to be heard expressing disgust
and amazement, and wondering what m
Sheol the drama is coming to.

Fine dramatic work is being done at Ade-
laide Royal. Titheradge has shone brightly
in the machine-made epigrams of Oscar
Wilde, and Mrs. Brough as a strange suc-
cession of repentant and tearful ladies.
Beatrice Day has generally too little to do.
Lizette Parkes has come on enormously. She
has the gift of “holding” an audience, and
should be another Nellie Stewart. Plim-
mer, always sound, was a charming Incon-
stant George, though possibly not quite the
George the author had in his eye. Denms-
ton, in the same play, did perhaps his best
work. It is a fine company, and the princi-
pals stand down in turn, or take tiny parts,
in the true artistic spirit. At the Tivoli
are Ike and Will Scott, who sandwich in
with comicalities a most realistic bit of
bare-fisted fighting; also Wilson Hallett,
with his baby-cry, and the popular Charles
Johnson, who cakewalks. At the Kings,
Johnson and Wells do the same thing in
combination (are all buck-niggers named
Johnson, these times?). Miss Beaumont
Collins and co. (notably David Baird) give
tabloid Thrills. The acting is good, the
plays are good, but the note is always
forced, the loud pedal on all the_ time.
Millie Doris, whatever one may think of
her methods, is the idol of the house. The
leading picture-shows, West’s, _ Wonder-
graph and Empire, are specialising in fire
and water-bathing scenes, submarines, lady
swimmers, wrecks, and the ' burning-up
of R. Haggard’s She. St. Patrick and co.
gave a national concert at the Exhibition,
and the Huddersfield Bellringers (why
“Ole Huddersfield” last week only the
printer knows) have had a successful week
at the Town Hall. Their chiming wea-
pons are equal to anything, from Wagner
to “Harrigan.”
** * *
The panto, at Her Majesty’s (Melbourne)
will also proceed to the verge of Easter week,
or thereabouts. The panto, has now topped
the century, and to-morrow (Friday) that
estimable leg-show will get a final fillip
from the rallying of its members at a
Theatrical Carnival on East Melbourne
Cricket Ground. The Carnival needs only
fine weather to ensure a big benefit for the
theatrical funds that absorb the proceeds.
If the weather turns dog on the proposed
festivities the absent public can send along
their shillings by post to the secretary.

Melbourne Athenaeum Hall betrayed its
awful lack of proper ventilation when the
Repertory Co. took the stage for three ex-
hibits of rational- drama, the third of which
—“Lonely Lives” —was to happen after this
publication had gone to press. “Chains
and “What the Public Wants” came and
went on Thursday and Saturday last week,
the audience each time being boshter. _ Eli-
zabeth Baker’s “Chains” is a drab-tinted
study of Cockney suburban home life, photo-
graphically depicted, and dealing mainly
with the discontent of a young married
clerk whose salary compels him to add a
lodger to his means of support. He would
go to Australia to look for a larger income
on broader lines of industry if his loving
little wife didn’t whimper at the thought
of him taking the risk —alone. Also, various
relatives and friends indignantly or scorn-
fully counsel him to stick to his irksome
little job in a London office. His only sym-
pathiser is a sister-in-law who is sick of
serving in a shop, but is equally indisposed
to buy her “freedom” by marrying a tame
widower of the senior clerk persuasion.
Nevertheless the moody _ malcontent has
brought himself to the point of departure,
intending to escape by strategy, when his
wife joyfully tells him of a new responsi-
bility in store. Thereupon he settles down
again to the contemplation of a dull future
in the Chains that circumstance have forged
for him. In any other crowded Old
World city than London the same conditions
of life are general conditions, and the same
pros and cons, of argument would lead to
the same dead, wall of fact. Even a young
Australian married man in a small billet
gets no encouragement from his wife and
mother-in-law to travel to the Northern
Territory in quest of the free and easy life.

The third and last week of “East Lynne”
is passing away to slow music at the King s.
The revival might have been prolonged, but
a wise manager never allows the old cam-
paigner to fight its popularity to a dead
finish. He drags it oft the boards in a fanly
Vigorous state, and keeps it fit for future
engagements. On Saturday the aggressively
moral and temperance drama of “A Girl s
Cross Roads” will be revived for one week
only. At the Princess’s the eccentricities
of Mrs. McSweeney will last another week,
or longer. Last Saturday a matinee per-
formance was thrown in, and Maggie
Moore’s passing references to St. Patrick
melted the audience to floods of cheers.
For some unaccountable reason a lady en-
gaged in collecting upon behalf of 27 orphans
calls on I. Cohen, who forthwith acquires
the delusion that she is his son’s fiancee
and is flabbergasted at the brazen, Niobe-
like manner in which she skites about her
27 kids, after admitting that she is un-
married. He is not prepared to part
from the £3O donation for which she
clamors, but eventually compromises for
£l5, less five per shent. This Hebrew
fantasy is being committed by Hay-
man and Franklin, new arrivals at the
Opera House. The male, who is a repre-
sentative of an immemorial type of vaude-
ville Israelite, is humorous enough and has
a commendably clear enunciation, while his
partner is a heavyweight Rebecca with a
certain amount of mature dash. The new
turn is not as diverting as that provided by
Terry and Lambert, the Yankee frivollers;
nor is it as interesting, from an aesthetic
standpoint, as the Casselli Sisters’ active
and intelligent legs; but then, again, it is
less mournful than the cross-talk indulged
in by 11. Rule, comedian, and a jumpy con-
federate in the dress circle. There are many
Rules in life, and 11. of that ilk is not the
golden one.

To produce a film like “The Flight to
Death,” which figured at the Prahran Lyric
(Vic.) last week, seems like wasting good
photography on a misbegotten story. The
yarn deals with the machinations of Nihil-
ists, but several of its incidents are quite
unaccountable, and even this paper’s trained
bloodhound failed to scent out a good reason
for the last motor smash. A fairly charm-
ing drama is “The Baby and the Stork ;
but “The Crime and the Criminal pulls
the public leg to excess, and drags the long
arm of coincidence out of joint and all rea-
son. Spectacularly pleasing is “By the
Zuyder Zee,” a study of a flat, flat land
where the nation, has been pumping for IUUU
years to avoid being drowned.
Among the starters at West’s Building,
Melbourne, on Saturday, was “Convicts Nos.
10 and 13,” a pictorial convulsion in three
acts and innumerable scenes. It is too much
dragged out, and the only thing that is as
inconsequent as the two ends is the middle.
The obliteration of a bad man over a good
big precipice is the principal feature of Ihe
Half-Breed’s Daughter.” Half-breeds mostly
live in mountainous countries for the ex-
press purpose of having a handy precipice
about the house. The strong suit of Aunt
Zoe goes in for Painting” is the arduous
imbecility of aunty’s relations. As regards
the film, “Beware of Magic,” the man who
describes it as a “screaming farce ought to
get six months for defamation.

For three weeks in all “The House of
Temperley” will furnish the stage at Mel-
bourne Royal with a suggestion—ranging
from faint to middling—of the t»ad old Re-
gency period in England, when gents of
fashion flaunted loose morals and tight side-
whiskers, and their preposterous neck-gear
was leading up to the bronchial troubles
which have set their descendants coughing.
“Temperley,” as was recorded last week, is
a mere spectacular play of stoushing senti-
ment, with no dramatic punch to speak, ot,
nor any feminine interest worth mention-
ing. The final curtain falls on a cheap and
foolish military episode, ending in smoke
that leaves the heroine presumably mourn-
ing in a distant country. But seeing that
the heroine carries no weight in the casual
narrative, nor gets any public sympathy tor
loving one brother whilst intending to marry
the other, her supposititious tears don t
count. The only bit of early 19th century
muslin that appeals to students of human
nature is Ginger Stubbs’s girl
Brown) who bounces into Tom Cnbb s
Saloon with the idea that she isn’t going to
let her Ginger return to the prize-rmg and
be * damaged on the “dial” or. elsewhere.
When she learns that the belting of her
best boy will be worth £2OO and extras to
their domestic joint she withdraws her pro-
test, and leaves Ginger in the hands of his
backers, which is a truly womanly repul-
sion of feeling, anyhow. Tom Cnbb s Saloon
is the most satisfactory scene in the lem-
perley” series, although the ‘harmony
gathering at the three tables presents. more
character as regards its clothes than in its
cross-fires of conversation. The incidental
set-to between little Ginger and Joe Berks
Harry lames in the Coorong Desert (S.A.), preparing the way for the Dunlop Relay Race.
is as realistic as it can be, considering that
the fiery bantam is obviously too small tor
the iob. It has more snap in the prelimin-
aries and stoushing details than the subse-
quent prize-fight on Crawley Down, where
the Down is all in the background, and the
ring is pitched between a couple of rural
buildings. - Nobody views the battle from
those tempting points of vantage. lhe
fight with the “raws admits ot vast
improvement in stage managing at the
ring-side, apart from the fact that an in
troductory boxing display between Sam
Grey and Starlight seems ridiculously out
of place in this connection. There is no
furious uproar of and blas-
phemy around the combatants. The specta-
tors don’t implore their respective fancies
to blanky well kill the other bloke, as pat-
rons of noble art were wont to do of yore.
Fifteen rounds' are supposed to be running
their gory course, whilst the curtain hides
the combatants for one minute, but no hor-
rible language is shrieked from behind that
curtain. The prize-fight is. too dashed re-
spectable. Nevertheless, this journal must
modify a previous allegation that Harcourt
Beatty and the hired recipient of his raw
biffs are always smiting the air. As a mat-
ter of fact, Beatty often gets in the way ot
gentle punishment, whilst his doomed op-
ponent suffers many of the ills that flesh is
heir to. His nakedness, seemingly, is be-
ginning to color, like a clay pipe. J® l * l "
perley” has another week to run, so the
chances are that Arthur Styan’s bruised
candidate will have to be replaced ere the
season closes.
“N.L.”: This morality play is all very well, but,
after all, I think I prefer the immorality play.
London Stage Society has just produced “Travel-
lers ” bv Norman McKeown, of Sydney. The plot of
the thing is laid in an Australian mining town, and
half of the critics praised it, if not up to the
skies, at least as high as the ceiling, and the rest
blamed it down to the cellar. The author was told
that it was “a work of considerable courage and
ability,” and that “he only succeeded in showing
how faint is the line which separates the sublime
from the ridiculous” ; that it “suggests the work-
manship of Mr. Shaw,” and that it is “not merely
non-commercial, but also non-dramatic and non-in-
tellectual, too.” You can toss up these remarks and
take the one that comes down first.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Busk” : The Tom and Jerry scene of Tom Cribb’s
parlor, in “The House of Temperley,” recalls the
pugilism of 1812. John Gully ought to have been
brought forward as a spectator. Truly the most
astounding man in fistic annals. He was a friend of
Cribb, and the pair once took a joint benefit. Guiiy
retired from hitting live meat in 1808, when he
had fought a number of battles, especially those
against Gregerson. There is a saying that a man,
to achieve success, ought to be once stone broke.
Gully, the son of a butcher, afterwards publican, got
into the Fleet Prison for debt at the early age of
22. This was the foundation of his career. A little
amateur scrapping enlivened the tedium of the gaol.
The ability of Gully roused the interest of the Game
Chicken, who bought him out. Gully soon repaid
the Fowl from his winnings. Then he became a
publican. From this stage he advanced to bookmak-
ing, and he bookmade on a very large scale, being
quite the Joe Thompson of his day. He managed
to lose £40;000 in one whack, but was not knocked
out. About this time Gully had to pay large dam-
ages for battering his partner, Kidsdale. As is some-
times the case, his losses were an advertisement.
After all, he kept his position as the foremost book-
maker. Like Jack Wren, he progressed to owning
racehorses, but never singed a wing, like that bird,
by dabbling in playhouses. Gully upset all the
axioms about money that is easily got being money
easily lost. He kept gaining hand over hand. Be-
came a big coalmine owner, and reached almost
the best circles of society. His daughters were re-
markably handsome. He was twice married, and
had 12 children by each wife, total 24. He sat for
five years in the House of Commons, as member for
Pontefract. Death got him in 1863, at the age of 80.
It was his grandson, William Court Gulley, M.P. and
Q.C., who became Speaker of the House of Commons
in 1895.
“Brannigan”: Thus “Vulcan” (B. 7/3/’12):
“Consider the new Portus-Talbot firm’s programme—
Jansen, magician; nigger minstrel troupe; concert
artists; aviator Hammond, etc.—one-man shows,
vaudeville, platform events and out-door amuse-
ments, which the Combine wouldn t touch with a
telegraph pole—yet.” Which sounds all right until
you ascertain that “concert artists means the gre
Cisneros and a whole pyramid of supporting voices
for oratorio, etc. (commencing at Sydney next
June). I’m not in the confidence of the Combine, so
I suppose I must accept “Vulcan s word that it
would not have Cisneros on its mind. All the same
I wouldn’t take anybody else’s word for it.
The fund for Armes Beaumont closed at £483
ss. 5d., and it was handed to him with all due
pomp, at the Town Hall, by Mr. R. A. Molesworth.
CJ. Madden, who was president of the committee,
would have done the deed, but he was unavoidably
detained elsewhere, or pledged to interview a man
about a dog or something. The total amount col-
lected was £487, and, inclusive of bank charges,
the expenses incurred amounted to only £3 14s. 7a.

A PERTURBED BILL-TOPPER SPEAKS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Chapman-Alexander mission revisits Australia.
Disquieting news item.
None dare assert that I’m a jealous mime ;
I’d be much hurt to be termed “vain” or “cocky” ;
Yet though alone I reigned, a star, one time,
Just now my throne seems tottery and rocky ;
My crown, in sooth, there’s danger of me doffing—-
Revivalists are sighted in the offing!
They’ve been before ; their coming I resent.
What makes me sore —they’re such big opposition.
Bill-toppers they, mummers whose time is spent
Saving (they say) their audience from perdition ;
My reputation as a draw they’ll break up,
And, worse, while doing it collections take up !
For many years have I adorned the stage,
And it appears my Art has been far-reaching;
My picture post-cards, colored, are the rage;
Ido not boast—the public likes my preaching.
What wonder, then, these rivals raise my dander,
This double-turn —Chapman and Alexander!
I’ll beat the drum anew, for should I let
Outsiders come and take the stage’s centre
And all the limelight, Work I’d have to get,
And, as a mime, the stage-door no more enter;
Walk up, walk up, my friends! My mumming’s
grander—
I’m cheaper, too, than Chap, and Alexander!
N.S.W.
EARDLEY TURNER.
THE STIPEND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At a meeting of the wardens of the Anglican
Church, when the motion that the church be pain ed
was put, the rector moved an amendment that his
sCne-nd, which had not been paid for two months, be
naid instead, the amendment being put by way of
drawing attention to the fact. . Mr. R—-, m reply,
said there was too much politics in the pulpit. #
Bundaberg item.
Whatever are we coming to
When parson makes so much ado
About the things of earth —the wage,
The food, the raiment?
We think the paint is wanted worst.
He thinks we ought to pay him first,
And threatens that he’ll call around
For early payment.
Our shepherd waits a month or two
Without a stiver of his screw,
And vows he cannot live on air
And soft evasions;
We only say we’d rather see
Our chapel shine resplendently
In virgin white, than strive to meet
His expectations.
So lay the paint on fair and thick
We love to see our temple slick,
No matter whether parsons’ wrongs
Are ever righted;
So long as all is fair without
What care we if the heathen flout,
And call our church a sepulchre
That’s only whited?
So long as pastor gets a crust
And carries on —of course he must
Put up with things—although his hopes
Get daily fainter;
And if we can’t his prospects mend
He still —we hope we don’t offend —
May drop the church and —happy end!
May turn a painter !
QUEENSLAND.

A tiresome, sloppy squabble between
neighbors that got into the Melbourne courts
lately was interesting chiefly because of the
amazing amiability of one of them. The
parties were a master-mariner’s wife and an
orchardist, of Blackburn (Vic.), and the
bother arose over a handful of ■cherries. It
appeared that in December last the lady was
allegedly seen by the orchardist to enter his
garden and annex a few cherries, and he be-
came annoyed and handed her a summons to
answer a charge of petty larceny. The case
being dismissed the lady got busy and sued
the cherry-grower for £99 damages. When
the case came before Judge Box he was so
shocked, first at the extreme bitterness
shown between the master mariner’s wife
and the orchardist’s better-half, and secondlv
at the gross perjury one or the other was
committing, that he declined to settle the
matter himself, and remitted it to a jury.
Last week the plaintiff told the jury she
never took any cherries —she was, she said,
miles away at the time —and was, in fact, a
much harassed and long-suffering woman.
She yielded to everybody. Her neighbors
had complained about her fowls —and she
got rid of them. About her dog—she poisoned
it- About her goat—she gave it away.
About her calves—she sold them. About
her daughter’s habit of singing comic songs
when they were playing sacred music. She
liadn t smothered her daughter as yet, but
she was a peaceable woman, and would do
anything for a quiet life, and she might
have done even that had they not accused
her of taking cherries. She was a most pro-
testrul feminine, blit her appearance was
against her, being large and bounceful, and
the jury apparently reckoned she was
making a deuce of a fuss over nothing, for
it gave a verdict for the orchardist without
leaving the box.

still consists largely of a stern-chase after
the sorcerer and the witch. In the North-
Eastern division, for instance, there were 227
cases in the “Court for Native Matters” last
year, and 82 of them were prosecutions for
sorcery. It is the old pourri-pourri trouble
and the white man is beginning to feel that
Jt going to be with him until the Papuan
is civilised” which won’t be to-morrow or
the next day. The nigger dies, and the
other nigger, who

killed him, con-

be the work of
some third un-

wherefore the other
nigger proceeds to
wipe out the un-
friendly nigs-er, or
some relative of
his, or else sug-

trouble should be

Recently one no-
torious wizard,
who knew just the
right thing to do
to make bad wea-
ther and spoil the
taro crops or the
fishing, was ar-
rested, and was be-
ing brought along
the coast in a canoe
by two native
policemen. Then the
Law, though it had
arrested lrm for sorcery, suddenly remem-
bered that if it kept him a prisoner he would
probably make bad weather and thus drown
the lot, so one-half of the Law begged his
pardon and pushed him ashore. Not, of
course, that it believed in sorcery any more;
still the Law observed that it had only one
life, and it wasn’t taking any risks.

“Petronius”: This is how some alleged
Australians regard their nominal country.
Speaking at the Sydney Irish National For-
resters’ banquet, the boss cockie, a gentle-
man named MacNamara—
Went on to say that their Society’s aims could
briefly be placed under five headings, namely, first,
to develop national aspirations, and respect for their
religion ; secondly, to foster love for poor old Ireland ;
thirdly, to foster the spirit of fealty to the British
Empire and the Government under which they lived;
fourthly, to foster patriotism towards Australia ; and
fifthly, to cultivate the habit of thrift among the
members of their Order.
Australia comes fourth and thrift fifth. As
for the habit of washing it isn’t even men-
tioned.
WHATS IN A NAME?
Melbourne, Sunday.—“ Should We Legalise the
Tote?” was the topic adopted by Rev. T. S. B.
Woodfull for an address to a very large congregation
at the Wesley Church.
His answer was an emphatic negative. The sug-
gestion that charity should be assisted by the legal-
isation of the totalisator was worthy of scorn.
Gambling would be wicked, legalised or not.
Rev. Walter William Woeful: “ Accept
the proceeds of gambling! Never! Take
the unclean thing away!"
A citizen of Sydney, named Day, has had
a narrow escape from the clutches of the
Law. One Saturday in December there
was an unpleasantness in Redfern, and an
adjacent policeman was suddenly hit on the
jaw and “put to sleep” for 20 minutes. On
recovering consciousness he remembered the
occurrence and arrested Day. At the trial
Day produced several witnesses of the as-
sault, who all swore that he was innocent.
The magistrate, however, like most magis-
trates, took the word of the intelligent
policeman, and awarded Day three months.
But Day, peculiarly enough, was not satis-
fied, and appealed to Judge Rogers. The
police-court witnesses were reproduced and
again declared that if they were struck pink
or something Day was guiltless; and, further,
that one Robinson should s!and in his place.
Robinson, who was in court, was put on the
stand and promptly and emphatically denied
that he stoushed the policeman; whereas
Rogers looked sourly upon Day and refused
to disturb the original conviction. Pre-
sently, however, a breathless Crown Prose-
cutor announced to the Judge that Robin-
son had admitted, outside the Court, that
he was the author of the intelligent police-
man’s downfall, and the Bloke on Top being
satisfied by hurried declarations, Day was
hastily summoned from the cells and dis-
charged. The Judge, however, didn’t even
offer to shout him a drink, and, so far, the
magistrate hasn’t thought of apologising.'

Melbourne Argus has seemingly forgiven
Chief Justice John Madden his offence of
fining that law-abiding journal for contempt
of Court in the. Peacock case. Whilst -its
distinctly feminine indignation was at white
heat the Argus boycotted John. It ignored
his existence outside of the courts. If he
opened a high-toned tea-fight with a long
and eloquent address on the philosophy of
the muffin or what not, the Argus turned
its hundred eyes away from the honeyed
wordy citizen, and pretended to be deaf. He
continued to play speaks, but the ruffled
news-organ was not playing listens. Time,
however, must have healed the journal’s
wounded feelings, or perhaps the genial
Waxcellency sent it a goose at Christmas.
Anyhow, it referred to his exposition of the
aim of the Educational Congress in Mel-
bourne as a “speech of admirable lucidity.”
TWO YEARS AGO.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Will E.S., met gentleman picnic two years ago,
Meadow Bank, wite F.A., Herald Office. —Romantic
morning paper advt.
Two years ago, on Meadow Bank,
The sun was bright, the world was gay;
From Cupid’s brimming cup they drank,
The shy E.S., the bold F.A. ;
Ah, where’s the lover who can say
What happiness they learned to know
When first they met, that golden day
Two years ago ?
Two years ago, he could not guess
How long her memory would cling;
She seemed a Mash in idleness,
And not the true, authentic thing.
He bought her no expensive ring,
Nor made the costly wine-cup flow.
He only sat her on*a swing
Two years ago.
Oh, yesterday! Can nothing make
Your sweetness linger with us yet?
Must all days die, ere new days break?
And we rush onward, and forget
The glory when the sun has set ?
(I beg your pardon. In this throe
I thought of —someone, whom I met
Two years ago.)
Since then, F.A., in Cupid’s car,
Through much experience has dashed,
And eyes as radiant as a star
On him their tender light’ have flashed ;
But Cupid’s trumpets have not crashed
With such magniloquent Yo-ho
As when the sweet E.S. he mashed
Two years ago !
Now, yearningly he thinks of when
Half-finished was his picnic spoon,
And longs to meet E.S. again
Beneath the pale, romantic moon,
Where they twin souls will coo and croon
In plaintive accents soft and low,
Repeating what was once their tune,
Two years ago.
Alas, alas ! These lovers twain
Had better keep themselves apart.
Lips will not sing their ancient strain,
Eyes will not ray their ancient dart.
No kiss, no crime, no dream, no art
(So fast moves on this world of woe!),
Are what they were in hope and heart
Two years ago.
Grim is the sentence written down,
The sentence of relentless Fate
Which bids the present turn its frown
Upon the Past with bitter hate ;
Closed is the door, and locked the gate:
Man finds not zest—and Woman, no !
In eating of the fruits they ate
« Two years ago.
N.SAV. - -■
ADAM McCAY.

The present situation of Sydney is that of
the short-sighted person who bought a full-
grown ostrich, and then found he had nothing
to keep it in but the cage of a deceased
parrot. The immense population heaped up
through the congestion policy is Sydney’s
ostrich,- and the inefficient tram service is its
parrot’s cage. The spectacle of the willing
ostrich trying to wear the cage on his tail
after the manner of a bustle, and pretending
he is inside, is distressing. Now an indig-
nant City Council has drawn the attention
of Chief Railway Commissioner Johnson to
the disgraceful and dangerous overcrowding,
and requested him to “issue immediate in-
structions that this practice cease forth-
with.” There must be a lot of people nowa-
days who are less miserable than Johnson.
The heavy artillery of “Snell” is heard in
the distance: —
A bulgy parson in Melbourne talks of the “hideous
immorality” that mixed bathing has induced from
Port Melbourne to St. Hilda (inclusive), and I want
to know what he means by it. “Hideous immor-
ality” should mean a lot. Being a not-too-mixed
bather myself, I have been keeping an eye lifting for
the hideousness, but my luck is not in. How do
the parsons find these things ? All my research has
led only to a conviction that mixed bathing has
served to make said stretch of sands comparatively
respectable. Before the mixed wets and partially
drpwneds took possession this was the camping
ground of those who dragged each other about in
token of affection. Here Bill and Liz twined them-
selves in knots, and decorated the whole foreshore
irnhuman clumps, night after night, a b'ne of mis-
conduct utterly repellant to the prowling Wowser, and
the cause of many vehement moral upheavals on the
part of that eminent legal geyser Madden. Now
the clumps are gone, and these cuddling camps are in
the possession of dank, blue persons, with seaweed
in, their whiskers, and dripping females with dis-
solving complexions and skimpy hair, washed up by
the sea. If the young people loiter in the street
the Wowser howls. If they loaf in the parks the
Wowser bellows. If they run down a flat place
into the sea the Wowser shrieks. What are we to
do for the Wowser?

“Geofred” sees our friend the enemy at a
picture show: —
I went because The Bulletin had told me that
“The Japanese at School” were to be filmed, and I
wanted to see how the young idea of Tokyo and
Nagasaki learned to shoot out from among the
back-markers and land in the front rank within a quar-
ter of a century. I saw the Jap. girl learning sewing,
washing, cooking, and other branches of household
work ; but nowhere did I see her being taught book-
keeping or type-writing. This made me think that
perhaps the Jap. girl is not trained to push a coun-
tryman out of a billet that she can take at a lower
wage, but that she is trained in domestic duties and
impressed with their overwhelming importance. Then
came the same girls being taught nurse’s work, in-
cluding first-aid attention to broken arms and legs
or split heads, etc.—the sort of damage that might
happen to anyone on a battlefield. Physical culture
and all classes of drills for physical development were
gone thsough. Then came the Jap. boys. Kids
running up from eight to 18 or 20 were wrestling in a
ring. As one was outed another would rush on to
either “best” or be “bested,” till only one was left
supreme. The methods were sodden and rough;
yet almost every kid came up smiling. The sight of
100 of these Japs doing dumb-bell exercises was some
sort of a revelation, but it wasn’t to be compared
with the shivery spectacle of these same jokers learn-
ing which part of the target it is the best to hit.
Next morning I saw that the Rev. Peace-at-any-
Pricerg were nagging at our own cadets again, and
'asking whv the kids were taken out in the evenings
to learn - the “butchering business”? If only those
fanatics would go and-, persuade the Japs to knock
off their nonsense, I’d feel a lot more comfortable.
DELAY IS DANGEROUS.
There is no class of ailment that affects the sys-
tem quicker , than bowel complaints, and any delay
in treating diarrhoea or colic is-dangerous. Chamber-
lain’s Colic, Cholera and Diarrhoea Remedy is the
only remedy that is absolutely reliable in cases of this
kind, and as some member of the family is always
liable to an attack at any time it should always be
kept in the house. This remedy never fails to give
prompt relief.*
The letter of “Germicide” :
A long, iuug - tune ago, somewhere in the waybaek
epoch when “Liberalism” still regarded Willis as a
human being, a certificated nurse bought a piece of
ground in Killara (N.S.W.), and started to build
a private hospital on it. Months passed, and then,
Propputy woke with a howl to the effect that vested
interests, private enterprise and the rights of man
were being blasted by the lady’s venture. The “lead-
ing citizens” of Killara foregathered and assured
each other that the place was destined to be not so
much a healing-establishment as a germ-farm. It
was predicted that Disease would descend on Killara
like a blight, and ravage the health and beauty of
the local golfers. A state of panic set in. The
question of Microbes became more popular, as a sub-
ject for denunciation, than the Menace of Labor. A
public meeting of residents was called with the
object of assailing the proposed hospital, and its
authoress was asked to attend, after first fumigating
and disinfecting herself and boiling her clothing.
She did so, and was roundly insulted, after which
the gathering dispersed shuddering. Marrow-freezing
statements continued to be made concerning the grim
potentialities of the hospital, and on the 9th inst.
it was decided to hold yet another meeting. This
time there was a sanitary expert, in the person of
Dr. Ashburton Thompson, present to sift the evi-
dence L* the complainants. One of their chief
claims was that they had agreed to the erection of
the building under a misapprehension. Whereupon:
Dr. Ashburton Thompson: “Why did you pass
the plans for this house?” Loud bray in reply: “We
thought it was to be a private residence.”
Dr. A. T.: “How many guests, then, did you ex-
pect the owner to entertain?” Bray not so loud:
“Five or six.”
Dr. A. T.: “That, according to the plans, would
mean two or three rooms a-piece for each guest.”
Sudden absence of bray.
Dr. A. T.: “Show me the house that in your report
you said was 32ft. from the septic tank of the hos-
pital?” In reply a 'confused murmur to the effect
that they had meant to say that the septic tank was
32ft. from the back fence of the hospital.
And so on. It appeared, in pretty short order, that
the agitation against the establishment of the hos-
pital had emanated from a few land-owners in the
district, who had become afflicted with vague fears
that an institution of the sort might have a preju-
dicial effect on the land-boom which is raging so
satisfactorilv on the North Shore just now. As ■ a
matter of fact, private hospitals don’t affect, the value
of adjoining properties prejudicially, as witness the
condition of Darlinghurst, which is fairly creeping
with them. But, anyhow, why Killara should be ex-
empted from these. institutions, which are neces-
saries of civilised life so long as the public hospitals
remain un-nationalised, is a darksome mystery, and
the suggestion that it lias any such right is a Unique
example of Suburban Hide.
* * * *
Nothing on 1 his earth travels half so fast
as a really shocking story, and, if it is
booked through under such a label as “Miss
Brown and the King of Korea,” its round-
the-world passage by all the fastest routes
is booked ahead for the next 20 years. Once
fairly started, it makes the earth a sort of
saucer track, over which it reels at breath-
less speed, with good old Indignant Denial
pedalling madly in its wake. Some little
while back, Melbourne sat up with a jerk
when the dreadful news flashed through that
Miss Brown, a missionary’s daughter, had
been taken into the harem of the King of
Korea, and afterwards married by her father
-—presumably to the King. Most people said
“Horrible!” and passed the story on, and for-
got it. But the Foreign Missionary Secre-
tary wrote in haste to the senior missionary
in Korea, who replied wearily, after the lapse
of precious weeks, that he thought the old lie
had been dead and buried long ago. Then,
apparently for the manyth time, he recapitu-
lated the alleged bald facts, which were that
the King had hired a French lady, who
was formerly employed at the Russian lega-
tion as housekeeper, to look after the furni-
ture and other things in the palace, and to
assist in the entertaining of foreigners.. bne
had never been in the harem, but was simply
busy on a plain, drab and unsensational
job of supervising the feather-dusting and
afternoon-tea-ing. The rest was just Yankee
reporter. Hereabouts we see Indignant ive-
nial roll by in pursuit. But we also see that
Miss Brown and the King are streaking
away miles in the lead.
* * * *
Once more has Private Enterprise been
interfered with and discouraged. On March
5, a chauffeur of pigs was hustling a few up
Sussex-street, Sydney, when one of the
humble brothers of Fat became exhausted.
The chauffeur dragged it. to a sheltered spot
to rest, and went on with the rest ot the
family. Then Private Enterprise, in the
person of one Jackson, is alleged to have
wandered along and .sold the recumbent meat
to a passing dealer for 10s. When the
chauffeur called back a quarter of an hour
later for the resting bacon, he found nothing
but the low-spirited pig-smell; the brisk,
business-like transaction had been com-
pleted. The alleged seller ought to get on
when he gets out —that is, of course, pro-
vided he gets in.
* * * *
“D.”: The chief characteristic of the
“masked burglar” who has been enjoying
something of a boom in the Sydney press ot
late is incompetence, bordering on ihibe-
cility. His attempt on the assets of Miss
Hilda Spong panned out nothing, though it
the lady’s story is correct he nearly got
away with a small supply of lead. His
effort on Bellevue Hill was equally futile.
After rendering the only occupant of the
house senseless with fright and “ransack-
ing” the place from cellar to roof he fled
empty-handed. His Randwick venture
brought him an old tablecloth; on the
other hand, he lost his mask and was chased
off the premises in horror. In the writer’s
opinion the full-blooded style of “burglar”
is a misnomer as applied to this bungling
impostor. He appears to be simply a pry-
ing nuisance with a taste for bellowdrama
and an impaired nervous system.
* * * *
Another old trading ship of Sydney and
Polynesia has gone to her long-deserved rest
—the barque Loongana, owned by On (Jhong
and Co., of this, city. It went on shore at
Butaritari, in the Gilbert Islands, in Janu-
ary last, after having bumped or scraped
against every reef in that group during a
full 33 years. Its predecessor was the
ancient brig Isabella, also owned by On
Chong and Co., which, 34 years back,
wearily entered Butaritari Lagoon and
settled its aching keel on the coral bottom.
The tattered bones of the lady are still
visible. The Loongana was Tasmanian
built, and the stories which could be written
about it and its Chinese supercargoes, and
its English, German and Norwegian skip-
pers, and its multitudinous brown women,
and its numerous sins, rums and devilments
would have kept Robert Louis Stevenson
busy to this day.
LOST—AN INFERNO.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The empty churches impotently sigh and plead
and groan. The masses peer and gape and grin.
Rid of the ancient fear of hell fire they are, at least
in part, unquestionably; but are they better for the
riddance? It must be doubted. — Melbourne Age.
Oh, give us back our Hell! The masses gape
And peer and grin, all heedless of their souls ;
We cannot scare them with our Bogey’s shape,
Nor fright them with the dread of burning coals,
Nor wield, as once of old, our outworn spell,
And make it pay... .Oh, give us back our Hell!
The masses prosper; yea, they make good cheer.
But hark! above the fortune of the State,
AYhat awesome sound is this that strikes the ear?
It rises from the void collection-plate!
Where now the thrip’ny-bit we loved so well,
Terror’s percentage? Give us back our Hell!
For, once we lose the old effective threats—
The pitchforks, tails, hoofs, horns and satyrs’
legs—
The sceptic age its reverence forgets
And cooks our goose (that laid the silver eggs!)
’Tis better thus? But where’s the prof—er, well,
The Board’s own tithe?.... Oh, give us back our
Hell!
VICTORIA
EPISCOPUS
THE STRIKERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
A Melbourne Archdeacon, preaching on the in-
famy of race suicide, points out that it is a middle-
and upper-class sin. In the swagger suburb of Cam-
berwell families have only one infant where the
families of plebeian, and contemned Footscray, the
grafter’s suburb, have two.
How shocking ’tis to have to tell
That in superior Camberwell
Such wickedness is rife,
While Footscray wives allow full play
To nature (so archdeacons say),
And do the State good service. They
Are giving it new life.
Kate Smith, of Port, no duty shuns,
She gives Australia little ones ;
But Vera Smmytthe, of Kew,
From grave maternal tasks refrains
The one thing she can do disdains,
And with her wealthy lord remains*
A non-producer, too.
Though she may name it what she like.
The swagger dame is out on strike- —-
A shocking thing to see
When one recalls the curses grim
The “classes” hurl at Bill and Jim,
Who stop, for no illiberal whim,
The wheels of industry.
And shall the thunders of the morn,
The leaders choked with hate and scorn,
Assail the culprit wife?
Nay, these reserve for Jim and Bill
Who Capital would seek to kill!
Compared with that, small crime to spill
So cheap a thing as life!
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.

“Jaloom” : For making the mildest-man-
nered cow madder than the tiger of Sosh there’s
nothing to equal a “blow
out” of peaches. I had
it demonstrated for me
when I went to board with
a farmer.. Fresh meat
was seldom “on,” and Ted-
die, the farm-hand, missec}
it. He waited till the
peach season, and then saw
steak ahead. ( One morn-
ing I perceived him coming
from the garden with a
bucket of peaches. I con-
templated more peach
stews. Instead, Teddie ad-
ministered his dose to the
“beefiest” cow of the herd.
Some few hours after, dad
and mum started for town
in the old buggy, and
while they were crossing
the paddock the cow
rushed furiously at the old
horse. Give Teddie his
due he had the two dogs
ready, and with their aid
we Were able to beat her
off. While we were sym-
pathising with the old
couple on their narrow es-
cape the cow was at the
other end of the paddock
knocking down all the
suckers and anthills she
came across. Teddie sug-
gested that she should be
shot before she killed any-
body. To this the farmer
consented. With the aid of
a red rag she was brought
into range. Teddie did the
rest, and we had steak
next morning.
“J. 5.8.”: One “E. W. O’D” (B. 15/2/’l2)
says ■ the. triek of sinking post-holes short belongs to
the Speewah period. Well, I reckon the man who
has the holes passed before the posts are put in be-
longs to the Cow-cocky period. The commonest thing
is for two men to sink and two more to put up;
but no back-country fencer ever ran the risk of hav-
ing his post-holes filled with water and damaged by
stock while he was waiting to have them passed.
In putting up a cow-yard or calf-pen, it may be the
practice to wait until Woolly Whiskers pokes a
stick in to see if the orifices are down to Plimsoll
mark, but not in fencing outback. As far as post-
splitting goes, it’s a matter of timber more than
grain. If the trees are a good size, and, when down,
yield a good crop of posts, all well and good ; but
if a lot of falling has to be done for a few posts,
it’s all blight and misery.
“Given two nails driven into the same
tree at different heights, would they become fur-

J. H. Maiden advances in his latest part of “The
. ,
Forest Flora of N.S.W.” American experiments

“B. de D.” : A bullock in London put up
a record in climbing stairs a month or so back.
With others he was being rushed to his doom at
the Islington market. With some premonition of
the fate awaiting him, he dashed for safety into the
entrance of a four-storey block. He got up 57 steps,
went round eight sharp turns, and emerged on the
flat roof, which was a clothes-drying “ground.” The
drovers went after him with lassoes, but he refused
to be. lassoed, and charged everybody who got on
the roof. One drover got behind a chimney stack
and fired shots at the beast, but missed every time.
The bullock allowed a photographer to take his pic-
ture, but was roused to fury when a reporter ap-
peared with his notebook. The pressman dodged
behind a chimney pot, which was rushed by the bul-
lock and smashed, so the Archimedean lever gathered
up his coat-tails and fled for his life. In the end
the bullock, was shot by a bobby, cut up in his
skyey bower to the accompaniment of much blood
and muddle, and carted away in sections. The most
notable feature of the incident was the absence of
Winston Churchill.
“Mallarapoonie” speaks:—
The Champion Mean City of Australia? Certainly.
Perth (W.A.), and an easy winner at that. Rightly
has it been described by one of our most thoughtful
citizens, as the Indigent Relation of the rest of
Westralia, and the Beer-Sparrer at the Pub. on
Westralia’s street corner. For years and years
Perth has battened, vampire-like, on the rest of
W.A., and more particularly on the goldfields por-
tion of the State, but, as for finding capital for the
development of these regions, well, does a pea-and-
thimble operator usually supports his missis and the
kids ?
Perth’s treatment of Albany, its rival with the
fine natural harbor, has; since the beginning, been of
a: brotherly nature, I don’t think. Since Albany
has had its overseas trade filched from it, and has
been refused, a railway into the forest country, that
might have made it a bicker and better export
centre for hardwoods than Bunbury, its existence
has been just a grey, stale-fish sort of thing, with
long spells between drinks. The arrival of a steamer
is now an event at Albany, and when the steamer
departs, Albany turns into bed, as a means of using
up time.
But, in so long and persistently denying Esper-
ance its railway to the goldfields, Perth is seen in
its spirit of most brilliant hoggishness. It is afraid
poor little Esperance would attract about 2s. Pd.
worth of goldfields trade, which Perth considers is its
right by inheritance. The antagonism of the gold-
fields to the bloodsucking methods of the metropolis
is an affair of long standing, and there is good rea-
son, too, for the feud. Examination of records goes to
show that Perth has less money invested in the
mining stocks of its own State than any other capital
city of the Commonwealth. Next after London,
Adelaide has supplied most capital for mining ven-
tures in Westralia. London, Adelaide, Melbourne
and Sydney represent about 90 per cent, of invested
capital. Paris represents another 5 per cent, or so,
and the balance is made up by Timbuetoo, Valpar-
aiso, Perth (W.A.) and a few other foreign places.
Persons seldom try to float mines or any other in-
dustries in Perth. They are invariably bluffed off
by notices of “Commission business only.” In those
three words is summed up Perth’s attitude to the
rest of the State —the attitude of a small, mean
Jew running an unimportant pawnshop in an obscure
side street.
Perth has never given the collieries at its own
back door a lift, if it could possibly get the black
goods from abroad. The treatment of Collie by the
powers that have sat in Perth has been of a kind
calculated to make hewers of carbon revolt. And
its scurvy treatment of its great north country
makes the top end of W.A. the most unimportant
part of Australia. Yes, from denying Esperance its
legitimate rights, to hounding O’Connor to his grave.
Perth’s record is a beauty.

“Aiteea” : I reckon the Fijian women are
the ringers at turning brother octopus inside out. I
have seen them at it repeatedly. They tease the
marine devil with a stick until he grips, when they
haul him forth, turn him out like a sock, and drop
him into the tucker basket. Have heard in various,
places that the thing tastes not at all bad, but I’ve
never put my own teeth into him.

THE CREED OF COW.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bathurst, Friday.
At the inquest to-day into the death of Mrs. Alice
Hannah Wilson, of Evans Plains, who was found yes-
terday morning with her brains blown out, a finding
of suicide was recorded.
The 11-year-old son of the deceased, in the course
of his evidence, said that on getting up at six o’clock
yesterday morning he saw his mother lying in a pad-
dock near the house. He went up and spoke to her,
and as she did not answer him he knew that she was
dead. There was a gun lying near the body. He did
not give any alarm, but went up and got the coui as
usual.—A recent news item.
The stars may vary in their shining courses,
The marriage ties*may tangle and dissever,
And Socialists may threaten our resources;
But Posy, like the brook, goes on for ever.
Aye, Posy, with her sacred teats and udder,
Is queen of all our wide dominions now ;
Before her shrine we must salaam and shudder —
Oh ! Heaven bless the Cow !
What matter if a wearied wife and mother
Ends all her woes and worries with a bullet?
Our grief in well-skimmed cow-juice we shall
smother —
But while a teat’s on Posy we must pull it!
The sun may stay behind the bleak horizon,
Unnoticed by the cow-slave who is bilked
Of all the things he fixes longing eyes on—
But Posy must be milked!
Philanthropists’ humanitarian measures
Are Socialistic, meddlesome and prosy.
An agitating curse is he who treasures
Those juveniles embrutalised and dozy.
What matter if the Horned Abomination
The voice of conscience, love and hope has stilled?
It was so from the very inchoation —
The milk-pails must be filled!
An infidel is he who boldly preaches
That Private Enterprise must be contrary
To all the precepts of Our Faith, and reaches
The milk of human kindness in the dairy.
So let us hang him to a handy rafter;
Yes, nearby where the sad cows ruminate,
In order that they, too, may join in laughter—•
But quick ! the milk-carts wait!
Let us uplift our voices in thanksgiving
'Because we’ve learned to stifle our emotion—•
Because we’ve found the Elixir of Living
Contained in Spot’s and Posy’s creamy potion !
And let us fill the House of God with pleading
To Him who runs the Milky Way on high—-
And answers to the slaver’s interceding—
That Posy won’t go “dry” !
And let us send our saints to save the savage,
Whose faith is false, whose thoughts are most
untender,
Whose simple soul the world’s temptations ravage,
That he allegiance true to God may render.
But never mind the brats whose hearts are broken
(The cannibals with scorn might view them
now) —
The young slaves, who with all their griefs unspoken,
Are sacrificed to Cow.
N.S.W.
R. J. CASSIDY.
A SINK OF INIQUITY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The people consume strong drink, they frequent
picture-shows on the Sabbath, Sunday is washing-
day with many of the women, and the men paint
their cottages on the Day of Rest. —Indictment of
Wonthaggi (Jc.) by the Rev. C. Thomas.
Wonthaggi, you are lost indeed,
A town deep-steeped in beer and
gin ;
Where very few are callous-kneed
Through - bending with the goodly
breed;
Where many do their sixpence in
On Sunday pictures—which is Sin.
In vain with sacerdotal gab
The pastor rises to explain ; •
The wicked men their paint-pots grab,
And where the town was painted drab
By saintly souls, these sons of Cain
Make haste to paint it red again.
For six; long days W'onthaggi men
Are toiling down among the coal,
Accumulating dirt, and then
On Sunday they wash clean again.
Eternal fire must be his dole
Who cleans his skin before his soul.
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.

“Vic” : At Ivanhoe, on the Yarra, in the
region where the late • engineer Thwaites wanted to
expand the river into a lake, the picture showmen
have lately held an exhibition of the landing of Bat-
man and his purchase of the Melbourne district
from the blafckfe. The affair was ludicrously incor-
rect. The Batman transaction was not in that
quarter; more probably it was on Plenty River or
Merri Creek. Batmaji carried the business through
without difficulty. He had N. S. Wales aboriginals
with him—men who had served him in pacifying
the. Tasmanian blacks. Certainly the N.S.W. blacks
and the Victorians understood each other fairly well
—it was like Scotch and Irish talkers qf Gaelic.
The document, preserved in the Melbourne Public
Library, was prepared long before by Gellibrand.
Only the price of the purchase—the blankets,
knives, etc.—had to be inserted. Then Jika Jika,
or rather Jaga Jaga, with the other chiefs, appended
their names, and the thing was finished. In this
matter Batman acted on behalf of the Port Phillip
Association, and no doubt that ; is why, when the
agreement was quashed, the Government paid up
about £3OOO as compensation.

“Hee Kee”: “Boronia” states (B.
25/l/’l2) that the jarrah pile skies the wipe some-
where beween the eight and fifteenth round in his
fight with Teredo Navalis, Esq. If this is so, per-
haps “Boronia” will come to light with some ex-
cuses for the longevity of the props of the old jetty
at Fremantle, the high level bridge at North Fre-
mantle —both of these were constructed some 50
years ago by the uniformed gent with the Govern-
ment brand on his pants—the Causeway Bridge,
and several other heavy-whiskered Westralian struc-
tures. In the Perth Museum there is a portion of a
jarrah pile which was submerged for about four
decades, and if “Boronia” were given a crack on
the head with a splinter of it, he’d wake up in
eternity. I’ll admit that some of the piles in the
more recently constructed Fremantle wharves have
had to be replaced. What the difference between the
ancient and modern jarrah is I cannot say, unless
it is that Jarrah, jun., is suffering from the curse
of Socialistic legislation, and isn’t as vigorous in
his youth as the old man was.
“Gouger,” concerning a plague of winged
cannibals:—
When the dirty old Barwon and Narran overran
their banks, aided by the tremendous flood waters
from Queensland, Lightning Ridge was left stranded,
with miles of water covering the box-tree flats on
every side. The flood brought down millions of mos-
quitoes, loaded with billions of eggs, which the
fiercely hot sun soon hatched. The next two months
was reinforced Sheol to man, beast and bird on the
opal field. The wretched horses made sleep almost
impossible for the first few nights of the plague, by
galloping over the ridges and crashing through the
small scrub, in their efforts to brush off the swarms
of winged devils that pursued them. After that
smouldering fires were made by burning green buddha,
and as long as these were tended and the suffocating
smokes kept up, the half-maddened beasts had some
relief. Before sundown long lines Of horses would
come in, and stand around waiting for the fires to
be lit. Then the animals would back into the
smoke and remain there all night, with only their
noses showing in the open. One morning in our
camp we found that two miserable kangaroo dogs
that had been left on the chain all night had perished,
and 14 fowls from the married people’s quarters were
dead—literally bitten to death. After that it was an
education to see every domesticated animal on the field
line up to the fires at sundown, the horses pushing
to their places, the fowls cackling and scurrving
through the horses’ legs, the dogs curled up, with
only their noses and front paws out qf the smoke,
while, on the outskirts, even the thick-skinned ?oats
would back their hinder parts into the reek. Cook-
ing and eating the evening meal was a dreadful opera-
tion. My mate and I would pile on the green bushes,
make a rush for the damper and plates, then squat
in the smoke, swallowing as quickly as we could half-
cooked chops, mosquitoes and smut, washing down
the black curses with pannikins of blacker tea.
Then we’d light a smouldering cow-dung fire at the
moijth of the tent, take our boots off in the rancid
smoke, and make one frenzied dive under the net
intd the -bunk, pinning the net down after us as if
50 ravenous bailiffs were scrambling to get in. But
the mosquitoes soon discovered how to get through
the! net, and after that there was no peace for the
field until an Indian unearthed a sunnlv of art mus-
lin. Had it not been that the flood was all round
us, Thus making escape impossible, Lightning Ridge
wou'ld have been abandoned in a fortnight.
Advices received from London intimate
that a Permanent Exhibition of Patents now exists
there. At this inventions can be tastefully displayed.
Diplomas, silver medals, a gold medal and a Grand
Prix are to be annually awarded. In a lecture
theatre a cinematograph machine will display various
exhibits at work. The promoters (learn patent attor-
neys and engineers, Phillips Ormonde & Co., of
256 Flinders St., Melbourne) appeal to all those
wbq want to buy or sell inventions or secure either
sales capital or publicity-*
“Iluia”: “The Haek”(B. 28/12/T1) seeks
information re the combustible properties of the
sperm whale. I have a bush place near Opotiki
(M.L.) and a humpy 100yds. from the beach. A
whale died about 500 miles out at sea, and after a
more or less protracted summer cruise, ran ashore
at my front gate. After weighing the cost of shift-
ing the shack, 1 attacked the stranded problem with
a respirator and a box of matches. The fireworks
display at the Crystal Palace is a fool to what fol-
lowed ; but the aroma was so overpowering that I
could not stay in the humpy until I had dragged
in a dead horse as an anti-toxin. Since then I
always scatter a few whales about the bush before
lighting, and I invariably get a good burn.
“N. or M.”: Tasniania’s whaling indus-
try, which has been moribund for the past 20 years,
is sitting up and taking notice, the battery having
been applied by a Norwegian co. Seven whales have
fallen to the harpoon of the Hobart and its attend-
ant Mimosa since the vessels began to exploit South
West Cape, and, if things continue to go thus well,
a trying-out base will be established at Port Davey.
Apparently the “fish” are ventur-
ing back to their old haunts, for,
last year, Smithton, on the
N.W. coast, was the over-
whelmed recipient of a stranded
school of 37. In the old and
thirsty days, oil and rum were
the two rivers of Pactolus, and
one of Tassy’s first publichouses
hung out the sign of “The Whale
Fishery.” This pub was opened
in 1805 by a man named Hop-
kins, a servant of Lieutenant-
Governor Collins, and the in-
augural supper was presided over
by “Bobby” Knopwood, the sport-
ing chaplain of the settlement.
At that time the Derwent oppo-
site the town was often so
crowded with spouting whales
that it was unsafe for a boat to
cross to Kangaroo Point (Belle-
rive). Whalers from all parts of
the world swooped upon the
harvest, and sailed off with gorged
holds until, the fisheries being
under no control, the whales were
almost exterminated and the in-
dustry fell down dead. The
most prosperous year was 1837,
when the value of the products
officially noted was £135,210;
but those figures did not include
cargoes taken by foreign whalers
who did not put into port. It
was a lavish and lawless time.
A crew of American whalers once
made a descent upon Maria
Island, and, overawing the repre-
sentatives of the law, set up their
trying-out plant, and refused to
budge. The Law dodged out by
a back door to Hobart Town, and
applied for soldiers or dynamite
or something, but by the time re-
inforcements came along, the trying-out was finished
and the Yanks had gone.

“Move-On” meets P. Enterprise, J.P.: —
When you go to Maoriland the Tourist people tell
you that you should by all means climb up the Wan-
ganui ; and then they pass you over to the tender
mercies of Private Enterprise. The trip by motor-
boat and steamer down the river is about 18 hours
wide and 140 miles long. The bare fare is two pun’
ten. Trippers have to stay the night before the
scart at Tamaranui and breakfast at the time when
the rooster tears the atmosphere with the anthem of
his beak. A halt is made at “The Houseboat” for
lunch, and about 6 p.m. the vessel reaches Pipiriki,
where everyone has to put up at the dry hotel.
You start next morning at some freezing hour and
breakfast on board “The Houseboat.” Feeds and
beds are charged for at stiff rates and make extra
profit for the trip-purveyors, who run the whole river.
The motor-boat on the first day carries passengers
only, but as we had about 40 aboard at £2 10s., I
reckon we could have bought the boat. The steamer
carries mails and goods, and half the time is occu-
TOUGH!
BOLL FROM OUTBACK: “Look 'ere, old bloke. If yer'd killed this blessed sheep when yer
wei'e a kid it would have saved me a ’ell of a lot of trouble .”
pied bumping into the bank and working bales of
wool aboard and dumping odd sacks of flour off to
vociferous Maoris. It mustn’t be imagined that
there’s any gilt-edged luxury attached to the tour
either. On the motor-boat some passengers scramble
for deck chairs ; the others have to be content with a
seat on a “form,” and as this part of the run fills
up about 10 hours, a whole lot get restive and want
to walk. Mostly the Government looks after the
peripatetic tourist, and travels him and feeds him and
sleeps him at reasonable rates. But the Wanganui
River is abandoned to Private Enterprise, which is
preserved here, I can only suppose, as a sort of
Horrible Example.
NOTHING TO FEAR.
Mothers need have no hesitation in giving Cham-
berlain’s Cough Remedy to their little ones, as it con-
tains absolutely no injurious drugs. This medicine is
not only perfectly safe to give to small children, but
it is a medicine of great worth and merit.*
“Lakekamu”: “Don Guillermo” (B.
23/11/’ll) is -not exact in all he says about the
Papuan as a laborer, but hits the nail on the. head
in quoting the munificent salary paid to the average
plantation overseer — £l2 per month, and a cook-
boy. The real trouble with the Papuan recruited
for plantation labor is that he is perhaps the first
of his tribe to ever do any real work. For hundreds,
and I may be safe in saying thousands, of years^the
natives have never done
more work than would sup-
ply them with food and wea-
pons, consequently the sud-
den jump from the Stone Age
to the Steel cannot be made
in a year or even 10 years.
It has taken the European
centuries to attain his present
civilisation; and surely the
native cannot be expected to
leave off the habits of his
centuries all in a moment. It
is as if “Don Guillermo” tried
to harness a Brontosaurus into
a bread cart, and expected
the beast to finish the round
without breaking anything.
Moreover, progress in convert-
ing the raw material into a
good laborer is slower than it
otherwise would be, because
most of the overseers „ are
quite new to the coun-
try, and know nothing either
of the language or of colored
people. No wonder, then,
that the natives don’t under-
stand what the boss wishes
done. Patience is the only thing
to use when breaking in a raw
mob of Papuans, and it isn’t
the easiest thing to cultivate,
especially when you’ve got a
Board of directors that meet?
in a comfortable office afar
off and clamors for returns.
Astonishing the effect of circumstances on
cases. Here are the most scornful foes of
badges, signets and regalia, as worn by
unionists, putting up quite a feverish fight
against the efforts of the Melbourne Uni-
versity students to throw off their absurd
gowns, and the mortar-board headgear that
is more ridiculous than a Fijian’s coiffure,
and less picturesque than the top-hamper
of a Papuan. “Jawn” Madden says Austra-
lians, more than any other people, have
need “to retain the seemly accompaniments
of the discharge of grave business. (Ap-
plause.).” At his suggestion the University
Council resolved to enforce the wearing of
gowns by means of stiff fines. “Jawn” is
shocked at the manner in which Australian
democracy is battering down the walls of
Caste, and is greedy to have “class” cling
to its regalia, so that, when it loses its ram-
parts, it may entrench itself behind its rags.
True, the tailor makes the man—but always
the man who is incapable of making himself.
Nothing doubtful in “Montserrat” Lime Juice.
It’s good and pure. Being the genuine juice of cul-
tivated lime fruit, “Montserrat” is a grand, healthy
drink for the hot weather.*

“Boswell” visits -the site of a continent’s
capital:—
About a mile from the main road, which runs
through Kink O’Malley’s Federal property, a num-
ber of cadets were sheltering from the heat wave
under a clump of trees. They had been lunching,
and their discarded paper bags came scurrying across
the plain before a head wind, end over end, until
they pulled up against the rabbit-proof fence guard-
ing the road. There wasn’t a vestige of grass to
intercept the fugitive bags over the whole distance.
Further along the road, hundreds of rabbits lay rot-
ting the sun, emitting a fouler stench as each
successive vehicle scrunched over them. Afterwards
we crossed the Queanbeyan or Canberra “river.” It
was higher than usual, for there had been an over-
night shower up-stream; but the most I could
discern was a few willows, a bed of stones and sand,
and a puddle like that which lies by the roadside
after a heavy storm. The puddle had not been im-
proved by the invasion of a regiment of geese intent
on the extermination of the tadpole kingdom.
An interesting liltle craft is lying in Rose
Bay, Sydney. Built in 1874 as a racing
schooner, it was christened the Olga. Then
its name was changed to the Privateer, and
it became the cruising yacht of a wealthy
owner. Some years afterwards a total re-
formation was made in its habits ; it became
the property of the London Missionary So-
ciety, and for a quarter of a century it has
been dodging the Devil and the coml reefs
under the name of the George Brown —so
called after the “father” of British mission-
aries in the South Seas. The old craft has
now been purchased by a Norwegian, who
is fitting it out as a deep-sea houseboat. His
idea is to take his family long cruises around
the Australian coast and through the Dutch
Archipelago, and sniff out likely mineral
propositions as he goes. So the George Brown
becomes the Grethe, Ihe new name being that
of the wife of the new owner.
The recent big fire at Briscoe’s, in Mel-
bourne, demonstrated a scientific fact and
offered a powerful hint to architects and
builders. The warehouse was a brick shell,
crossed and laced by girders, with a brick
dividing wall fitted with fire-proof doors,
and with fire-and-burglar-proof doors at the
rear. When the firemen had at last got the
upper hand the back portion of the store
was a blackened mass, feet deep in ashes.
The front was in its normal state, with
clerks and storemen busy among the stock
as usual. The rear doors, made of heavy
sheet iron, were warped and twisted all
ways, but the doors in the dividing wall
showed little sign of damage. They had
been built of Tasmanian blue gum sheathed
with thin sheet iron, on the principle that
hard wood must have a free supply of air
in order to combust, and as the heat was
never quite severe enough to melt the iron
and let the air in freely, the wood had stood
the test.
THE NEW BOHEMIA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Vienna, Wednesday.—The miners of Bohemia de-
mand a 25 p.c. increase in wages.— Cable.
The miners of Bohemia, a sordid crew are they,
AVho ask for faithful labor a slight increase in pay ;
Not so the old Bohemian who mined with brush and
pen,
And treasure gave for nothing to shallow surface
men.
The old Bohemian miners—deep sinkers that we
knew—
Are mostly underground now, or sinking someone's
brew ;
But reefs that they discovered are making new men
rich,
While in some dark bar parlor their palms for
“thrippence” itch.
The miners of Bohemia? No, no! they don’t exist!
The world knows not their labor! Like figures in a
mist
They pass and are forgotten. The world of them,
bereft,
Can find of them no traces, but treasure they have
left.
N.S.W.
E. FRANCIS.

Kilmorc, Sunday.
Alter two days of Ideal weather the
troops have been called upon to lace
the discomfort of rain, borne on the
wings of a gale. The men were cn-
gaged watering their horses when rain
burst over the camp. They made for .
the tents as quickly as possible.. The
Commandant (Colonel Wallack) pointed
out that it was no use the troops being
sent out in the rain, for many of them
had come to the camp without a change
of uniform, and It was a very easy
matter for Influenza to attack a large
body of troops. However, so that the
morning should not altogether be lost,
a series of lectures was arranged.
