A streak of great joy fell upon Australia
last week, when one Harold Farleigh wrote
to 1 rime Minister Fisher to propose com-
pulsory church attendance for all Austra-
lians between the ages of 12 and 21. The
writer gave his solemn assurance that if
this matter was duly fixed up, a blessing
wouhl rest upon Fisher “like a dove.”
\\ hether Fisher likes the idea of a dove or
a sparrow or a vulture or any other bird
i esting upon lum is nnotlier matter.
Ex-Justice Ilolroyd, of Victoria, is writ-
ing his Reminiscences. He should, though
he probably won’t, include in the book the
yarn of how the present Melbourne Gas. Co.
was fixed up There used to be three cos.
u the city, but in 1878 they were united,
and the cost of running the fusing scheme
through I arliament was £6700. Some share-
holders started to growl, but the wiseacres
winked and said, “Mum’s the word.” Ilol-
royd, then the leader of the Vic. Bar, was in
charge of the business, and he and Webb
(also afterwards on the Bench) drafted the
Bd! and put it through the Select Commit-
tee. lhe poor old public wasn t represented
by even a clerk from the Crown Law Office •
hence the pleasure, with all its cunning
draftsmanship, got through with no light to
speak ot thrown on it. At £6700 the job
was dirt-cheap.
An unadvertised Australian, who is doing
particularly well in London, is artist “Jack”
Rower, of Adelaide. Power has made him-
self something of a luminous spot in the
tog by virtue of his ability and grit. His
particular forte is animal painting. He
draws and paints dogs with peculiar skill,
and makes courageous adventures into big
hunting subjects. His talent in this line
has secured him a clientele among the hunt-
ing classes in England—classes that have
beans, and are not chary of scattering the
desirable vegetable. Power had to make a
mg light, but he is now quite “there,” and
his paintings are featured at most of the
shows ot any importance.
Passed over Jordan recently, from Auck-
land, Eugene Bellairs, who tasted life when
it was raw, and frequently dripping, in the
early days of Maoriland. lie was one of
the few who escaped from the massacre of
Luropeans at Wairau by Rangihaeata,
nephew and lieutenant of the Napoleonic
Kauparaha, as the upshot of a land deal.
Bellairs told some hair-raising accounts of
that horror, but, at the time, horrors were
too common to occasion undue surprise, and
Bellairs, like many another, was no sooner
clear away with his life than lie was again
tossing bones with Fate for it. He was
through the subsequent Waikato war and
several other enthusiastic disputes, but
Death overlooked him or forgot him or soine-
tmng until the other day, when he passed
out quietly just as the clock was striking
00.

From the Oldest Inhabitant:—
A controversy has
arisen in the S. M.
Herald as to the iden-
tity of the first ice-
maker in Australia.
The demolition of
Obed West’s old
home in Barcom Glen,
where Mr. Nicolle had
his first plant, gave
rise to the argument.
Nicolle started his
works in 1860 ; but in
the very first ’6o’s
this writer was pre-
sent, .in a primitive
building in Franklyn-
street, near Elizabe'th-
street, Melbourne,
when the first bar of
ice (the first so far as
Melbourne was con-
cerned) —• something
like pig-iron—w a s
turned out there by a
Mr. Harrison, a Gee-
long newspaper pro-
prietor, who had pro-
duced the commodity
at the sleepy town on
Corio Bay, before es-
tablishing the Frank-
lyn-street works; so
it is highly probable
that the first ice made
in Australia was produced at Geelong. Harrison after-
wards represented the allegedly-sleepy town in Par-
liament.

John Ridley, who probably invented the
reaping-machine (there were other claim-
ants), is to have his memory definitely hon-
ored in South Australia at last. ’ The
Chamber of Manufactures had a trifle of
£l5O in hand towards a memorial, and now
an anonymous donor has given £lOOO more,
with the idea of founding a scholarship at
the Agricultural College, Roseworthy. The
first result will probably be to revive the
old controversy once more.
The Henri Salmet who, according to a
cable of the 7th inst., did a non-stop flight
from London to Paris in three hours ”5
minutes is chief instructor in the Bleriot
school in London. The feat has only been
achieved once before, and, curiously enough
it was by Salmet’s predecessor at the Bleriot
school, Pierre Prier. Prier took three
hours db minutes over the job. Shortly
afterwards he joined the Bristol Aeroplane
Co., and designed for it its famous mono-
plane, the most efficient article of the sort
on the British market. The Frenchman
dominates the flying business to a painful
extent in the U.K. just now.
Died at Manly (N.S.W.) last week, aged
1 0, J. P. W right, one of the Sydney men
who touched things and turned them to
gold. His wife, who died only the other
day, talked as proudly as her husband did
about their “start” in N. S. Wales. He
was a bootmaker, and they tramped up to
Bathurst, where he got a job at his trade.
Ihe shop he worked in-was his before long—
and that was how things usually turned out.
He capsized the old idea about the cobbler
sticking to his last for, though he remained
head of Wright and Co., the big shoe-manu-
tacturing firm, he also practically ran the
-Laylor Bros, jam business, and dabbled in
coal, woollens and a heap of other things.
He was a good Protectionist, and also for
s °™. . was .th e employers’ representative
on the Arbitration Court.

NO OCCUPATION.

am I to do now ?”

PRIVATE JANE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The negotiations for a union of the three Liberal
organisations in Victoria, the People’s Liberal party
(until recently known as the Commonwealth Liberal
party), the People’s party and the Australian Wo-
men s National League, have been reopened The
hope is expressed that the tactics of the Australian
Women’s National League in insisting on preserving
its separate identity will be modified so that an
effective union may lie attained. There are no signs
of that league making any real sacrifice in the in-
terests of unity.— Melb. Age.
As the first faint rays of daylight on the battle-field
are falling
Comes the sound of dim things stirring and of ner-
vous voices calling:
“That you, People?” “Y'es, I’m People!” “Right-
o! This is People here!”
Then the sound of something ripping on a thorny
point. “Oh, dear!
Here I’m in another tangle! Help me quickly some-
one ! ’Otv!”
In reply both People’s voices wildly bellow. “Wot’s
up now!?
W’y you’re alius gettin’ tangled w’en we’re startin’
a campaign !
W’y carn’t you put on trousis like a soljer, Private
Jane?”
“BRUTES!... .1 want to look a lady when I
face the enemy !
And how dare you even think I’d let the Labah
party see
I had limbs like any servant! Why, good gracious,
are you mad?
NO! I simply won’t unsex myself for any stupid
fad!
And now —if you’ve no more questions—will you
come and cut me free?
But please note that I preserve my feminine iden-
tity !”
So the trousered allies, growling, go to help the
skirted one,
And make ready for the battle ere the rising of the
sun.
When the sun is brightly shining, and the time is
past for marching,
Two distracted parties caper round a female busy
starching
Two new blouses bought for battle at a city bargain
sale,
While the drums of Labor legions rumble fiercely
down the vale:
“If yer won’t put on th’ trousis, will yer wear a
coat an’ ’at.
Then yer might look like a soljer w’en be’ind some
rocks yer sat!
<t° on, Jane! Do make yer mind up, ’fore the place
goes up in smoke!
Will, yer wear an ’at or ’elmet?” “NO! I’ll
wear my velvet toque!
And it’s no use you men talking, for I’m satisfied
I’m right!
And I won’t go out to battle looking like a perfect
fright!
The idea! Me in trousers! Why, I really never
heard
Such impertinence and rubbish! It's too utterly
absurd!
If you really are so anxious for this uniformity,
Then I give you both permission to attire yourselves
like me!”
So they argue while the thunder of the drums hangs
in the air
On a question of great import, namely: What is
Jane to wear?
N.S.W.

The Mutual Life and Citizens’ Assur-
ance Cos., Ltd., which is the Citizens’ Life Associa-
tion with the M.L.A. and the Australian Widows’
Fund grafted on to it:—
Ordinary Life Department.

1910 .. 941,605 481,526 84,252 2,524 373,303
1911 .. 1,047,150 534,801 95,864 5,275 411,210
This co. has done better work for life insurance in
Australia than any other office in the past 20 years.
In the first place, it pushed Industrial insurance,
and, though that is comparatively poor stuff, it is
better than nothing, and its quality is_ improving;
moreover, the system trains hundreds of people to
become Ordinary policy-holders who wouldn’t have
any sort of insurance at all if they didn’t have an
Industrial policy to begin with. But that is per-
haps the least of this office’s big services. Somehow
or other it developed a remarkable business organisa-
tion, partly no doubt because the men who were
running it were going to have a share in the profits
that would come with success. For many years one
great Australian office strode out far ahead of all
others. Its competitors didn’t push it anywhere in
the race. Naturally, the time came when it almost
forgot that it was in a race at all. At last it woke
up to the fact that somebody was rushing up along-
side it. Still, the great office might not have
worried much if the men who were in charge of the
Citizens’ hadn’t grasped out and brought first the
M.L.A. and then the Widows’ Fund under their
roof. That not only made an office big enough to
command attention everywhere; but the fact that
these two old-established offices, run by the most
conservative men, were prepared to come in, gave
the Citizens’ and its assets a certificate that would
be a good enough introduction anywhere. The race
became really serious then; and thus, by setting
something like anew standard, especially in the
matter of expenses, the Citizens’ (which became the
M.L.C. after the amalgamation) did a service to tens
of thousands of people who haven’t got an M.L.C.
policy and perhaps never will have one.
A word was said just now about the remarkable
business organisation having been due more or less
to the fact that the men who ran the Citizens’ were
interested in the profits. Under the purely mutual
system the policy-holders get all the profits. The
mutual system doesn’t, however, make profits. James
Garvan, the founder of the Citizens’, tried to devise
a scheme that would do that. He therefore proposed
that one-fifth of the profits should go to share-
holders, who would be mostly men in the co.’s ser-
vice. It isn’t much use arguing whether the plan
succeeded or not. It is certain that something suc-
ceeded. The one-fifth of the profits are still, of
course, paid. Down to five years ago the payment
was lumped with “surrenders and bonuses,” and in-
cluded with the claims. In the table on top this
practice is followed throughout for the sake of uni-
formity; but if the truer plan is followed, and the
bonus is added to the exes., the results would show
up as follows:—•
Written To Re-
Income. Claims. Expenses. Off. serves.
1908.. £667,435 £262,576 £86,405 £157,054 £161,400
1909.. 692,089 308,232 71,644 1,745 310,468
1910.. 941,605 464,638 101,140 2,524 373,300
1911.. 1,047,150 515,005 115,660 5,275 411,213
The position with regard to policy-holders’ bonuses
is this: to compete with other offices, four-fifths of
the M.L.C.’s profits must be as good as five-fifths of
those in the purely mutual office. The M.L.C.’s claim
is that self-interest saves so much waste and secures
so much better service that it pays the policy-holders
handsomely to share the profits to 7hat extent.
The figures are unquestionably very striking. Even
if the shareholders’ bonus is reckoned in with the
expenses, the rate for last year was only £ll os. lid.
p.c. of the income notwithstanding the big volume
of new business. Charging the old or renewal busi-
ness 7 p.c., the new business cost 89 p.c. of the first
year’s premiums—an extraordinary achievement. Ex-
cluding the shareholders’ one-fifth, the new-business
cost was 61 p.c.; and an office which can do that
has certainly some remarkable force at the back of
it. As for the policies taken over from the M.L.A.
and the Widows’ Fund, the trustees for the latter
report: “The average annual surplus for the three
years five months ending March 31, 1910 (the
date of the union) was £32,223 on a 3-J p.c. basis
of valuation. On the same basis of valuation, the
surplus for 1911 would be about double that aver-
age.” In other words, under the management of the
M.L.C., profits available for bonuses have been just
about doubled.

The expense rate shown there is 40.9 p.c. of the in-
come. That is much better than it used to be, when
Industrial business was started in Australia, but
still 4|d. in the shilling is too much money to spend
in chasing business and collecting premiums. Yet
the only visible plan for bringing the cost much
lower is the universal insurance of wage-earners, the
premiums being collected by the employers and paid
in by them ; and unfortunately that would leave out
a whole lot of people who ought to be insured.
The Widows’ Fund had an Accident business and
the M.L.A. had policies with Accident benefits. All
this business has been put into one branch, which is
making considerable progress:—
Accident Branch.
Expenses
(including To Re- Re-
Income. Claims. Divs.). serves, serves.

It has been very profitable so far; but English ex-
perience shows that it is a class of business liable to
fly up and hit the underwriter at unexpected times
and in unexpected places. For that reason, no doubt,
instead of passing much of the surplus to the credit
of the co., the latter is building up large contin-
gency reserves.
When the insurance canvasser is hooking a
fish the first thing the fish ought to be sure about
is the stability of the basket he is going to be
landed into. Big bonuses are all very well; but the
bonus won’t be paid perhaps for 30 years, and a lot
of bad things may happen in 30 years. The office
with a share capital has a small advantage here.
The purely mutual office can’t pay out more than the
policy-holders have paid in, plus interest earnings,
no matter what may have been promised them.
There is no outsiders’ fund on which they can draw.
With a share capital there is another fund; and if
the policy-holders themselves haven’t put in enough
to pay the face value of the policy and the declared
bonuses, or if the money has been lost in bad invest-
ments, then the shareholders must make up the bal-
ance, to the extent of the subscribed capital. This
is no doubt calculated to make this office prettv
careful about its investments, and probably explains
why it has nearly half (49 p.c.) of its total assets
in public securities. Of the balance 39 p.c. is on
mortgage, and only a trifle over 2 p.c. is sunk in
freehold property. The Ordinary branch funds earned
£4 10s. 3d. p.c., the Industrial branch £4 7s. Bd.
p.c., and the Accident branch £4 9s. lid. p.c. Yet,
in spite of these earnings, the M.L.C. section (which
includes all current business) bases all its calcula-
tions on a 3 p.c. rate of earnings; and all the sur-
plus actually earned above that rate will be avail-
able for bonuses.

*This includes “premiums purchasing paid-up policies
allocated from cash bonuses not drawn.”
tThis includes £17,221 “cash bonus declared for
1910.”
That calls for a little explanation. Until 12 months
ago this co., like most others in the life .insurance
business, did not show its bonus payments in its
statement of revenue and expenditure. Then, how-
ever, it changed the form of its accounts and set
out two new items; on the expenditure side: “Cash
bonus declared for year”; on the other, “Single
premiums, including premiums purchasing paid-up
policies allocated from cash bonuses not drawn.”
It is probable that the “single premiums” are
wholly or almost wholly the society’s bonuses for
the year, employed to purchase additions to the
policy-holders’ insurance; and if the £16,033 repre-
sented by single premiums is deducted, the year’s
income was £134,217. That makes a fairer com-
parison with previous figures.
This society confesses that it isn’t aiming at big
business figures. There are one or two very strong
British offices which don’t canvass at all, and rely
upon the saving in exes., and the consequent big
bonuses, to get new business for them. There is a
lot of wisdom in the scheme —-provided too many
don’t adopt it —and The Bulletin has tried to
bring the Commonwealth Government up to the
scratch and establish an insurance office on these
lines. Meantime the City Mutual follows the plan
with variations. It canvasses, certainly, but it de-
clines to force business, on the principle that it’s
the last possible £IOOO that costs most to get, where-
fore it leaves off before it reaches the last possible
£IOOO. The rate, nevertheless, is still high:—
Expenses Compared With Total Income.
1902 ..£23 3 2 p.c. 1907 ..£22 18 2 p.c.
1903 .. 22 3 9 „ 1908 .. 25 17 1 „
1904 ~ 21 13 7 ~ 1909 .. 27 7 7
1905 .. 22 4 7 „ 1910 .. 23 16 4 ~
1906 .. 24 5 6 „ 1911 .. 20 13 10 ~
Almost everything depends, though, on the proportion
of new business secured: obviously an office that
dozed and merely received renewal premiums and
paid out claims could run itself at very little ex-
pense. If this co.’s renewal business is charged 7
p.c., then the new business cost 91 p.c. of the first
year’s premiums; and over that record the office is
quite justified in throwing bouquets at itself. And
in the art of making bouquets and throwing them
at his society managing director Crowley, of the
City Mutual, isn’t behind any other insurance person
.in Australia. The worst 'trouble of the small office,
as a rule, is to keep the policy-holder after it has
got him. But in that respect this office fares better
than most. Last year more surrender values were
exhausted than usual; yet the proportion to new
premiums received was no more than 74 p.c., and
in the great A.M.P. it was as much as 60 p.c.
Bonuses come from three principal sources—from
the reduction of costs below the rate assumed in fix-
ing the premium; from interest earnings in excess
of the assumed rate; and from a better mortality
experience than was expected. This office can’t show
much in the way of low costs, but its interest earn-
ings are unusual. On the average funds at com-
mand last year they amounted to £5 7s. 4d. p.c.
—very nearly 1 p.c. above the rate earned by most
societies; and even f p.c. on the funds means '£3735
a year available for bonuses. High interest earn-
ings, however, often mean big risks and large losses
through depreciation of securities. In the table on
top of these remarks there is a column showing
amounts written off. Down to three years agp these
were chiefly sums written off securities, and the bulk
of them must be set against the high interest earn-
ings. During the past three years the totals have been
larger; but £IO,OOO of the aggregate is still to the
credit of a contingency fund—a provision, no doubt,
for probable losses in the future. Asa matter of
fact, the profit on the sale of securities in 1910 was
£1578 in excess of the amount written off securities
(other than furniture, etc.), and last year the net
profit was £2362. Even allowing that jt has been
comparatively easy to make profits in the rising
markets of the past few years, the society’s record
in the matter of net earnings has been very good
indeed. The mortality experience is algo good; and
when policy-holders live longer and therefore pay
in more premiums, and allow of the accumulation
of more interest than the actuaries calculated upon,
the actual revenue in excess of the anticipated
revenue goes to swell the bonuses. The bonuses as
for 1910 were worth £17,221 in cash: this was
equal to 21 p.c. of the renewal premiums received
during 1911. Considering that the office is neither
big nor fashionable, and therefore has to battle for
all it gets, this is a good result —provided always
that the actuarial reserves are calculated on a suffi-
ciently stringent basis.

F. W. Hughes, Ltd. (Sydney), manufac-
turers of wool-tops, who commenced business (so far
as this co. is concerned) with a deficit of £11,940,
representing goodwill ( £11,566) and preliminary
exes.:—
Written
Profits. Dividends. Off. Deficit.
Maj r , 1909..
Dec., 1909.. £17,411 7p.c.= £4,998 £4OOO 3,527
Reserves.
Dec., 1910.. 18,587 10 „ ~ 13,357 1,703
Dec., 1911.. 12,506 6 ~ „ 9,329 4000 880
The Hughes firm had been carrying on a wool-scouring
business for many years at Botany, and the proSpeec
of a Commonwealth bonus on wool tops produced for
export led to the promotion of this co, to carry the
scouring a step farther. The new capital asked for
was for an equipment of combing and other
machinery. Under the Bounties Act of 1907 a
maximum of £50,000 was set apart to encourage
this industry, payable at the rate of £IO,OOO a year
from 1909 onwards. Other tops are produced in
Australia, but, so far as this writer knows, only
for use in Australian manufactures ; and these goods
don’t carry any bounty. Apparently, therefore, the
only call for bounty is made by this co.; and, un-
less the present law is altered, the assistance will
come to an end in 1914 —which isn’t very far to look
forward to. So far the co. has published only three
sets of accounts, and only once did it set out separ-
ately the revenue derived from the bonus. That was
in 1910, when £6688 was drawn from the Com-
monwealth Treasury. The output of tops in that
year was 1,060,048 lbs., and as the bonus payable was
lid., there obviously wasn’t much unexported stock
as at Dec. 31 to draw bonus upon. Last year the
output was 2,248,000 lbs., and as lid. was still pay-
able, £14,050 would have been drawn if there hadn’t
been a limit of £IO,OOO set on any one year’s draw-
ings. If the full £IO,OOO was drawn—and that
much seems certain —it is plain that there was very
little profit apart from the bonus, which, as remarked
just now, comes to an end in 1914 under present
arrangements, \vith the current year the payment
is reduced to Id. per lb.; but ns the output is
expected to reach 2,800,000 lbs., the maximum will
still be called for. It is possible that, inasmuch
as only a few hundred pounds were drawn in 1909,
something in excess of £IO,OOO a year is now paid
out; if so the profit apart from the bonus is still
further reduced. In that case the prospect becomes
still less cheerful. The bounties may certainly be
renewed, perhaps on a reduced scale; but Parliament
will no doubt want to know first what amount of
labor the new industry is employing. 4nd it will
have to be admitted that top-making is a mighty
poor substitute for cloth-making so far as employ-
ment is concerned. Still, though the financial pros-
pects wouldn’t be very bright without the bonus,
the bottom wouldn’t actually drop out of the
business, as a market is being built for the tops
and “the demand continues to be most satisfactory”
(directors’ report, 31/12/’,11).
The capital is £IBO,OOO in £1 shares, on which
£15,011 is uncalled. Overdrafts and trade debts
total £58,734; on the other side, stocks and liquid
assets total £71,673, The goodwill item is now
exceeded by the reserves, even after allowing, for
the dividend; but there appears to be some addi-
tional paper in the item—“ Plant, premises, machin-
ery, lease and water rights, £158,874.”
The Colonial Meat Export Cos., Ltd., seems to
be half-cousin or something to P. W. Hughes, Ltd.
It has the same chairman, and the two directors who
were re-elected this year afe directors also of
Hughes, Ltd. Its object is to pack meat, mainly for
export; and probably it has friendly relations with
the other co. with regard to the disposal of the mut-
ton’s overcoat. This co-operation could no doubt be
to the cos.’ mutual advantage, though there is pretty
certain to be some grumbling about it, especially
when one co. paid only 6 p.c. last year while the
other paid 10 p.c.

In 1907 this co., after just 30 years’ hard gouging,
decided that it was about time it looked for an-
other mine; because a 3ft. seam won’t last for
ever, even if you have got about 3500 acres to peck
at. The unfortunate part of the business was that
the co. hadn’t built up a fund for the return of
capital or the purchase of another mine. Yet it did
a lot better than it deserved, under the circum-
stances, and, though it had to dj;op dividends for a
while, a brisk trade at the good- prices fixed by the
Vend enabled it to buy and equip anew mine—the
Whitburn, in the Maitland district —without getting
into a bog of debt. This shows the position now
and as at. June, 1907, just before the new land was
bought:—
June, 1907. Dec., 1911.
Liquid assets . . £16,881 £19,402
Liabilities .. .. 3,336 15,773
Surplus . . . . £13,545 £3,629
The new mine contains 546 acres freehold and a small
area under lease from the State; and, though this has
been bought, opened up and equipped, the co. has
now only £9916 less of a cash surplus than it had
just before the deal was made. (“Gash” here in-
cludes stores.) The Ocean part of the old mine has
lately been abandoned, but the new mine is now a
steady producer, and is showing good faces. But it
isn’t a big area, and the co, ought to start build-
ing up a fund for either the return of capital or
the purchase of more lands. It can’t expect to go
on having the same, luck as it has had over the
Whitburn; a bad market, for instance, would have
put it pretty close to the rocks. The capital is
£IIO,OOO in shares of £ll each paid up to £lO.

After one or two false starts this societj' went to the
Court 101 years ago and got a clean sheet on condi-
dition that it wrote off one-half its share capital
and paid the old depositors 4 p.c. p.a.
That transaction accounts for the profits on pro-
perty sales shown above; they are really only the
surpluses realised over the severely written-down
values, and some of the dividend paid nowadays
from that source is merely a return of capital for-
merly wiped out as lost. That sort of thing will
necessarily stop some day, because the properties
are not infinite. This little table shows, in fact,
that there is not a great deal left:—
July, ’Ol. Jan., ’ll. July, ’ll. Jan., ’l2.
Liabilities .. £37,015 £33,188 £35,877 £36,182
Advances .. 19,998 70,455 80,388 95,486
Foreclosures.. 72,305 35,266 30,973 19,932
Cash, etc. .. 2,778 911 234 340
Thus the society had £72,305 of properties on hand
101 years ago, and there is only £19,932 (book
value) left now; hence on past records no more
than £SOOO or £6OOO further “profit on property
sales” can be expected. But while the profits from
this source are nearing an end, it is worth noting
that the dividend has never absorbed all the ordinary
profit; so unless some bad accident happens, the
stoppage of profits from sales shouldn’t affect the
dividend. The biggest risk lies in the possibility
of a slump in the value of the properties on which
advances have been made, or which have been sold
on terms. But building societies ought to be on
velvet so far as most of their advances are concerned.
They fall in when the boom is sudden and the burst
quick—when the people who have borrowed money
on inflated securities haven’t had time to pay off
n _* an y instalments. This present boom hasn’t been
that sort: it has been gradual and prolonged, and
so many instalments have been paid on the majority
of time-payment bargains that no ordinary shrink-
age could reduce the value below the amount that
remains owing on the security. That’s the good
side of building society business to-dav. The
gloomier side is that the State will almost
for certain go on doing more and more to
help the people get homes on the best pos-
sible terms as to loans and repayments. In other
words, building societies are right up against the
most powerful of competitors, who isn’t looking for
any profit whatever.

This co., with a capital of £102,758, owed about
£300,000, mainly to depositors, when the boom-
bubble burst just on 20 years ago. Practically all
of this money was in mortgages and vacant lands;
and the return was so poor that in 1895 depositors
consented to accept 2J p.c. as a permanent thing.
It was proposed then to revalue the assets and write
down the capital; but on reviewing the situation it
seemed very doubtful whether the capital was big
enough to fill up the gap, so nothing was done.
Little by little the assets have been realised, and
they have mostly brought so much less than the old
boom value that £78,651 of the capital has already
disappeared, that being approximately the difference
between the cost of the amount realised for the
assets so far sold. There are still £34,615 of assets
awaiting realisation, plus £10,632 represented by
“borrowers and land purchasers’ balances, including
foreclosed vacant land.” The co, owes £23,399;
deduct £1556 cash in hand = £21,843. To pay
that debt there is £45,950 of assets of various kinds.
In the past 10 years things have moved like this:—
„ ' 1901. 1911.
Net. liabilities .. .. £87,480 £21,843
Non-liquid assets.. .. 128,733 45,950
Thus £82,783 of assets have paid off £65,637 of
debt. At the same rate the remaining £45,950 of
assets should realise about £36,000, which would
pay off the rest of the debt and leave nearly £16,000
for shareholders. But whether the sediment will pan
out worse or better than the lot already got rid of
this paper doesn’t know. All it knows is that the
directors report that “valuations of the co.’s princi-
pal freehold lands were made during the year by
Richardson and Wrench,” and the directors “find
that the book values were consistent with such valua-
tions.” Which may mean that the shareholders may
save a few pounds more out of the wreckage than
was calculated above.

Perhaps that last line is right, and perhaps it isn’t.
During the year 12,000 new shares were issued, ap-
P ar ’ an h ss. was called up on them,
the directors refer to the dividend for the past half-
year as “at the rate of 10 p.c. p.a., with a bonus of
2s p.c., equal in all to 9d. per share for the period.”
there are 52,000 shares, and 9d. per share means
£1950, whereas 7} p.c. on the paid capital
( £23,000) means £1725. Previously £IOOO (5
p.c.) had been paid; so whether the year’s total
div. is £2725 or £2950 isn’t certain from the ac-
counts. Also £897, “payment in respect of new
shares, has been appropriated out of profits.
That may be a bonus or dividend, or it may be
the excessive exes, of the new issue. In the table
it has been deducted from the profits; but it is all
sloppy and uncertain, and sloppiness isn’t a good
asset to bring to the assistance of a concern of this
kind.
Twenty years ago the capital was £40,000, and
the co. owed £25,000 on mortgage, and about
£6OOO in other, debts. At that time it had a
leasehold, which it was writing down in a leisurely
way. . Even before the boom broke it wasn’t doing
anything extra, and was paying only 4 to 5 p.c.
dividend. After the smash it did worse still.
Values had of course shrivelled; nevertheless it
hung on to its old valuations until 1897, when it
decided to face the trouble and write cauital down
to £20,000. At the end of 1897 it ‘still owed
£26,516 on mortgage. For some years it paid
only bread and cheese dividends, and put all the
rest into the premises or used it to reduce the mort-
gage. Twelve months ago all it owed was £15,987.
But as times grew more prosperous the old microbe
bit the management, and the divs. bolted from 4 p.c.
in 1906 to 121 p.c. in 1911. Moreover, the co.
plunged into more buildings—which perhaps were
justified—-and the reduction of the mortgage has been
forgotten. Apparently the building additions are
going to cost over £IO,OOO ; but if it stops at that,
the new share issue will pay for the lot. Apart
from the management’s recent inclination to “go
gay,” the record of the past 12 years has been very
good. In the 11 years to the end of 1910 the
excess of liabilities over liquid assets was reduced
from £25,760 to £15,760 —an exact £IO,OOO less.
And over £3OOO had been spent on the freehold.
Last year more capital was got in and a good deal of
money was spent on buildings, so the calculation
stops at the end of 191 Q. Business ha? been boom-
ing; revenue, which was down to £14.191 in 1899,
reached £23.999 last year. But the time will come
when it will probably creep well down towards
£14,191 again; 'for the city caravanserai which
caters for the country visitor is hit more than most
when bad seasons come, and the giddy time in the
city is scratched as a luxury that can be done with-
out.

Cosmopolitan Prop. (W.A.) has evidently
left its once profitable Itookynie mine to its _ fate.
The plant has beeh sold, and part of the mill is
going to the Bullfinch. Until the flat reef went
wrong in depth the property was one of the best
gold producers in the State, and paid £129,000
in dividends.
The other day Rowans’ Find took a crush-
ing out of its newly-acquired Morgans show (W.A.),
and announced itself perfectly satisfied with the re-
sult. Now news is to hand that the return did not
pay, and that in, consequence the mill has been
closed down. To clear the matter up some official
light ought to made available.
* * * *
V. F. Shallcross, managing director of
the British and Foreign Development Syndicate,
is getting excited. , Mines Minister Collier got back
at him in a minute replying to Shallcross’s state-
ment that the application for forfeiture of the
Queen’s Cross mine at Coolgardie was based on a
mere technical breach of the labor conditions.” Collier
says that “it was a most flagrant evasion of the
conditions of lease.” The Warden recommended a
fine of £25; the Minister overruled him and for-
feited the show, and then Shallcross talked of “at-
tacks on investors.” Shallcross’s Syndicate is also in
trouble with Fraser’s. The Syndicate has treated its tn-
butors badly, and now asks for 12 months’ exemption.
Joe Kearney is up against the shepherds with an
application for forfeiture. Fraser’s has three lodes
developed by 41 miles of drives, crosscuts, shafts and
winzes, and showing a capacity of production of
200,000 tons per annum. Shallcross’s Syndicate has
been running at a loss of £lll a month, and Shall-
cross says that £IOO,OOO is wanted to equip the
mine for a 200,000-ton production. Machinery cost
£20,000, and an offer was recently made to sell
the mine and plant for £3OOO, which looks as if
the Mines Department is likely to decide on a change
.of tenants. The published figures of loss amount to
the statement that the profits of crushing, cyamdmg
and royalties have been eaten by pumping costs.
Fraser’s is 22 years old, and very little has been
done on it for six years. The present syndicate
got the purchase money from old sands and slimes.
Tributers, directly and otherwise, have produced
£30,000 more, and that hasn’t gone back into the
mine. Fraser’s doesn’t want any £IOO,OOO work-
ing capital; but it does want a good 30-head null
and an ownership by somebody who will give the
mine a chance by not starving it.

South, at' Ora-Banda, have had their mine well
developed for them by three option parties, who
each did a lot of work and’ then threw the proposi-
tion down. First, Freedman and Johnston optioned
it at £BOOO, and then the Associated Northern had
it at £12,000, and, lastly, the Gwalia South sampled
it at and passed it out. All the Gimlet
shows are' too erratic in gold contents for close
sampling, but the mill says that 1600 tons of
Gimlet South ore bulked 335. 6d. Owners ask
£17,500 for the mine now, so that every optioner
has lifted the price.

Dorrie Doolette brings tbe Corinthian
North to the East for flotation. Fred Morgan esti-
mates ore in sight-above 100 ft. from the south boun-
dary to 700 ft. north at 158,625 tons, value £195,448.
At the bottom level sulphides worth 425. have been
proved over a length of 255 ft. A winze is down 30ft. m
sulphide bulking 80s., and boreholes in both walls
of the winze show ore worth 50s. The Corinthian
North lode averages 31ft. wide; pay ore at surface
has been stripped for 700 ft., and the sulphides show
no trace of arsenic.
The Blair Atliol Coal and Timber Cos. has
hatched another sixpenny dividend, making its sixth
to date. The output in January was 8473 tons,
which constitutes a record. February’s output was
hampered by many idle days due to the strike atro-
city. Over 100 men' are employed at Blair Athol.
“Kobbi”: Many Peaks (Queensland), the
servant mine of the big Mount Morgan, has just de-
clared a dividend of 7 id. a share. The young co.
was originally in 2041 shares at £l, on which last
year it was able to manage a dividend of 30s. Since
then, each share has been split up into 40, making
81,640 shares at 6d. So the present distribution of
7-Jd. is equal to 255. under the old order of things.
Many Peaks is a satisfactory concern to be a share-
holder of, for it simply fattens, while Mount Morgan
does the work and pays a royalty of 2s. 6d. per
ton. (Mount Morgan uses a little over nine tons ol
Peaks ore to every 22 of its own.) Mount Morgan s
end of the bargain is equally good. For the half-
crown it gets a ton of flux which enables it to
heavily cut down its coke bill, and it has 245. worth
of copper from it into the bargain. Out of 507
tons copper obtained from Mount Morgan smelters
in February 114 are credited to Many Peaks.
* * * *
At the Duchess mine (Hampden, Cion-
curry) the bottom of the winze from the 550 ft. level
is showing 2ft. of lower grade ore (9 p-c. copper).
Optimistic local opinion reckons that the deepest
level has 300 ft. on ore, but nothing is said about
average width or quality. A sampling of a few
inches of ore cannot surely be taken as representa-
tive of the big reserves. There is a lode 550 ft. deep
and 300 ft. in length to figure on, so every Ift. m
width gives 165,000 cub. ft. of ore, and it 10 cub.
ft = l ton, the average width of the ore throughout
the mine will have to be 10ft. to give 165,000 tons.
No doubt there is a difficulty in estimating reserves
when a bulge may measure many feet wide, running
out to a narrow streak, and when the richer ore shoots
are alternated with blanks. Of course there are the
mines at the Trekelano, Mascotte and Hampden to
back up the Duchess, but the deeper ground in all
of th p m is unproved, and that means something in
considering the value of a Cloncurry mine—or, in
fact, any mine—for most of them get poorei at
depth.
* * * *
At Chillagoe (Q.) the lead furnace shut
down because of a collapse of brickwork. Last
month this furnace ran 14 days, and smelted 1380
tons of ore for 221 tons of lead, 11,4640 z. silver
and 1990 z. of gold. Railway receipts for the first
11 months of the financial year to March 31 total
£B7 120. Because of the damage done by flood and
fire the net revenue for the current year will pro-
bably show a decline, and that is one reason why
Chillagoe is calling up the remaining Is. pej share.
Mungana (N. Queensland) will recon-
struct, and its half-million shares will m future
be liable to calls. It isn’t likely that more than Is.
per share ( £25,000) will be required, because with
ordinary luck a profitable output of ore should be
maintained. Mungana closes its financial year on
March 31, and at the end of last year (31/3/ 11)
there was a profit of £14,700. For the 12 months
1530 tons of copper, 1874 tons of lead and 300,000
oz. of silver were produced at the Chillagoe smelting
wofks from 22,489 tons of ore. No doubt these
shipments were of higher grade than the average of
reserves throughout the Girofla and Lady Jane mines,
still they are evidence of sound value. The Girona
has been worked down to the 610 ft. level, and the
Lady Jane to 420 ft. The flow of water m both
mines is heavy, and there are no mines in North
Queensland where pumping plays a more important
part. Some years since The Bulletin suggested
that concentration might effect some slight economy
in the treatment of the lower-grade lead ore, but the
complex nature of the ore, the cost of mining, and
the limited output prevented any successful imita-
tion of Broken Hill methods.
Great Fitzroy is to reconstruct if share-
holders agree to the scheme that will be laid before
them at an extraordinary meeting on March 28.
It is proposed to raise £67,500 new capital by the
formation of anew co. in 900,000 shares of ss.
each, nominally paid up to 2s. This means that
Is 6d. per share is to be called up. Two-thirds
of this (£45,000) will go to retire some of the
debenture debt, leaving £22,500 (less expense)
available for development; The first issue ot
450 000 new shares is to be underwritten by deben-
ture holders. An important feature in this scheme
is that the balance of the debenture debt is re-
deemable by setting aside half the cash froxn the
proceeds of reserve shares and half of any net profit
that may be made. For 12 months ending June
30 1911, Great Fitzroy produced 69,593 tons of
ore, but, although the extraction of copper, gold and
silver (contained in matte and blister copper) was
fairly good, the heavy working costs ( £1 18s. oa.
per ton of crude ore) put the concern m the rnua.
There has been a continuous mopping up ml ail
available funds by expenditure on new plant, pay-
ment of interest, and administration, and the re-
sult is, reconstruction.
* * * *
Brilliant Deeps at Charters Towers is the
most interesting show at present. The footwall
reef is capricious, as proved by the blank passed
through, but about 260 ft. west of the crosscut
the level entered a good make of mineral stone, so
each breaking down of the reef from that point has
been anxiously watched. For the first 200 ft. west
of the crosscut the reef gave on the average, two
tons of stone per foot, and the quality was good,
for £3084 was obtained from 430 tons of stone.
Still a long blank rather damped local enthusiasm
and the share market has fluctuated a good deal.
The latest news is that No. 4 level, on its westward
course, is nearly opposite the corner of the Brilliant
Blocks’ lease. The Blocks is crosscutting at
2000 ft., and has a long way to go to reach the
Deeps’ boundary, while the Brilliant Deeps No.
level is at a vertical depth of 2540 ft. The Deeps
co is in 150,000 shares, so at 12s. the market
valuation is £90,000. It certainly seems a large
amount except to the eye of faith that can see the
rich shoot of stone extending above and below No.
4 level. # *
At Mount Morgan one of the new turbo-
blowers is in its place, and when the three other
blowers are in the equipment—so far as the blowing
of enormous quantities of air is concerned—will be
thoroughly up to date. A heavy blast is required
to convert the basic ore that comes from Many Peaks
into oxide of iron, without which the silieious ore
from Mount Morgan itself canriot be slagged. Mount
Lvell uses tons of air for a similar purpose, and,
under its smelting practice, nearly two tqns ol
pyritic ore are smelted with one ton of silieious.
Mount Morgan, however,, uses nearly twice as much
silieious ore as pyritic, so the conditions are re-
versed, and before it can slag off the extra silica it
has to apply a strong blast of air to convert sulphide
of iron into oxide, and has also to add lunestone
pretty liberally to the charge. Although .lust at
present attention is concentrated on the smelting
practice, the high cost of underground mining is in
reality a much greater trouble. Fortunately Mount
Morgan ore is high grade; the average ot 81,4<0
tons smelted last half-year was 2.75 p.c. copper
and B.76dwt. gold, and while it can maintain that
quality, the prospects are sound, even if the liberal
mixture of basic ore lowers the average grade ot the
total ore smelted.
Old State mining engineer Jackson, re-
porting on the Malbon (Cloncurry railway), esti-
mates 100,000 tons of shipping ore m the Macgregor
mines, and a lot of lower-grade stuff. Macgregor
will rail its ores to Hampden furnaces. The ime
from Malbon to the Duchess is constructing, and the
projected Macgregor line will likely be extended
to Argylla. The Nor’-West Cloncurry line over the
Dugald and the Leichhardt will go past the Cuthbert
group to the Dobbin and Crusader, and will serve
Mount Oxide better than at present anyway, though
a big producer, such as Oxide is sure to be, must
go north to the Gulf waters. Cuthbert has shipped
4000 tons of high-grade ore by team to Cloncurry,
and has 16,000 tons waiting transport, with a great
tonnage ready for mining. Jackson quotes the
Warden’s statement that Mount Oxide has .proved
250,000 tons, and recommends the Cuthbert line as
part of the railroad that must go to the Gulf. Then
there will be a line from the Pacific Ocean to the
waters of the Arafura Sea.

The word “off” is given by the New Langi
Logan, Ararat (Vic.), starter now that things are
ready for the No. 2 bore to go up. No. 1 drill went
into country soon after the historical announcement
that green rock had been struck somewhere or an-
other in the main under level. If the field gets
going now no flogging will be necessary to carryt'*
two Cathcart mines forward concurrently or to help
the Langi Logan South out of the bog in which it is
stranded. The Bulletin believes that the Lan
Logan mines have a good chance, but.it warns all
who look for a big rise in scrip, supposing the No 2
bore reaches wash and a good prospect is got, that
a weary wait must follow before the gutter is risen
into, before the wash is drained, and before gravel-
ling is begun. In the meantime work at the Cath-
cart and the Central Cathcart promises well.
Good yields from the Cathcart would provide funds to
meet the Langi Logan’s calls. Manager Bryant, if
he wishes to hustle work in the big lead, will have
to hit the Cathcart tributary soon, or he will get a
howling lot of dervishes at him.
Minister Mcßride, at the christening of
the Central Blue battery, Bendigo, the other day,
puffed out a story of the fortune he had missed by
chucking up the sponge at that claim. Peter was
early in the field, and water beat him and his pals
when a few feet away from the reef. Tl ?us he missed
sharing in the £76,800 of dividends shelled out of
the treatment of 20,400 tons by the Central Blue
plant. This cuts up at £2 Bs. per share, so that
the crape on Peter’s arm can be understood. Cen-
tral Blues have topped the £ 5 mark since Peter
spoke, by reason of the hope that a bigger battery
will bring profits from the stack of low-grade dirt
awaiting treatment. #

bably be interesting.

The John Taylor-run Anglo-Australian
Exploration Cos. had a rocky passage to report to its
shareholders at the last annual meeting in London.
The nastiest bump encountered was the sudden fall
ing off in the value of the ore stoped from the Mount
Boppy Gold Mine (N.S.W.), which resuUed in re-
duced dividends from that source. So much was
this so that, after paying its other expenscs the
Exploration Cos. hadn’t enough ieft wer to supply
a dividend of its own. Its present Australian shows
so far have proved a one-horse team, and now th
willing horse seems inclined to jib. However, thing,
aren't desperate, for it will probably get going again,
and some of the other animals may brighten up altei
a little coaxing. In the nine years up to last April
the Mount Boppy has paid in dividends £395,383 on
a capital of £121,000, equal to close on 330 p.t.
of the capital, or a yearly average of 36 p.c. me
two neighboring shows, the South and Korth, have
been turned out to grass elsewhere, and M tar the
South does not seem to have found much to fatten
on at the South Lucknow. The North, however, has
had better luck, and something may yet result from
the Blue Spec at Hargraves.
Another of the co.’s holdings is in the Malta Gold
Dredging Cos (N.S.W.), which promised well for a
start, but soon found a barren patch directly it got
off the main channel of the river. The Tasmania
Gold Mine brings up the rear, and (as mentioned m
a recent issue) is still wrestling with the problem
of how to pump 100 or 150 tons of water for every
ton of ore raised, and make the job pay. According
to recent news from the mine, the opening of the
bottom level (1500 ft.) has started well, 50ft of
driving east having exposed an average lodge width
6ft., assaying loz. 6dwt. gold per ton. At the
time of the London meeting the Exploration Cos. was
contemplating anew promotion, the proposition being
an alluvial property of 100 acres, known as the
Mountain Run Option (N.S.W.).

* * * *
The Bigelow Patent Rock Breaker commends itself
to mine managers who welcome fin effective machine
which is cheaper to maintain and repair than Gyra-
tory and other crushers. This machine is of the
well-known Blake type, but has some additional im-
provements which have justified in practice all the
claims advanced by the patentee. The work of the
crushing i& done' on the down stroke of the pitman
instead of the up stroke; there is no strain on the
pitman cap; two large side rods resist the thrust of
the toggles, and enable the jaws to be regulated
within a large range; the back toggle is so made
as to resist all legitimate strains of crushing, but will
shear under exceptional conditions and save the
machine. The Austral Otis Engineering Company,
of South Melbourne, have installed a great number
of these machines, and hold the sole manufacturing
rights for the Commonwealth.* [1

Extract from the Finance Chronicle, London
(1/9/1910). —“The Colonial Mutual Life Assurance
Society: Its moderate premium rates and its prompt-
ness and liberality in the settlement of its claims
have gained for it a favorable and firm footing
throughout the Kingdom. The annual accounts
of the Society have all along shown a steady
onward tendency, and of more recent years a marked
activity, by the extensions of the benefits granted to
its assurers, the introduction of Accident assurance,
and a carefully-adapted scheme, combining Accident
and Invalidity assurance with Life Assurance under
one policy.”*

which are constructed without any valves, cams,

or kerosene oils, are represented throughout Aus-
tralia and New Zealand by Babcock and Wilcox,

the “Bolinder” engine has many advantages, viz.,
small oil consumption, ease of starting, no compli-
cated arrangement of valves or gears, being direct
reversible, fitted throughout with forced lubrication,
and absolutely no risk from fire and explosions, due
to using oils having a very high flash point. The
British Government and German -Navy are large
users of the “Bolinder” Marine Engines.*

Most fellows find that nagging
little worries get them down
more than the big ones do. —
When things won’t go right with
me I just stop whatever I’m at
and go on with something else,
then come back to the old job
fresh again; and when I feel
really tired out I get them to
make me a cup of good Tea and
drink it hot—as hot as I can drink
it. “ Robur ”is the Tea to use—
it is refreshing—very refreshing.
The Clerk.

15th MARCH, 1912.
We have received a small card from M. CHARLES
HEIDSIECK, of Champagne fame, posted from
Reims, setting out that he and his brother, M.
Henri, are actually the only Champagne Merchants
in France who bear and have the right to bear the
patronymic name of HEIDSIECK, M. Charles
Heidsieck also informs us that he only ships a
vintage wine, and the quality sent to Australia is
the same as that sold in London. His Wine has
been honored with the Royal Warrant of the present
King, as it also was by the late King Edward VII.
The 1904 vintage is in perfect condition, and the
Australasian Agents, Messrs. Swift & Company, of
32 O’Connell Street, Sydney, have received good
supplies for the coming season. We have no doubt
that sparkling “CHARLES” will be present at many
of the leading functions.*

Governor Chelmsford and Lady C. have

Customs Collector Spence, of Invercargill
(M.L.), where the wind comes from, is at
present collecting calm weather impressions
in Sydney and Melbourne. Mrs. Spence is
with the traveller. It’s about five years
since I met the shriek of an autumn breeze
in Invercargill, and even now the memory
thereof nearly blows my hat across the
harbor.
Concerning the township of Tam-o’-Shan-
ters near the storm-beaten Bluff, Inver-
cargill used to print a postcard with a
bored-looking cab-horse dozing beside a
lamp-post. It was called “the most south-
erly cab-rank and lamp-post in the world ”
Ihe most southerly lamp-post has long since
been blown out, for the Chilian and the
Argentine have been carrying the thing the
intoxicated person clings to into latitudes
where Invercargill’s eagles never flew. As
t h e cab-rank, I don’t know, but it seems
that the hackney dog-sledge and the frozen
hansom will be at the Pole very soon. And
then the name of Invercargill will be
dragged in the mud as a thing of no ac-
count.

goers Dramatic Club, with the clever Elia
Crone in the name-part, produced the piece
on August 24, 1910. The Bulletin picked
it out as a winner, though it has mostly
languished in a brown paper parcel ever
“Ron Harrie * s Soing to drag
Bad Mrs. Bennett from her dusty hiding
place, and the lady is off to N’York and a
gay lire forthwith.
M Hugh Ward, Mrs. R. H. Todd and
7 ’ t Hoodman P us h e d a welcome home
to Mrs. J. C. Williamson into the Australia
drawing-room the other day. A large
gathering of matrons and maids, a few shrieks
in hats, and a slight masculine flavor of
frock coat collected round tea tables and got
through a mixed programme of songs on ice,
etc., with much tea to wash down the re-
marks, and some melody to sit on the head
or tu e tea, in case it should prove rebellious,
feid Macdonald’s melodious warble cut
through the chatter for 10 minutes. Then
the chatter rose up again with renewed fury
and fell upon the food supply and the repu-
tations of its dearest friends, and when it
had finished its attack on the latter its
dearest friend’s reputation was a fleshless
skeleton fleeing into the wilderness.
Black and white threatens to storm this
city at the autumn dress parade. Mrs.
J. C. Williamson, at her welcome home party
last week wore an ivory-tinted gown—ail
wool and more than a yard wide —with a hint
of Vienna in its drape of black lace. Some of
the best-frocked women present retired into
black charmeuse, with a foam of white lace
on their headgear. One youthful dame,
with no sharp gables in her curved outline,
added a great blob of violets to her raven
garb. Perfectly turned out in dove-grey,
Mrs. Willoughby put ordinary flesh to the
blush, for she glode across the room like an
emancipated French fashion plate, mounted
on mere shoe-leather for the occasion. Mrs.
Maxwell Allan was another graceful silhou-
ette in grey, with a fluff of chiffon aeroplan-
mg on the roof of her hat. Frank Greene,
superlatively groomed to an eyelash, saun-
tered in. Hilda Spong wore a negative
expression, and a stamped robe of some
diaphanous silk tied up with stamped black
velvet, rather tight about the knees. J.C.W.
himself, swathed in silence, eyed the conver-
sation for five minutes over the rim of a
tea-cup.
Miss Lottie Gilbert, like a juvenile steam-
tug, navigated Bessie Major—just back from
London—across the Mrs. j.C.W. party.
Bessie looks well, and keeps her fighting
weight in its accustomed place. She records
18 months’ work with the Asche-Brayton co.
with a pleased smile.
The Criterion Theatre has been raking in
coin with a steady “chink’ during the Portus
and Talbot management. The Jansen raid
on this city is the cause. The Woolly Sam
McCaughey-—a flourishing child of 76 sum-
mers or thereabouts —and other sprightly
youngsters (Mr. Gregg and Dr. Watt, I
fancy) had a box one evening, and dallied
with the Wizard behind the scenes, while the
juggler juggled on the stage and wiped a
heat wave off his forehead with a cannon-
ball.
The Stage Society and Society are to
have a gala evening between them on the
28th inst., when “Prunella or Love in a
Dutch Garden” is set down for presentation.
This fascinating Pierrot play, by Laurence
Housman and Granville Barker, will be pro-
duced by Max Montesole at the St. James’s
Ilall. He was producer to the English
Drama Society, and was for some time with
Tree, He is to play Pierrot himself, and
the part of Prunella has been given to Miss
Molly McArthur, daughter of Mr. W.
McArthur, ex-M.P. —he who was Whip for
the British Liberal party for so many years.
Other well-known people in the cast are
the Misses Merivale, Innes-Noad and Bestic,
and Messrs. M. C. Stephen, A. C. Stephen,
Winchcombe and McKeown.
South Australian pianist Annette Scam-
mell unpacked her technique in this town a
few weeks ago, after four years in foreign
parts. Now she plunges (though not in
bathing costume) into the German habit of
inviting music tasters to sample classic
chords at monthly afternoon rallies in
Paling’s concert room. A knowing-looking
booklet, with its slim cover tied up in brown
ribbon, came my way. It is loaded with the
menu for four piano assaults. The first is
dated March 30.
Baritone Wilfred Manning, who is one of
Auckland’s important chemists in commer-
cial moments, and a leading constellation in
amateur opera in his spare time, is now trip-
ping over the uneven pavements of Sydney.
The Palace Theatre hummed with excite-
ment on Saturday night, when the right way
to be mdrooned, without losing a hair-pad,
burst on the better feelings of Everywoman.
Frances Ross,’ the charming Sylvia Vicars,
who forgets all the things she ought to re-
member in defence of good Mr. Dawes, wore
a handsome mane of tawny hair streaming
down her honored back, and kept her fringe
in curl even while cannibal Gabbett smote
the air with an axe. Roland Challis, as
Mamma Vicars, didn’t wear her mane down
her back, but she stuck to her hair-pads, and
was (presumably) hacked to pieces behind a
shelter-shed in an elaborate coiffure.
“For the Term of His Natural Life’’ makes
a play that fits on to the masked burglar
melodrama now running a successful season
in suburban villas. The shuddering woman
who hides her new teeth in the flower
pot will easily picture the baleful eye of
a masked cannibal glaring down the chimney,
while a voice like a gramophone with all
its fangs protruding demands her best even-
ing dress or her life. We are still waiting
for the law that will make it a hanging
matter for women of the Pretty Creature
type to grow an hour older than Frances
Ross looked—by the grace of Providence-
on Saturday night. Vera Remee, a mere
youngster, has been practically trained
by Walter Baker during the last few
months. As Sarah Rex she wormed
her way across the third act with
a Cleopatra smile and the stagecraft of
a Mabel Trevor. The girl’s mandarin silk
draperies, in this scene, showed a sernent’s
trail of glittering jet, and her brow was
steeped in an Old Nile pattern of Worst
Woman bandeau. The heathen raged, but
not furiously, in a first-night audience at
the Palace. Young China, with ropes of
raven hair flowing over a gorgeous theatre
wrap, wedged in between pale brown and
white friends, is a frequent patch of color
in the best seats of our city theatres.
Lucas Malet’s last novel, “Adrian Savage”
(Dymock’s), wants chopping down to about
half its present length. Its brilliant liter-
ary style is partly submerged in its elon-
gated conversations, which begin in Paris
and meander through Bull’s Island after a
rough crossing from Calais to Dover. With
Lucas Malet the parental Kingsley manner
survives in a masterly use of the English
language, though it falls down exhausted
in the vivisection of emotions that struggle
for breath under tons of verbiage. French
Gabrielle, with her beauty and charming
clothes, and English Joanna, the plain and
wealthy spinster who puts her duty to God
and her diary above such trivial sins as ex-
pensive mourning, are delightful character
sketches. Joanna sheds candle grease and
tears over her diary at a time when the
average woman is looking for a burglar
under her bed.
A French child in the Lucas Malet man-
ner is a mixture of a Persian kitten, an in-
fant and a philosopher. “Pray, do not be
severe with M. Dax any more, mamma,”
says the tiny Bette. “You have, perhaps,
dear mamma, been so on my account, there-
fore it is for me to plead with you.” Im-
agine spanking an infant Chesterfield like
Bette! It would seem like caning a uni-
versity.
“Fur?” said the little shopgirl reflec-
tively as she lovingly smoothed a huge muff.
“Yes, fur’s going to be worn a terrible lot
this winter!” And just then the wild cry
of a passing rabbit-o echoed in the shop.
What unfortunate furred thing grows the
2s. lid. neck-wrap which leaves its dyed
hairs on the lapel of the best youth’s coat?
While on the subject, the story of the girl
who owned a real sealskin coat occurs to
me. It was such a desirable garment that
she allowed particular friends to hire it
from her for special occasions. It was a
profitable arrangement which brought in
pocket money and kept the moth at bay, till
a careless maiden left it hanging on her bed
at a fashionable hotel. It promptly van-
ished, and now the anguished owner seeks
vainly for her property in second-hand shops
and on the backs of passers-by.
The Otway slid out of this port last week,
with the crash of snapped ribbon streamers,
and the loud concussion of breaking hearts.
The outward-bound Australian cricket team]
faring forth in search of the cinders, was
tlm cause. When a cheerful young flapper
isn t eating chocolate drops at the shrine of
a matinee idol—Higginson has filled this
arduous billet for quite a while—she is put-
ting flowers on the altar of Hordern, or
drinking in the sweet music of Trumper’s
batting. So the cinder-searchers were
molested for souvenirs by adoring damsels
till the Otway’s gangway tore the air in
twain.
In Portland (Maine) there’s a club with
a long-haired musical name—the Kotzchmar.
It is called after a triple alliance in sound,
a man who once toiled there as pianist, or-
ganist and composer. The Kotzchmar Club
meets every month to bring forth a learned
essay on music. The world may, or may
not, strain its ears to catch the results. As
likely as not a good part of the world allows
the chariot of Catsneeze (which is easier to
say than the other name) to thunder past
unheeded. In May this assembly of Blot-
smash, or whatever the word may be, will
distribute its laurel leaves on the world’s
greatest living soprano. John Oakes Burke,
of Portland, dropped an informative line to
Melba the other day, in which he proudly
remarked that she was the subject of the
May essay, and lie was the. writer. And
after that there Avas dead silence in Heaven
for the space of one hour.
Have you ever noticed how a young man’s
fancy runs to drab, as a color? Even the
favorite mother-in-law joke is generally
dressed in a drab bonnet, and the man of
middle-aged thoughts often guesses this
muddy tint if he’s shoved into a tight corner
by a female query. I am reminded of this
by a blue gown worn by pretty Mrs.
Knowles (Lily Titheradge) the other day.
She whisked through a crowded drawing-
room while a male friend hurriedly indexed
her arrival. “Where is she?” “Over there
in a drab dress,” says the Invaluable He.
It was one of those soft yet vivid blue
gowns which have the shade of a sapphire
where the thin silky stuff floats into folds.
It was so blue that it was desecration to
call it by any fancy name. To a man it
was just a drab frock with a pretty face on
top of it. Women do dress for other women,
after all.
Yours in the city of uproarious trams,
VANDORIAN.
