MELBOURNE CHATTER,
Melbourne, March 18, 1912.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The late Zeal was a close friend of David
Syme, despite the fact that they drank dif-
ferent brands of politics. The friendship
dated from their young days, when they
were both interested in railways, Zeal as
engineer for Cornish and Bruce (the great
railway contractors of the early ’6o’s) and
Syme as a sub-contractor for sleepers and
other softgoods. The large and hefty Syme’s
way of flinging the heavy slabs of timber
about to show languid workers how it
should be done took the little engineer’s
fancy, and they chummed up. Zeal always
held to his engineering interests. He had to
do later with the Echuca-Deniliquin line,
and from the time he entered politics was
consultant engineer for the Government.
Though a member of the House of Sleepers,
he was progressive—as Conservatives go —
and was continually shaking up his brother
logs. Because he could keep awake longer
than any of them they put him in the chair,
and thenceforth he presided over the dreams
of the Stout Landlords.
Although little William Zeal, now de-
parted, lived and died a bachelor, there was
a romance in his life which gave the only
known sentimental touch to his career.
Some years ago, he offered his title and
mature self to a young Geelong girl, and
promised her a round-the-world honeymoon;
but the lady spurned him for a penniless
local youth who died after a brief period.
Zeal did not attempt to lay siege to the
widow. Seemingly, he was content to have
missed his last elderly ’bus.
Auntie Phipps, of the Fuller household,
wonders plaintively why General .Report is
so anxious to return her to England. The
lady declares she loves Australia, is per-
fectly contented with her lot (of small fair
nieces), and has every intention of staying
until the family packs up.
Miss Muriel Farr, of Adelaide, who has
temporarily parted with her health in the
unnecessary cause of “Liberalism,” returned
the other day from a Gippsland cycling tour
to be feted and fed by the feminine leagues
that live at variance in this city. Miss Farr,
unlike the stiff-collared political female,
scores most through an appealsome woman-
liness.
Mrs. S. P. Maclcay (who used to be Fan
Dango) gave a little dinner during the
week to a number of Westralians. Camelot
was decorated with scarlet blossoms, and
the pretty hostess wore a tunic of pearls
over white satin and numerous diamonds.
The Mackay baby, who is popularly known
as “Peter,” has flaxen hair, lovely blue eyes,
and a complexion of milk and roses.
Two notable knots will have been tied
this week before The Bulletin breaks
out. Wednesday, the 20th, was fixed for
the. McGowan-Walker literary and artistic
splice; and on the same day, Miss Gray, of
the.Riverina, was to take the name of Park-
Smith from a landed proprietor of the same
locality. The event was to transpire at
Scots’ Church, Collins-street, with refresh-
ments and speeches to follow at three o’clock,
at the Oriental. Special interest attaches
to. this bride as a niece of artist Millais’s
widow. The aunt twice married greatness,
for she was John Ruskin’s wife until she
got her historical divorce —in camera. Miss
Gray’s sacrificial robe was to be the usual
Empire, done in satin and adorned with real
lace, a gift of far-off relatives.
One of Melbourne’s pretty girls, Zara
Hope Morris, goes shortly to Sydney to
bridesmaid Marjorie' Lord, her handsome
cousin, who is to marry Henry Osborne, - of
the extensive Osborne clan. The bride-elect
is gathering in some gorgeous presents,
amongst them a dressing-case containing con-
siderably over a hundred guineas’ worth,
and many other glittering burglar baits.
Someone will have to present the bridegroom
with an automatic pistol, a baton and a
bulldog if the treasures are to be kept in-
tact. Or possibly he might swear himself
in as a special constable.
Mrs. G. F. Morris (widow of the late
owner of Fairfield Vineyard and the cellars
thereof) is off to London by the Orvieto,
and will probably help to further congest
Europe for a while.
The suburbanites who do not keep their
fingers on the pulse of the social world, and
who happened to be in the vicinity of the
Flinders-street station, on a recent Tuesday
after lunch —these dull human vegetables
thought they had struck a fight or a fire
when they found a surging, excited crowd
being shoved about by policemen. At last,
after much craning, they discovered that the
attention of the mob was focussed upon a
striped awning that leaned against the som-
bre sides of St. Paul’s Cathedral. Then
some remained as ignorant as before, and
some remembered with a guilty start that it
was the day of the “Jack” Baillieu-Beatrice
Madden wedding. The original intention was
to have a quiet piece of joinery, but the
families on both sides are extensive, and
even the “intimate friends” meant a large
mob. On top of that came a Governor and
Melba —circumstances warranted to raise a
shout even at a funeral. The plump and
pretty bride arrived with Pa in the family
coach, but left in a spruce motor for the
Baillieu humpy on the Macedon slopes.
Lesly Madden, the sole remaining spinster
of the Madden daughters, took her long
familiar part as bridesmaid. She and Athol
Levey, blue in gowning (like Mamma Mad-
den), sported cerulean chiffon and satin, with
blue roses in their hair. A miniature Bail-
lieu nephew helped a tot of a niece to carry
a barrow-load of train. Mrs. Pat Osborne,
more dimpled than ever, wore yellow silk
muslin and lace. Melba, underneath a Gains-
brough hat, flung back a majestic black
velvet coat to show a black and blue gown.
Beatrice Madden’s "nursey” sent all the
way to Ireland for the lace to hang on her
ex-charge’s back at the bridal. The veil has
been falsely charged with being a Dago pro-
duction. “Venetian point” some called it;
but the Italian fabric is a heavy needle-made
lace worth so many guineas the square
inch. If “nursey” could have nanned out
Venetian point three yards or so square
she could take up residence at Menzies’.
“Jack” Baillieu, the bridegroom of the
month, paraded his full name when signing
the register—Maurice Howard Lawrence
Baillieu. He has half a dozen brothers all
bearing similar burdens. All the same, they
answer gladly to “Joe” and “Dick” and
“Jack.” s
The Robert Kerrs, of Sydney, parents of
pita, who is shortly to marry Edgar Cohen,
journeyed last week to Melbourne, and un-
packed their traps at the Grand.
The Mayoress of Bendigo pervaded Mel-
bourne for a few days recently, but had lo
rush home to her own city. Arduous duties
lay ahead of her, it was said. Amongst
them were a presentation of prizes to sun-
dry King’s Scouts, a Kirby concert, and the
paper flower meetings for the Bendigo Fair,
which happens in the undermined city at
Easter. What are such functions without
a mayoress? What, indeed! Little better
than home without a mother.
Last week, Miss Turnbull accompanied a
son of Ballarat medico Woinarski to the
altar, and caused a crowd of people to at-
tend a wedding feast at the St. Ivilda house
of her aunt, Mrs. Grattan. The handsome
Grattan cousins descended from their sheep-
ish acres near Dookie and became fashion-
plate bridesmaids in peach and silver, pink
roses and mob caps. The ceremony was at
eight o’clock or thereabouts ; so the women
guests bared their shoulders and covered
their heads with picture hats. Aunt Grat-
tan, garbed magnificently in blue of the
glittering, elusive fish-scale shade, wore half
a blue ostrich on her head. The other half
of the bird is supposed to be still running
about in Africa. The bride was a dainty
little bit. of white almost hidden in girly
surroundings. Much champagne was poured
during the supper, which was spread in
marquees. While the departing two were
being pelted with rose leaves, a lynx-eyed
waiter picked up a roll of bank notes from
the floor, and coursed round the guests like
a greyhound in search of the owner. It
was then discovered that the bridegroom
was the dropper. This was a-had begin-
ning of married life.
Louise Smith, who married linoleum mil-
lionaire Dyer, sends a few friendly lines
and a picture of the Sphinx from the Nile,
where she is honeymooning and studying
hieroglyphics.
The Purchas man, who wedded Dorothy
de Jersey Grut and carried her off to Wes-
tralia, owns a voice which is related to the
priceless Melba warble. The Purchas
grandmother was a Dow, and was closely
related to Melba’s mother, who had been a
Dow before she was a Mitchell. The voice
trilled through that side of the family.
Mrs. David Mitchell’s singing used to de-
light the visitors to her country home.
Melba figured largely at the Purchas-de
Grut wedding, which, in consequence,
scored heavily over contemporary functions.
Dorothy Gotton allied herself, last Thurs-
day, to Frank Doyle. The bride belongs to
the extensive Janet Clarke clan, and sister
Nina (who married cousin Frank Clarke)
was engineer-in-chief of the holy function.
After the event, Frank Clarke made the re-
ception informal and happy by waiting out-
side the house gate for the wedding party.
The Frank Clarkes have the house that
Vera Clarke-Landale rented until her mar-
riage. By the way, the Smiths are threat-
ening to build another mansion in St.
George’s-road, Toorak, now that the Clarkes
have taken such a violent fancy to their
original home.
Gus Dudgeon, a stepson of Agar Wynne,
is next week marrying Miss Innis, sister of
Melbourne Herald’s City Editor. After
the ceremony, the pair depart for a trip to
England. The sea-trip has been ordered Jby
the medicine man of the prospective bride-
groom, and his best girl talks gaily of nurs-
ing him through the early stages of the
honeymoon.
Alberto Zelman, bright son of a gifted
musical father, is to be married, on March
00, to Miss Jenkins, who at one time wore
the name of Maud Harrington on the J. C.
Williamson boards. She is the daughter of
the Jenkins who made a big pile in the fish
trade, and lost the bulk of it through taking
the advice of land-boomsters.
The Barttelot aide has been told off to
accompany the Parliamentarians next
month to the Northern Territory, and make
ready the way for Denman.
A gilded bachelor who married the divorced
wife of his best friend is still waiting for
the honeymoon tour to begin. His bride is
busy arguing with the Law for the posses-
sion of the child of husband No. 1; but the
Law is long winded and quite unsentimental,
and the fight can’t be settled in one round.
Dalvin House, Noorat, which was built
by the late Neil Black, lias been purchased
by Mrs. Claude Bostock Palmer, of Terang,
the Marjorie Staughton of other days.
The Educational Congress has been filling
Melbourne with serious faces of the Spank-
you-for-your-own-good type. The owners have
been judiciously mixing play—serious, im-
proving play with valuable talk. On
Thursday, they dined at the Grand, and re-
ceived some advice from Fisher. President
Barrett also entertained all hands and a drove
of local society at a Heimath gardeL party.
The Denman lady trailed about th?s lawns
for a quarter of an hour in a lovely lilac
silk frock, with Miss Quirk and secretary
Vernon in her train. Mrs. Barrett, in white
lace, helped her doctor husband to pump-
handle the numerous guests. The Pinschofs,
in fine array, headed a large section of the
ultra-musical set; and Hal Elvins hurried
about under his long-haired hat. Medical
folk were much in evidence. Dr. Mary
Booth, in soft grey garb, spared one of her
valuable hours to come and idle sociably!
The pretty Lincoln girl who married Dr.
Farnsworth, of Sydney, was with her sister
and medico brother-in-law. Mrs. Dave.v
sported smart black and white trappings,
and the learned dons of the University ac-
companied their ladies and beamed on all.
A counter attraction to the tea and chatter
was provided by Heimath’s acre or so of
natural bush, which is tenanted by paddy-
melons, wallabies, kangaroos and native
bears. The quiet-loving guests pursued
these natives with parasols and cheap con-
versation.
A joyous meeting with Melba in its midst
found a corner of the Town Hall free of
carpenters, and straightway began to con-
jure up visions of the Melba Hall, which
will shortly sprout for the University Con-
servatorium. The songstress, with camel-
lias in her hat, and a face dimpled with
smiles, handed over her matinee cheque for
£lOOO odd. (The Government’s £lOOO was
in the hands of the silver-haired Prof.
Peterson, whom, so Rumor says, embraced
his small wife in the Treasury elevator'
after successfully raiding Premier Weary’s
office.) The Mayor was on hand with his
Mayoress, and John Madden and his dame
sat close by. Sugden, of Queen’s, spoke,
and Laver, of the extended hair, smiled.
Mrs. Peterson fairly bubbled with delight
under the elbow of her Professor; and Mrs.
Premier Murray was a cool grey presence.
Mrs. Orme Masson, who has worked like a
dairy-farmer in the cause, stood quietly at
ease. Later, the Mayor took the important
people away for a glass of wine. Melba
made as if to pour her share into the crater
of an inverted silk hat, then slid the glass
behind the glittering cylinder. Still more
money is needed, but the advice of the
soprano is, “Get the building started and
mortgage.” The health of the bricks was
drunk, and the thanks of all reverently laid
at Melba’s feet. “I am as happy as can be
to help,” said she, and slipped away to her
motor.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
The Methodist Confereneers urged warmly and
for about the 4-millionth time that the hotels
should close at six, and the wrath of Mr. Bung
is terrific. As one to whom the bottle holds
but scant allurement, 1 may say that this anti-
alcohol business appears already a shade over-done.
A recent project to start a new club in our midst
died swiftly and emphatically. It was informed that
it could not be allowed a license, and what is a
man’s club without a license? At the Ladies’
Club the hostess who would give her guests
stronger drink than barley water must take a bottle
in her pocket.
Saturday’s Melbourne express brought us Mr. and
Mrs. Cuthbert Burgoyne, one time of this State. He
is part-owner of P. B. Burgoyne and Co.
The authorities, spurred on by the frantic pro-
tests of motorists and such-like, are thinking about
doing something to our awful roads. One sugges-
tion embodies a tax on all wheels- —bicycles in-
cluded.
We have our mixed-bathing “problem,” like the
less-sainted cities of the Commonwealth, and the
dailies are flooded with letters expressing horror
of the “don’t-know-what-we-are-coming-to” brand.
The snag which the holy ones are up against is the
fact that the city fathers of Glenelg and kindred
suburbs have grown to appreciate the financial bene-
fits of surf-bathing, and are now as unwilling to
abolish the paying pastime as a Yid is to fling sove-
reigns into the sea. Prom which it may be safely
deduced that herein the cause of Stiggins is about
to take the knock.
The March Show terminated in a cheerful storm,
which spoiled some of the exhibits and deposited the
roof of the main grandstand some 150 ft. away from
its rightful locality. It was a good, healthy storm,
and howled more furiously than twins. The attend-
ance and gate money were both below last year’s.
A big advance was apparent in the ladies’ hacks,
both as to horses and riders. Mrs. O’Leary was one
of the few who clung to the old side-saddle. Other
competitors were, more or less elegantly, astride.
Most of those who didn’t patronise the Show
on Saturday went to Port Adelaide to watch the
Junior Eights and Senior Pours disport themselves.
But the weather was awful, and the function had to
be postponed till Monday.
A new croquet club was opened at Walkerville
on Saturday, with the assistance of Mrs. F. W.
. Young and Mrs. (“Bishop”) Wilson. The secretary
is Miss Dora Muecke.
Miss Lea, of Robe, has returned from her trip to
India, and is now staying at the South Australian
Hotel with her mother. Dr. and Mrs. John Corbin
and the Charlie Jessops are also back from a trip
to Melbourne.
The Lyric Club seems to have successfully weath-
ered the financial storm which recently threatened
its existence. New premises have been secured in
Pirie-street, a good winter programme has been
arranged, and the committee is happy.
At Mount Lofty, a few days ago, Miss Britten
Barden was married to Mr. Brookman. The cere-
mony was of the non-uproarious order—ln fact,
there was not so much as one bridesmaid. The
bride, who is in her early twenties, struck an origi-
nal note by appearing in a wedding garment of
mauve. Mr. and Mrs. Brookman are to honeymoon
in Japan.
There died last week at Kadina, William Beare,
LL.B.—member of a S, Australian family which did
good work for this State in the early days. His
brother, Professor Beare, of the Edinburgh Uni-
versity, was the past graduate of Adelaide Uni-
versity.
The Humphrey Martens leave for England this
week, taking with them a son to be sent to one
of the Universities, and a small daughter to be edu-
cationally “finished.” Mrs. Poulten—also a medi-
co’s wife—gave a tea to bid them “good-bye” last
week.
From “Rosna,” temporarily of London: —
Artist George Coates is meditating a descent upon
Australia with some of his very best daubs. If all
goes well he will be with you about October.
One-time fiddler Maud McCarthy, who is now Mrs.
Mann (having married a Canadian journalist of that
ilk), has been lecturing to London on Indian music.
The girl has lived in India some time, and her lec-
tures are punctuated by weird and wondrous Indian
noises, sung to weird and wonderful Indian
instruments. The cream and intellect of Oxford are
to hear the lectures shortly and be uplifted thereby.
A performiance of Arthur Adams’s “Pierrot in
Australia” vras given to a politely interested audi-
ence at the Little Theatre the other afternoon. The
part of Liz was played by Dorothy Spencer, a
daughter of Maurice Brodzky.
Pretty Alice Crawford is to play Emilia in Tree’s
production of “Othello,” which will happen at an
early date.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

So fat, there is no assurance that the Bill
for increasing the salaries of N.S.W. poli-
ticians will include a proviso that the new
salaries won’t be largely expended in bribery
and corruption—as Attorney-General Idol-
man says the old salaries to a great extent
were. A good half of his case for an in-
crease was based, not on the fact that mem-
bers’ services were worth more, but on the
statement that they had to spend so much
in bribing truculent hat-passers and collec-
tors of one kind and another —in mollifying
sports clubs and charities with hard cash—
m order to get a fair chance of re-election,
that they must have more in order to live.
But unless members are absolutely prohi-
bited from giving donations to any institu-
tions within their electorates, and it is made
a crime to appeal to them for donations*
the State will be merely supplying more
money for the blackmailers who hang
around local politicians. In other words, it
will be merely providing more bribery and
corruption funds. And it doesn’t want to
scatter its good money in that fashion.

It is mentioned casually that 1872 mixed
trains run annually on the Victorian snail-
ways between Melbourne and Ballarat
alone —almost the busiest section of the
system. As a matter of fact, most Vic-
torian country _ trains are mixed, and those
that aren’t mixed are muddled. It is a
thriftless system, very much akin to making
a tramcar a passenger vehicle in front and
a parcel delivery van at the back. England
abolished mixed trains by law away back
m the 80 s of last century, and has never
regretted their loss, but Victoria is petrify-
mg these days, and thinks of going back to
the bullock dray.

be , Gld Soldier: The Defence Depart-
ment bought about 1500 horses for artillery,
i. tr ? n ? port ail( i other services recently
and had them broken in by contract. However,
when the next general inspection occurred,
a pair of bolting horses scattered two drill-
ing regiments. As a result of the frac-
tiousness of the mounts and those harnessed
to tne waggons several citizen soldiers were
incapacitated and taken to the hospital. The
upshot was that five men were laid out, and
one of them, a warrant-officer, was so badly
S- h ! at + he be sent from the barrack
hospital to St. Vincent’s. There is no
suggestion that .the horses weren’t properly
broken in. The trouble seems to have been
due to the fact that there are not enough
men available to keep the animals exercised
and a newly-broken horse which has been
eating its head off in a stable for two -r
three days is a notoriously difficult propo-
sitmn to face. An alternative is to get
rid ot the horses and use motor transport
exclusively. The motor would cost more to
begin with,. but it would not want feeding
and grooming when it was not working.
-Lhe foreigner is almost everywhere taking
to motor transport, and is even mounting
his soldiers on the. steed which has neither
nerves nor a temper, and never gets tired.
-Lne Italians, after their experience in Tri-
poli, have mounted their rifle regiments on
bikes, and are looking for a light two-ton
motor-lorry to carry supplies for them. The
i rench are turning a division (16,000) of
their army into cyclists, and collecting 400
tight motor-waggons for their transport; the
whole to be kept up as a frontier force in
peace. Jhe example seems worth following.

THE DEAD JOSS!
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
London, Friday evening.—-Mr. A. S. Hewins (Tar-
iffite) has been returned unopposed for Hereford.
—Saddening cable.
Oh, for years we have made
The great cause of “Freetrade”
An Idol, a Joss and a Wonder!
For years we have vowed
That the Briton was proud
Of the system that “saved him from plunder’’!
We knew all was right
With old Gladstone and Bright
And their gospel—Long Hours and Low Wages!
Though tlie Tories might chafe,
We knew Britain was safe,
And we said so—in pages on pages!
With the Liberals “in ’’
Men would hold it a sin,
Which was instantly sure of rejection,
For any vile seat
To declare, or repeat,
Its belief in Accurssd Protection !
As we knew we were right,
We were eager to fight
When the least smell of danger was scented ;
But now we’re di&mayed,
I 1 or the great god, “Freetrade,”
In the last fight was NOT REPRESENTED !
N.S.W.
CLARENCE O’HAGAN.
ASHES
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Warner's team is on its way back to England
wath the “ashes.” —Undeniable fact.
Will Bull show joy when home the victors come—
And have brass bands upon the pierhead playing?
He may be through the coal-strike stricken dumb,
And much too glum for music or hooray-ing!
In fancy I can hear the poor old soul
Make this remark (aside), the while there clashes
The bitter labor-war.- “I starve for coal
God! What a time to bother me with ashes!”
s\s.w.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
English captain Warner struggled hard
to maintain an appearance of absolute neutrality
thoughout the cricket war; but he blew right up
at the finish. He had sent a cable, or written a
letter, or flashed a wireless, or something asking the
Marylebone Club to intimate that it desired the best
team to come from Australia. The noble lord, who
is head serang of the Marylebone Club, accordingly
cabled Warner that he did "hope the best team would
be sent “home,” and Warner handed the missive to
the press, and passed on his way rejoicing. At his
first port he inquired anxiously what the Board of
Control had done about it; and when he was told
the Board had done nothing, and done it strenu-
ously, the explosion took place, and the lava and
dinders flew up 1000 ft. He was aghast that the
Board had .ignored Lord Desborough’s cable —it was
Unthinkable, awful. Apparently the Board did not
realise what an honor had been conferred upon it.
Whether he will arrive at the next port dead with
horror is hard to say. Next William McElhone
arose and was shocked that Mr. Warner should think
the Board would dream of interfering in his private
correspondence with the Peerage even had it seen
the cable, which it had not, for Mr. Warner had
riot sent it along, and the Board knew nothing ex-
cept the vague statements in the press. w llliam s
medical adviser reports, however, that he is now
out of danger and able to sit up.- #
The N.S.W. XI. that dealt-put sudden
death to Queensland struck a catastrophe at Too-
woomba. The wicket was soaked and N.S.W. cap-
tain Diamond sent tlie local team to bat. Bolkard
took six for 18, and the side vanished for 74. lhe
Cornstalks went in to rattle off the required number.
But Eric Barbour got loose, and, scattering five
wickets for 12 runs, burst up the show. The top
score was that of Waddy, who managed to jump to
25 before being thrown out, and the natives beheld
with amazement an XI that had walloped Queens-
land dismissed by Toowoomba for 81. On the second
day the wicket was still far from virtuous, but Too-
woomba managed to compile 147, and as the pitch
was improving all the time, the Cornstalks, with
orily 14l required to win, were buried in great calm.
But Barbour again got loose, and this time got away
with nine for 38, and, before the N.S.W. XI. knew
what had happened, it was all out for 80, and dished
by 61 runs. Then the N.S.W. XI. opened its mouth
tot speak, but could think of nothing adequate, and,
bqing too stunned to close its mouth again, a fly
got into the cavity. But what we all want to
know is—who is Eric Barbour 7 Why has he not
been found before ?
Last week this column threw some bricks
at Mr. “Hughie” Trumble, having by a slip con-
fused him with his brother, J. W. Trumble. How-
ever, as the rocks went wide of the mark, the writer
feels more hurt than the secretary of the Mel-
bourne Cricket Club. Of course, the remarks hurled
at Hugh Trumble were intended for our old friend
J.W., and are hereby declared null and void as far
as Hugh Trumble is concerned. Although he has
not expostulated in any way, this explanation is due
to him. As most people who follow the game closely
know, both brothers have been mighty cricketers
before the Lords and Commons. But Mr. J. W.
Trumble’s international career was, comparatively
speaking, as brief as his stay in Sydney. He chose
the better part, and after his single trip to England
in 1886, dropped out and settled down to the serious
business of life, sticking to cricket only as a pastime.
Hugh Trumble (who did not come to Sydney),
however, ranks with Spofforth, Turner and Ferris in
history as a phenomenal wicket-getter, and distin-
guished himself in five successive invasions of Eng-
land between 1890 and 1902.
By 22 votes to eight N.S.W. Cricket
Association threw out the motion of no-confidence
tabled against the Association’s delegates to the
Board of Control. Practically, McElhone and the
Board were called on to justify their procedure dur-
ing the recent upheaval, and McElhone rose to the
occasion. The change that has come over McElhone
is quite remarkable. Whereas he once swung a club
with both hands, he has now developed into a wily,
subtle old fox who deals out unlimited rope for the
other side to tangle itself up in. Which the Sorry
Six did. But when this motion came before the
Association the members felt that they had met a
long-lost friend. William P. got loose in his best
old-time style, and dealt out stoush and refutation in
a fearful manner, enlivened with flashes of scorching
sarcasm. It was a great speech, and when it was
over the people, who moved the motion were wrecks
under the table. 4
So much has been said about that letter
of conditions which the South Australian Cricket
Association forwarded to the Board of Control, with
its cheque for £5OO, that there is a general curiosity
to know what the thing was really like. Here it
is in all its glory: — ,
“Herewith I enclose cheque value £5OO, which I
have been instructed by the South Australian Cricket
Association to forward to you, subject to the express
conditions following:—
“(1) The amount forwarded, or so much thereof
as may be required to pay this Association’s pro-
portion of the legitimate preliminary expenses of the
1912 team, shall be used for such purpose only and
for no other, and the repayment thereof rateably with
amounts advanced by other Associations shall be
a first charge on the takings of the tour, as on occa-
sion of visit to England of 1909 team.
“(2). Preliminary expenses shall mean payment of
passages of the team to England and hotel and travel-
ling expenses there until after the first match has
been played.
“(3)'No portion of the said sum of £5OO shall be
used in. making any payment to any player except by
way of a nominal advance as against his share of the
profits of the tour, and in such ease any advance
made shall not exceed £5O.
“(4) r No advance out of the said sum of £5OO
shall be made to any player who has agreed or
agrees to accept £4OO from the Board of Control
in, lieu of his share of the profits of the tour, as this
Association contends that the Board cannot legally
make such an agreement or pay any money in respect
thereof.
“(5) No payment shall be made for the salary or
expenses of the Board’s representative out of the
said sum of £5OO.
“(6) This Association shall only pay its fair pro-
portion of preliminary expenses, and any moneys
received from other Associations shall also be ap-
plied only towards legitimate preliminary expenses
as above set out, and not for any other purpose, or
so as to increase the proportion of preliminary
expenses which this Association should have to bear.
“I enclose receipt for your signature.”
Saturdaj’ saw the commencement of the
10 th round of Sydney First Grade cricket, and
proved itself a batsman’s day. The gems of" the
afternoon were 181, not out, made in 210 minutes,
by L. A. Cody for Paddington against Sydney; 127,
perpetrated in 120 minutes, by 11. Cranney for Central
Cumberland against Waverley; 85, accumulated in
55 minutes, by Hughes of Paddington against Sydney ;
and 72, biffed in 130 minutes, by Andrews of Peter-
sham against North Sydney. Among the bowlers
the best were Coomber, of Waverley, who blew out
the lamp of four Central Cumberland wickets for 22
runs ; Folkard of Balmain, who suppressed five Bur-
wood wickets for 39; and Davis of Waverley, who
blotted six Central Cumberland sticks off the map
for 49. At Parramatta, Central Cumberland, the
local club, met Waverley, and was saved from utter
rout only by Cranney’s 127, the whole XI making
only 215. Still, in spite of Cranney’s effort, things
look far from gay, for Waverley, in the fading light,
totted up 104 without losing one of its masts. At
the Cricket Ground, Petersham knocked 167 off
the North Sydney bowling before the latter got a
chance to put up 92 for the loss of the first wicket.
Paddington, up against Sydney at Rushcutter’s Bay,
astonished the locals and itself by banging up 419
for six wickets. Cody’s fearsome 181 n.o. was a
display which kept the Sydney fieldsmen very busy
indeed. After this effort Burwood’s showing against
Balmain at Birchgrove was flat, stale and dispiriting
as last night’s beer; the whole side vanishing for
a measly 121. Balmain did not brighten things up,
losing three wickets for 48. Redfern, the crack
team, had apparently struck its dull day, and made
only 334 against University, which responded with
one for nothing. Up at Chatswood Glebe faced
Gordon, won the toss, and smashed together 309 all
in one afternoon.

In Melbourne pennant cricket on Satur-
day, Northcote collided with North Melbourne, and
hit up 251 for five wickets, Cheswass contributing
123 not out. In its match with the ’Varsity, South
Melbourne acquired eight for 233, and would have
done better but for the ’Varsity’s smart fielding.
Ransford hit up 147 in elegant style for Melbourne,
in the Melbourne-Richmond debate, before he went
out l.b.w. Melbourne lost five for 352. In the St.
Kilda-Essendon concussion the Saints’ innings was
declared closed at nine for 331. Fitzroy was sacked
for a miserable 125 by East Melbourne, which then
annexed 47 runs at the expense of three wickets.
P’ran’s score against Carlton stands at seven for 226.
* * * *
Secretary Hill, of N.S.W. Swimming
Association, the ruling amateur body, has been a
busy man lately. When the N.S.W. Ladies’ Ama-
teur Swimming- Association decided to request the
reporters to leave its recent meeting, it laid up
trouble for everybody; but Mr. Hill seems to have
struck the lion’s share of it. For the be?t part of
a week he was kept busy explaining to the press that
he did not do or say the things attributed to him.
Nearly every paper in Sydney, including this one,
laid things at his door which he declines to have
on his premises. He writes: “I did not say that,
although my Association admitted both men and
women, it was not paying; nor did I say that I
had but little hope of the Swimming Association
recommending the nominations of the Misses Duraclc
and Wylie to the Olympic Council.... Also I am
positive no one at the meeting stated that no modest
woman would compete at the carnival to be organised
by Alderman Kelly, because they would have to mix
in the water with colored boxers and other undesir-
ables.” This is published in fairness to Mr. Hill,
but the moral of it all is: “Never exclude the pro-
fessional reporter, as his place will assuredly be
taken by the unprofessional; and the amateur re-
porter is the most fearful type of human being on
earth. He breaks up homes; lays the foundations
of life-long feuds between the man who requires
publicity and the fellow who gives it; and encour-
ages the drink traffic horribly, as only a sea of
whisky can drown the enmities he creates.

With his usually pale, sweet face tanned
to the tint of newly-baked and somewhat-overdone
bricks, Harry B. James descended on Sydney last
week ; after having motored from Melbourne to Ade-
laide, and then back to the shores of Port Jackson,
over the route to be taken by the men in the big
Relay Despatch ride from Adelaide to Sydney, at
Eastertide. Notice boards have been erected all
along the route, and the whole of the details. ; -ftxed
up, and all that now remains is to get the riders —
car, motor-bike and cycle—started. The Coorong
Desert is always horrible, but on this occasion
James found it somewhat worse than usual. The
car —the Venus with which Syd Day put up his
record between Melbourne and Sydney—was loaded
up with rolls of matting, with which the long, bad
stretches were negotiated. If they were short ones,
ropes were hitched on, and all parties and the cook
hauled and hauled, and cursed the Coorong. But
the last straw was when the car pulled up at the
bottom of a huge wall of sand that rose like the
escarpment of a fort right across the road. All
hands got to, work with shovels, and changed their
opinions about pick-and-shovel-work not being
skilled _lab or (try it if you disagree) till they got
something like a grade, and then the matting was
. unrolled, and James, getting on board, let Gallagher
go for all he was worth. At about the third at-
tempt Gallagher, that is, Venus, snorted over with
one last convulsive kick and slithered down the other
side into a pit of its own making, where it stayed
till dug out and hauled on to the matting, which had
been rolled up, carried over the ridge, and laid down
afresh.
The contest will rage at Easter time,
April 5,6, 7 and 8, and is under the distinguished
patronage of the Australian Government. The Dun-
lop Rubber Co., as promoter, is, of course, attending
to all the details. In 1909 the bicycle relay ride
from Adelaide to Sydney was a great success, the'
despatch being whirled over the 1149 miles in 69hr.
3-smin. The presence of cars and motor-bikes in the
present struggle, and the consequent rivalry between
them and the reliable old push-bike, will lend an
additional interest. The push-bikes will leave Ade-
laide 24 hours ahead of the motor-bikes and 30 before
the cars, and the despatch they carry will be 368
. miles away, at Coleraine, in Victoria, before the
motor-bikes start to snort in pursuit; and the motor-
bikes will be plunging through the wilderness of Coo-
rong before the ears get going. There will be 65
relays of push-bikers, over distances varying from
10 to 28 miles, according to the nature of the coun-
try • 25 motor-bike relays', varying from 27 to 72
miles in length; and four relays of cars, varying
from 201 to 374 miles. It should be a fine tussle.
* * * *
The first chapter of J. D. Williams’
Cycling Carnival unrolled itself at Sydney Sports
Ground on Saturday afternoon and evening, and saw
some excellent racing and a tragedy, by which that
sterling rider and excellent sportsman, W. Bearpark,
lost his life, Bearpark was to have taken part in
the motor-cycle handicap, and was doing a trial
spin round the track. He had whirled round at a
dizzy speed several times, and apparently he threw
the throttle of his engine wide open as he swung
into the straight. The machine wobbled, and before
one had time to think it struck the fence, bounded
off it, and came down with a crash. Bearpark,
one of the coolest men who ever rode a bike, push or
motor, was thrown down on his head, and, unfortu-
nately, he was not wearing the skull-protecting cap.
He was placed in an ambulance and hurried away to
Sydney Hospital, where he expired a few minutes
after admission. The machine was not damaged,
and was ridden to victory by S. Hillsdon later in
the evening. Curiously enough, C. S. Crouch, the
well-known amateur rider, came down at the same
spot some time ago. It is worth while examining
that part of the track carefully, to see if there is a
lurking devil about it anywhere.
The racing was good and supplied about the big-
gest crowd of close and hotly-contested finishes ever
seen in Sydney. Every single event was fought
out in the straight, amidst wild excitement. The
motor-cycle races created great enthusiasm, and augur
well for the success of .the motordrome J.D.W. is
erecting. Another event that sent the crowd wild
was the struggle between A. J. Clark and Ivor Law-
son. Lawson won the first race, but Clark downed
the ex-Yankee very decisively in the second and
third. The carnival will be continued on the after-
noon and evening of this coming Saturday.
The Automobile Club of Australia (Syd-
ney) struck a rather unusual coincidence at its
hill climbing contest at National Park on Saturday
last. Twenty-one cars competed, and yet the first
three —Leslie Pye, J. Mailliard and G. F. Hill—
were the identical men who got home last competi-
tion two years ago and in the same order. The only
variation was that last time Mr. Pye took 126.8
seconds, and this year he snorted over the same in-
cline in 104 seconds.
* * * *
The Barry-McVea scrap, at Sydney Sta-
dium on Saturday, was a marvellous exposition of
what brute force and crass stolidity can do in the
ring against even a comparative overload of science.
For 20 solid and awful rounds, Barry, of Chicago,
was banged, and jabbed, and stabbed, and walloped
by McVea; and then, at the finish, was as strong as,
if not stronger than, his dark-brown competitor. The
toughness of these Yankees who have, during the
last few seasons, kicked up the Stadium dust has
been phenomenal; and their capacity to absorb pun-
ishment almost superhuman. Of course it is easily
explained: Any man who can attain maturity on food
prepared as described in Upton Sinclair’s “Jungle” is
beyond the power of the Devil himself to hurt seri-
ously ; though it might be remarked that some of
the grimaces he pulled would have made it hard to
distinguish McVea from the Devil. For he was a
very wild man sometimes. Still, it must be ad-
mitted, there were occasions, when Barry wasn’t
raging overmuch, and Samuel was doing as he liked
with the white man’s face, and the white man
clinched, when Samuel’s face took on a most remark-
able expression, while he held his hand aloft to show
the referee he was not hanging on. It was an ex-
pression that beggars description. It reminded one
of an early Christian martyr who had been forcibly
shaven and had tar poured* over him. At the con-
clusion of the rough-up McVea assumed a wrapper of
dove grey with purple .trimmings, and took his
ovation.
Mr. Barry struck a hard snag when in-fighting was
barred. All the Yankee fighters did, but it affected
Barry more than any of the others. He seems to
be absolutely ignorant of any other method of war-
fare. His ridiculous Yankee crouch, with his legs
fully extended, made it impossible for him to avoid
MeVea’s charges. His slowness allowed the dark
man to anticipate his fearsome round and over-arm
wallops to such an extent that he usually only tried
to strike McVea once every quarter of an hour, and
then only once with each fist. His utter inability
to time his blows, his insane method of circularising
all present that he intended (d.V. and MeVea’s left
permitting) to smite Mr. McVea, made it quite un-
necessary for the dark gentleman to be in the zone
of danger when the whirlies fell, for Barry sent them
along whether McVea was there or not. On one
occasion he forgot to issue his circular, and suddenly
lashed one of them right on the dark man’s chin and
neck. Over went the tall dark man, heels over head,
to come to a standstill on his haunches. After that
JdcVea declined to take any risks; in fact, he stood
Off, even when there was no risk; and had Barry
out loose with a regular cyclone of those wild swipes,
and kept it blowing at a good speed for about 90
seconds,, he would probably have got McVea. It
was somewhere about the 10th round that Barry dis-
covered what was plain to other people from the
first, and informed his seconds he was fighting to
last the 20 rounds. If Barry was only half the
boxer McVea is, he could eat that dark man inside
of 10 rounds. Why doesn’t Barry learn to box?
AVith his tremendous strength, his abnormal ability
to endure punishment, and his crass, thick-headed
courage, he would be among the world beaters if he
could box. What is the good of a boxer "who can’t
box? He is like a capitalist without any money.
Arnst, having given hostages to fortune,
has decided to meet, Barry and rake in the shekels
while the sun of youth is strong. So last week he
wandered with his wife to Bateman’s "Hotel,'Sydney,
and made merry. &t a 'feast? tendered them
by the scullers. Mfgi-'Arni't' Was presented with a
brooch of gold and gems. On Saturday jthey de-
parted for England on the Otway, iii cdUipany With
the majority of the Australian XI, At the Steamer
Bichard' bade the weeping friends, of the cricketers
have no fear, for in the event of a wreck, he would
pull them all ashore.
George Upward, clerk assistant of the
Senate —-the broad-shouldered identity who figures in
the foreground of Nuttall’s historical picture of the
opening of the first Australian Parliament—is start-
ing this month on a holiday trip to England, and all
the good amateur athletic “sports” of Melbourne and
thereabouts are invited to join in his send-off. Up-
ward was a mighty oarsman and sculler of yore, and
has been the mentor of many a victorious crew since
he joined the staff at Parliament House. It is as
vice-president and chairman of the Vic. Rowing
Association that Tactful George will be toasted and
buttered on the 23rd at Sargent’s Cafe. There are
two Upwards before the public nowadays,, and both
of them are George. The junior is a scenic artist for
the Combine. The “boy’s” talent for picturesque
effect in the playhouse was inherited; no doubt, from
his dad, who is a master of stage business in the
political ’ theatre.
* * * *
The cable credits veteran John Roberts
with the temerity to challenge any billiardist
champion or otherwise——to play him for any sum
with any balls,' ivory or composition. The message
says nothing about a stipulation as to the number
of points to be scored in a game, but it may be
presumed that the old man would impose a limit
on the trial of endurance. He would rather play
3000 up than iB,OOO up.- Anyhow, it is a remark-
able challenge for a man who is nearly midway in
the sixties to issue to the crowd whose leading
member is 20-year-old George Gray. As an exponent
of the art of billiards John Roberts is still the most
impressive Roman of them all. He is more likely
to make a break of 100 of more in brilliant style
within any given period of a session than any oppo-
nent worthy to meet him. But his capacity for long-
sustained effort has naturally weakened of late years;
his unexpected breakdown when he is playing
with beautiful ease and touch is due doubtless to
failing eyesight, and his nerve cannot possibly be as
iron as it was of yore. Dash, skill, judgment and
resourcefulness might serve to carry the veteran
triumphantly through a series of comparatively short
distance games, with restful pauses between, but he
can hardly have a hope of lasting through a long
and strenuous struggle with Gray, Stevenson or two
or three of the next best.
* * * *
Backers of Obi, who had bad luck when
the speedy hurdler got pipped on the Thursday of
the V.R.C. meeting, recovered their money with
interest at Caulfield last Saturday. Obi, starting at
four to one, won the St. Ivilda Cup with a length
to spare. With a vigorous rider on his back the
son of Coil is a fairly good horse in second-class com-
pany on the flat.

The Tote came out of its shell again at
Adelaide last Saturday, when it threw back to the in-
vestors on Lady Valois in the Trial Stakes £25 155.,
and solaced the people who picked the second, in the
same race, with £35 12s. for having failed to pick
the winner. Christian Moses does occasionally pay
odds equal to the tote on the winner, but up to the
present writer has never caught him paying out £ss
on a second prad.
“Hee Ivee” writes from the Sunset State:
Wren, somewhat widely known in Victoria, arrived
here recently with a cheque book and some looge
silver, and began to inquire about a suburban race-
course. Some obliging gentleman offered him one
for £70,000, but even Wren threw aside his native
modesty and kicked at that price. However, the
site was deemed by him to be a good one, and he
secured a 10 years’ lease of it. It is said that his
first offence with his new implement of evil will be
a £5OO flutter, and, according to the lines it is to
be run on, it would appear that Wren is here to aid,
abet and encourage the proprietary clubs in their
production of the sprint squib. Not 10 per cent, of the
horses racing in Westralia at present could contest a
two-mile run except under go-as you-please conditions.
This is probably accounted for by the weird fact
that there are only two events per annum on our
racing menu which touch that limit.
N.S.W. bowlers were sadly buffeted in
Tasmania, above all places. At Hobart, Southern
Tassy defeated them by 95 to 88 and 105 to 87,
while Ma scored only once with 109 to 86. At
Launceston, Northern Tasmania took the Ma Staters
and rubbed their noses in the Tamar in all three
battles. Tasmania is coming on, and the recent
phenomenal outbreak in cricket has been quite
eclipsed by this new upheaval in bowling. Is the
place waking up ?
Balmain, Sydney’s tattered suburb, re-
peated last year’s success by gathering in the
N.S.W. Rowing Association Pennant for 1911-1912.
The water-siders scored 134' points, while the nearest
competitor, Sydney, accumulated only 64, and Leich-
hardt mopped up 62. The other six were so far
away that they were quite forgotten. The Junior
Pennant passed to Leichhardt with 62 points, which
had for runner-up Enterprise, with 40. The other
seven tapered off on a descending scale, which ended
with Sydney and ’Varsity in the mud, with nary a
point between them.
Arthur B. Postle took his fill of glory and
incidentals at Auckland (M.L.), on Saturday, when
he defeated Jack Donaldson in a three-cornered chal-
lenge match. Postle won at all three distances,
skimming over the 75yds. in 7 l-ssec. ; traversing
the 150yds. in 14 l-ssee. and the 200yds. in 19sec.
In addition to the prize Postle bagged a considerable
side wager.
Some more records: Vedrines, the French
flying man, recently skimmed 94 miles in 62min.
23see. He used a monoplane, driven by 100 h.p.
engine. R. «T. Lewis, a Melbourne motorist, re-
cently drove from Sydney to Brisbane in 76 hours
gross. His actual driving time for the 700 miles
was 32 hours. Some of the stretches of road he
banged and rattled over were absolutely awful.
Judging by the state of the tracks there is a con-
spiracy between the N.S.W. and Queensland Govern-
ments to make motorists take their cars over by
railway truck.
DYSPEPSIA.
WHAT A SYDNEY WOMAN SUF-
FERED —AND HOW RELIEF
CAME FROM TAKING DII.
MORSE’S INDIAN ROOT PILLS.
There is no person more to be pitied than
the Dyspeptic, who, if he eats at all, is in
dread of the consequences, which he knows
from experience are sure to follow. When
a remedy is found to relieve the terrible
agony endured after every meal, a feeling
of gratitude only remains. Such an one is
Emily Barry, 98 Dowling Street, Surry
Hills, Sydney, who writes : —-
“I have in the past suffered the torture
of Dyspepsia and Indigestion, which lasted
for a period of eight months, during which
time I was under treatment in St. Vincent’s
Hospital, but that did very little good: I
used to gasp with pain, which I would get
between the shoulder blades, like the stab
of a knife. I would place my hands be-
hind my back to ease these pains, and keep
them there until the sinews in them would
become contracted. The sharpness of these
attacks was so dreadful that I could scarcely
breathe, and hardly walk about. At last,
seeing Dr.' Morse’s Indian Root Pills ad-
vertised, I thought I would try them. I had
not finished one bottle before I noticed an
improvement, and continued their use until
now I seem entirely free -from the trouble.
I have pleasure in recommending them to
others, and if you wish you can use this for
publication purposes.”

started off with a definite idea, and tried to express

often has eight stanzas completed before he remem-
bers that he was set going by observing a fair-
headed girl scrambling into a tram, and that the
purpose of the poem is to christen her Mary, tell
her she has the loveliest ankles and hair in the
world, and gently intimate that she has the right
sort of bosom to make a pillow for a poet.... Quid-

“Umhlatuzi” (from S’Afriea) :
In case the owner of £2 11s. 4d. or any other
large lump of capital should be driven out of Aus-
tralia by Socialistic legislation, and, being thus
driven out, should seek refuge in Africa, let me
draw his attention to a bilingual circular just posted
at all the Union’s railway stations. This notice sets
out that, on account of a shortage of colored labor,
special inducements are being offered to white men,
! “between the ages of 18 and 40, of good character
and physically lit,” to join the railway service. The
special inducements consist mainly of a revised rate
of pay, which for the Cape Province runs as follows:
First month, 3s. per day; next six months, 3s. 6d.;
next six months, 45.; thereafter, 4s. 6d. Men who
have reached the dizzy height of 4s. 6d. may be
utilised as leading hands.” Four whole days a year
are allowed as holidays on full pay, and if there
are quarters available thfe hands —who are under-
stand that they are graded as “temporary —may
nig in them. You will observe from these rates, etc.,
how amply justified the Chowth African war was,
and how wonderfully prosperous the subsequent im-
nortation of Ling Fat and Belt Hard has left the
country. Also, you will notice how advantageous
i it is to the white working-man to have a large black
i population to help in what is called thedevelopment
1 of the country. Here we usually call it the devil-
! mont” of the country.


Letters from Bibendum to a Self-made
Motorist.
IV.
df
Bi, Fulham Road,
London, S.W.
January 24th, 1912.
My dear Miles,—At the present moment your place is at
the business end of a moral toasting fork ; and for sound
reasons I’m just going to keep you well down on the coal-
glow for a goodish bit.
You see, I’ve never yet been convinced by the application
df
of any of the ordinary
amateur stage-direc-
tions for simulating
righteous indignation;
and even when a
fellow starts in on me
in a well modulated
silver - clarion - with-a-
cold-in-it voice—one
in which you can hear
“ Tve never yet been convinced” the tear-drops splash-
ing on his soft palate-like you have done, I naturally begin
to nose for signs of decay, all the same.
That is just why I have been sniffing round that “rotten
badly-built spare cover ” yarn you have been slopping such
a lot of bluff-juice over. And let me say, right here, that a
bad argument is like a dead rat under the flooring : the more
it is dwelt on, the easier it is to locate.
The mist ike you made was sending the King’s Evidence
Tight along and letting it talk for itself.
Your sending me that cover reminds me of the unsuccess-
ful bid William Anstruther Ronald Thorold Young—
•commonly known as Warty from his initials made to
regain the confidence of the Vicar of Loam-
well. When Warty was about twelve he
developed a keen interest in taking care of
any unconsidered trifles he could find lying
round loose in people’s gardens and back
yards. But in the still watches of one
murky night, he extended his operations to
the Vicar’s vegetable garden, and, next
morning, sold to that gentleman’s cook half
William
Ajistruther
Ronald
Thor old
Young.
a barrow load of fresh young potatoes.
That was where he came to grief. The Vicar’s gardener
was old and deaf ; but Warty must have calculated he was
blind and dumb as well. Anyhow the Vicar obtained the
permission of Warty’s widowed ma and demonstrated to
Warty that there was a strong cane that had no turning. For
three months thereafter Warty was so good that he began to
illustrate the fact that, for him at least, the straight and
narrow path was just about the widest thing in turnpikes
on the survey. Then he won the Sunday School prize
for an essay on Honesty. Next day the Vicar stopped
him and shook hands. Warty was carrying a bag. “A
splendid essay, my boy,” said the dear old Vicar, “and I
am sure you wrote it ,so well because the error of your
former ways has really struck home to you.” “Yes, Sir,
said Warty, with a little break in his pure young treble.
“ Ah, yes,” went on the Vicar, beaming kindly at his little
4.A
M
NX
“ A splendid essay my boy," said the dear old Vicar.
friend; “and you’re taking something home to Mother?”
“Yes, sir,” said Warty, “a nice vegetable marrow, sir. ’
And the Vicar would have felt no breath of suspicion if at
that moment some passing ducks had not quacked. The
game was up when the nice vegetable marrow in the bag
quacked back.
Spare the duck and spoil the yarn was Warty’s trouble.
Your trouble is that you ducked the spare and spoilt the
yarn just the same. You can’t side-step it ; every stain
round that burst is a double-bass quack to the effect that
you have left that cover out in the wet for weeks. The
soakage has gone right through ; which is another proof
of your utter carelessness ; and altogether I think you had
better start in, right now, to get sense.
Perhaps it will help you to know that that cover never
would have burst suddenly if you had kept it dry all the
time, and away from the light and air in a waterproof
wrapper. And here’s another bit of elementary informa-
tion for your guidance : If you store spare covers in a
damp atmosphere they will perish. Leave them in their
original wrappers ; keep them off the floor ; and see that
your garage is dry. Also, when you get home after a wet
run, don’t make a break for that bath and change, until
you have seen to it that your tyres have been lightly sponged
over and carefully dried. On this point, I might mention
in passing, some folks reckon they know more than their
daddy, and just let be on the dirt and the wet. But it’s
not what a man thinks he knows, it’s what he knows counts
and counts as money saved when it comes to drying
covers and filling up their surface cuts.
Your sincere friend,

Business, Robbery, etc.
“Fair and Forty”: Re The Bulletin's statement
(29/2/’l2) that the Life Assurance cos. do not know
of any 100 year-olds on their books. The big American
cos. assume that everybody is dead at 96. Thus, an
aged policy-holder in the Mutual Life of New York —
one Daniels—reached 96 last year. As he was officially
dead, he applied for his money, and the co. paid in
full.
* * * *
“The Hack”: I chanced on a new kind of compe-
tition the other day—bed-making. It was at a big
Melbourne lodging-house, which nightly provides
beds for about a thousand Wearies. Each bed-maker
is expected to “make” 60 beds an hour. Some
former employees (now dead) are alleged to have
been able to “make” more; but they were the aristo-
crats or boskers of the profession. Anyone who can’t
make at least 45 beds an hour after a week’s practice
is accounted a duffer, and is expected to return to
the Law or something easy—and a heap better paid.
* * * *
“Pick”: If any Ma Stater has an indisputable right
to curse his luck freely, and incidentally drop in an
extra-blistering anathema for the benefit of those
who defeated the Referenda proposals on the plea
that no combines existed in Ma State, they are the
South Coast fishermen. The fish trade ring holds
such a grip on the business that no distant supplier
has any chance of dealing in the metropolitan mar-
kets except through its agents, who supply the neces-
sary cases and ice for packing. The fisher knows
pretty well what the Sydney, consumer has to pay
for the meat of; the sea, but in his wildest dreams
he never imagines himself getting half that much,
and he can kick if he likes and he hanged! If he
kicks too hard the agents will ignore his application
for cases and ice, and he may then sit down and
watch his boats and nets rot. This, however, is only
part of his luck. Ostensibly for the want of decent
harbor accommodation, but really for the want of
decent opposition, the steamboat service is irregular
and uncertain. The fishermen expect some casual
old trough to call in at schedule time, and they
toil mightily to get their consignment ready for
shipment; but if the boat has got lost, or tipped
up, or run out of firewood for the engines, their
fish have to remain boxed up in the sun, until the
hum wakes the wharfman, who then boots them
overboard. So the lakes and inlets are often use-
lessly depleted of fish, the fisher at the wet end of
the job, and the consumer at the dry one, are both
snookered, and the fellow who smiles is the Dago
middleman, because the market isn’t glutted and
that is good for “de beez.”
* * * *
Someone has discovered another use for Australia’s
old, reliable blue gum. This time it is its paper-
making qualities, and a co., with a capital of a
quarter of a million, has been formed to pursue the
expected dividends, with Tassy as the probable start-
ing point. The demand for wood pulp suitable for
paper manufacture is eternally on the increase, and
dubious looks have already been ca(st on the sources
of, supply, but if the new scheme is successful, even
Randolph Bedford should have no further anxiety for
a year or two that he might yet have to write his
stories on slate, or scratch his verses on corrugated
iron with a bradawl.
* * * *
“Quidam”: If you live in a country town and
begin running bills, they are liable to get away from
you, and the end is vexation of spirit and sum-
monses. On the other hand, if you pay cash, you
“ ave to pay Just the same prices as if you tick things
up. The average farmer would fall stone-dead re-
peatedly if requested to pay for anything before next.
harvest, and the storekeeper has to allow for all this
m his prices; so the man who pays a credit price in
cash is an ass, and is laying out his money in a. most
uncommercial way. This is where genius comes in.
Once you’ve established confidence in a country town,
a particularly easy matter to do, tick up everything
you buy, but at the same time put the price of it in
the Savings Bank. Allow the account to stand over
until the pillar of trade and commerce begins to
attach pained memos, to the account rendered, “fear-
ing that • you have overlooked the matter”-—-which
means that you’ve let it stand over about a year.
Then draw the amount out of the financial edifice
where it is reposing, and pay the pillar with a dig-
nified expression, which gives him the idea that he
has foolishly offended a valuable customer. He will
then offer you a cigar. It may be a very bad one,
but to be seen smoking it is a guarantee that you
have paid a bill, and has a moral effect on other trades-
men, who know the smell of their rivals’ weeds. By
this brilliant system all you have to do is to buy
enough things, and you can live on the interest of
what you owe, practically the most ideal method of
existence yet introduced to this mundane sphere.
My bitterest regret is that in my callow days I have
been fool enough to pay cash whenever I had it. The
only alleviating circumstance is that I have thereby
missed smoking a lot of cheap cigars.
A COMPLAINT OP WINE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Professor Guthrie deprecated our Australian vin-
tages masquerading under borrowed names, and ad-
vocated distinctive local description. —Press item.
The pleasant juices of the South,
That in the crystal leap or dream,
Between the bottle and the mouth
In red or amber gleam.
Above the vines the lightnings fork,
The splendid suns arise and set;
The music of the popping cork
Is balm for old Regret.
Ye drain the draught, the light is shed,
A tang upon the tongue remains,
The sunrise rushes to the head,,
The moontide to the veins.
Young Bacchus, ivy-crowned, goes by,
The wonder wakes. on land and sea,
The gladdened moments learn to fly
On feet of witchery.
Or long or short the bottles stand,
And this is “port”' and that “champagne”—
New vintage with the elder brand
Of France or sunny Spain.
Mock port, mock sherry, mock champagne,
Mock everything that Europe grows—
The South Australian Aquitaine
With ruddy “claret” glows.
Young Bacchus ivy-clad goes by,
And, warmer than the suns of France,
The young glad splendors of the sky
On glowing vineyards glance.
New suns, new skies, a wider air,
New vintage foaming in the press
A new world’s vintage that must wear
An old world’s faded dregs.
Above the vines the lightnings fork,
The splendid summers rise and set;
What reck ye so ye draw the cork.
And find the liquid wet?
The inspiration of the grape,
Within your torpid souls aflame,
Fails of its force to bid you shape
For new delight a name.
Or long or short the bottles stand,
And this is “sherry,” that' is “hock”—
Glad juices of a younger land,
The old-world labels mock.
N.s.w.
MARY McCOMMONWEALTH.

IN BANANA LAND
Roosevelt is small potatoes as a big-game
hunter now. Two Queenslanders, the Curr
brothers of Abingdon Downs and Inkerman
stations, last year spent four months in
East Africa, where they bagged 17 lions, 40
hartbeeste, 21 zebras, and a whole truck
load of etceteras down through leopards,
hogs and giraffes.
“Jack” : I don’t want to be a policeman
in Queensland. When gathering names for
the electoral rolls, I’d be certain of being
accused by Fat of pursuing the Laborite to
the bitter end of the last beer in order to
rope him in, and of displaying a violent
desire to let the man with a stake in the
country look after his own vote or lose it.
And, during a strike, I’d be just as sure of
being mauled by the strike leaders and called
one of Cahill’s “Cossacks,” a “base, brutal
1 atoner,” and a “licensed assassin.” The
police, however, don’t seem to bear malice,
though,' as three-fourths of them at the very
least are good Laborites, it seems dead fool-
ish to aim mud their way. However, that
has been the habit all through this foolish
business, which has done a ton of injury
to Labor’s friends for every lb. it has done
its enemies.
Certificate, signed by all the residents of a
Queensland district, and handed to the de-
parting oldest inhabitant the other day:—
As Mr. Luke Pipe has decided to take his departure
for England, we, the undersigned residents of the
Bushley district, feel that we cannot lose such a
highly esteemed and respected resident without ex-
pressing our sincere sorrow and regret, and we have
much pleasure in giving him these few lines as a
reference to show to those with whom he may come in
contact that he is a perfect gentleman. Mr. Pipe
has been a valued resident of the Bushley district
for over 40 years, during which time he has been the
most prosperous farmer in the district. His good
qualities ’we all admit are unlimited. His
knowledge of farming is also unlimited, and for many
years he was the champion farmer of Central
Queensland and won first and special prizes for all
kinds of farm produce at the Rockhampton Agricul-
tural Society’s show. Asa fruitgrower he has also
taken a leading part, he being the only person in
the district that can grow apples successfully. He
is possessed of a wonderful secret concerning fruit
trees, which we hoped that he would at some time
have made known to us; but, unfortunately for us,
he is taking the secret with him. Asa weather
prophet he has no equal in the district, and this
gift is a great advantage in this country, especially
to the farmers. We hand this reference to Mr. Pipe,
feeling confident that he deserves all the eulogy
herein contained, and we sincerely hope and pray
that he may live many years and enjoy both health
and happiness.
If the Queensland Labor party wants to
commit suicide at next month’s State elec-
tion, one aid to that end will be the putting
up of Coyne and some of his dreadful col-
leagues as candidates. To do so is to asso-
ciate the party definitely with the insane
General Strike policy—the policy which
penalises union employers for the benefit of
non-union ones. It means a general en-
dorsement of the conduct of -4jhe tffb,culent
madmen who lately declared war against
friends and foes alike, against all industry,
and against the State at large, over a very
small question which was even then before
the Arbitration Court and in process of set-
tlement. The policy of the strike leaders
was that Labor should be the general enemy
—the anarchist and outlaw and mad dog of
society. And unless that policy is repudi-
ated, the party is likely to have a steep road
to travel for a long while to come.
Besides landing Brisbane workers in
grievous trouble and failure, the Queensland
strike leaders have pulled Premier Denham
out of the biggest hole of his long career.
Digby D. had promised to hold a special
session in April, to deal with the rejected
Liquor Bill. The Liquor Trade was arm-
ing for a big election fight, and the Wowser
was furbishing his cannon and calling out his
legions, and the multitude of his brolly was
like the reflection of the stars on the Sea of
Galilee. Now, so much has the wondrous
General Strike obliterated all other consid-
erations, Denham is able to drop the prom-
ised April session and the agonising Liquor
Bill, and go straight to the country. His
plea that it is necessary to drag the Par-
liamentary strike leaders before their con-
stituents at once is accepted on all sides,
and no Wowser shakes a gamp at Denham,
nor does one Bung throw a pint-pot at his
head.
Martin’s Pills are sold by all Chemists and

John Wilson passed out at Rockhampton,
the other day, and so the diminishing group
that saw that city in its beginnings has one
the fewer. Wilson arrived in ’65, and
worked and grew old, while a deal of town
property grew valuable. He came to the
scene of action in the Empress of the Seas,
with 490 others, and 32 died on the 91-days
voyage, mainly from “insufficiency of food.”
In other words, they died from a pain in
the place where the dinner ought to be.
J. W. Blair, one of Ipswich’s members,
who tore himself away from the Ividston
Government because he couldn’t get a wig
which he declared was promised him to put
on his youthful brow, lias returned to the
fold. He will henceforth chase the elusive
voter in the Government interest. Blair was
at one time- Attorney-General in the days
when George Kerr and Peter Airey flourish-
ed as Ministers of State.
Strike-leader Harry Coyne told a Too-
woomba-meeting that the Strike Committee
deserved credit, “the entire credit,” for pro-
viding the food supply and maintaining peace
and order during the strike. Amid the gen-
eral confusion it will soon be necessary to
appoint a Parliamentary committee to find
out if it wasn’t the farmers who really
struck against Coyne, and if Coyne’s army
prevented Denham from closing the port of
Brisbane against Badger’s steamers. The
present position seems to be that Coyne’s
forces didn’t strike and establish a sort of
blockade; rather they worked overtime to
earn food and gave it away for unspecified
reasons to parties unknown.

“F. 8.”: A recent Bulletin par remarked
that Queensland once well-nigh became a
Chinese province. Dr. Lang, in “Freedom
and Independence” (published 1852) stated
that 1000 Chinese were imported into More-
ton Bay alone, and that “that district has
contributed not less than £13,000 for the
importation of foreigners through the hein-
ous policy of the Colonial Office.” The Fat
Crowd dumped the first shipload of Chinese
into N.S.W. in 1848, and Brisbane received
a sample two years later. Within a few
years of the opening of the Palmer rush
there were 20,000 Chows in the district
back of Cooktown, and the police had a
picnic in suppressing a kind of civil war
that broke out between Celestials hailing
from different districts. Yes, the “Chinese
province” was well nigh a fact in the ’7o’s
of the lately-pegged-out century.
“Saith the Koran.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
From the Sons of Islam true,
Saith the Koran, it is due
(And in following out this precept they must faint
not)
That the likeness of the beast,
From the greatest to the least,
They shall carve it not in stone, and they shall
paint not.
For on Adam’s sons of old
Fell a madness manifold,
And a fear that struck them trembling all and
craven;
And they bowed them in the shade
To the things their hands had made,
And were worshippers of pictured things and graven.
And whoe’er shall fall from grace,
And portray the breathing face,
Or in stone cut out the living form and feature,
He shall bide in Eblis dark,
Till he lend the living spark
To the stone that he hath carven as a creature.
Dear Reformer, brother mine,
In your planning fair and fine,
Keep the rainbows in the back of your recital,
Or the folk in sudden fire
May demand that you inspire,
In the puppets of your dreaming something vital!
And in case your golden skies
Do not promptly realise,
And they find your pictured glories but a vision,
They will damn your life and work,
And condemn you (like the Turk
Does his Koran-breaking artists) to perdition.
QUEENSLAND.
FURNESS BORN.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, March 16, 1912.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
The local representaiive of the Amal-
gamated Society of Tame Governors has
been pursuing his onerous duties in his usual
determined and uncompromising manner
since last I wrote the last Epistle of Jane to
the Sydonians. He attended the Warwick
Show and expressed his sub-regal -apprecia-
tion of the four-legged horse and the 10-
gallon cow; he returned and was re-elected
Chancellor of the University without a
struggle; and on Monday he braves the
yellow pup at St. Patrick’s jamboree. His
health shows no ill-effects and his spirits
are excellent.
Ex-Mayoress Diddams was the guest of
honor at a hen convention tea at the Cafd
Majestic last Wednesday. Before she
could escape, a necklet of pearls, diamonds
and aquamarines was thrust upon her,
while a bangle for little daughter Eileen
was thrown in as ballast. Mesdames
Crase and Hetherington, both ex-mayoral
dames, made the usual few well-chosen re-
marks, and the fervid applause drew a tear-
drop to the recipient’s eye-lash. Leon Ber-
liner and Alma Y|ullen served the music,
and if any votes of censure were passed I
didn’t hear of them.
On Tuesday, dark-haired Bessie Morrisett
meekly promised to obey one Syd Baynes,
who is a son of Harry Baynes and brother
of Mrs. M. Edwards, of this city. The
bride was caparisoned in the usual satin and
lace, and enveloped in a veil that has
already done much blushful service, having
been worn by seven various brides. The
first of these was Mrs. Walker, mother of
Mrs. Espie Dods, the present owner. Judge
Chubb, in his capacity as uncle, handed over
the dutiful Bessie, and Nina Walker and
Yaux Baynes were towers of strength to
the principals. The honeymoon will shine
in the sky above Southport.
News of another engagement enlivens the
dulness of our afternoon-tea tables. This
time the feminine half is handsome Marion
Darvall, who has been holidaying in the
Boonah district, and the victim of her charms
is one Matthew Wilkinson, a nephew of the
late Bishop Hale. He is a pastoralist by
trade.
The Demosthenes bore Englandward Doris
and Blanche Philp, who will be joined at
Melbourne by their political parent. A. B.
Overell, his pretty wife, and his fascinating
sister, Cherie, were also included in the
manifest of the ship that is named after
the eloquent Greek, now deceased.
Emmie Powell, late of the Telegraph,
who is shortly to wed Editor McKinnon, of
the Darling Downs Gazette, is approach-
ing her new state through a labyrinth of
tea-parties and kindred dissipations. Old
Girls of Brisbane High School waylaid her
the other day and handed out a silver rose
bowl (which her husband will no doubt
use as an ash tray or match holder). On
Thursday, Mrs. A. Moon, arrayed in silk
and lovely lace, flung open the hospitable
doors of Conon, and entertained the lady’s
friends at a d’oyley tea, which was one of
the most charming in the memory of the
oldest Pekoe inebriate. Roses were every-
where, even on the tiny name cards that
marked the place of each guest, and rose-
leaf and confetti showers were signs of the
times.
The races to-day were quiet. Miss Celia
Cooper gave a tea-party on the verandah of
the stand, and Mrs. Real, daughter Kath-
leen and Phil Forrest were among the in-
vited.
Mr. and Mrs. Orchard extended the hand
of welcome to bowlers and their feminine
appurtenances at the New Farm rinks to-
day. The hostess, who was draped in
black satin and a short blue coat, provided
an inspiring advertisement of the benefits
of the hospital treatment which she recently
underwent.
Mr. and Mrs. Maxwell Macartney, of the
Darling Downs, are amongst the travel-
weary folk who have lately chaperoned lug-
gage in foreign parts. They have travellers’
tales to tell, especially about Ireland and
the struggles of their fellow-tourists to kiss
the blarney stone.
Yours, with forebodings of the winter,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
Still they come! Another coal-king in
embryo is W. H. Robertson who has tapped
6ft. of a coal seam at Stanley (C.Q.). The
distance from Rockhampton is 95 miles,
which makes it the closest seam to that city,
so if the newspaper gush is even half true,
things generally are expected to boom like
a Badger. Robertson manages a bank, by
the way, but has a hobby for coal-stuff as
his father of Porbanlea (Burrum) did be-
fore him. Naturally “it is splendid steam-
ing coal, no clinker, and 82 per cent, of
fixed carbon,” is only two miles from the
railway, and everything about it is just
lovely.
“Waliala” from a remote part:—
Mention is made ((B. 30/11/T1) of the acci-
dental discovery at Mackay of a cannon, alleged to be
one of the number jettisoned by Captain Cook when
the Endeavor tripped over a reef near Cape Tribula-
tion, ’way back in 1770. The chances are that
Cook never saw that gun. About 1890, a monument
was erected in Cooktown, near the site where Cook
repaired the Endeavor, and the matter of searching
the reefs for the jettisoned guns and things was
then brought forward by Captain W. E. Armit, who
was editing the Courier at the time. A good deal
of discussion arose over the matter, and finally Armit
went in command of a vessel and made a thorough
search of the reefs around Cape Tribulation, but
neither guns nor any other ironware could he find.
Armit was of the opinion (and no man was more
competent to give one on this subject) that the
growth of coral since the cannon were jettisoned—
over 120 years ago—had been sufficient to completely
cover up all traces of them, and, taking this fact
into consideration, the writer feels doubtful if the
cannon at Mackay can be one of Cook’s. Dozens
of vessels have been lost in Torres Straits and
thereabouts, and it was common for vessels of any
size in the ’6o’s or ’7o’s to carry a few cannon for
signalling purposes.

Government Notice.
Department of the Treasury,
4th March, 1912.
A PPLICATIONS wH be received up to 15th
April, 1912, by the Secretary, Department of the
Treasury, Melbourne, from persons qualified for ap-
pointment to the position of AUSTRALIAN NOTE
PRINTER.
Applicants must be capable of taking complete
charge of printing currency notes for Australia from
steel plates. Original dies will be manufactured in
London, but working plates will be required to be
made by the Printer in Australia.
A thorough knowledge of bank note printing, pro-
cess of preparing working plates and machinery re-
quired for printing is essential.
Salary: £BOO per annum. Five years’ engagement
guaranteed.
ANDREW FISHER,
Treasurer.
