ARE YOU
DEAF?
If vou are deaf, or going deaf, you will
be glad to hear of a simple and scientitic de-
vice which will restore your hearing, no wax-
ier how old you are. There is no ™y st ® ?
or secrecy about it; we describe it fully a
explain just how it acts. ,
You use spectacles as sight magnifiers, ana
in the same way you may use this sound
magnifier as an aid to weak hearing, du ,
unlike spectacles, the Wilson Common-sense
Ear-Drums are invisible and comfortable.
You can’t tell you are wearing them. No
one else can, either, because they are not
They rest the ear nerves by taking tne
strain off them—the strain of trying to hear
dim sounds. What a blessed relief to have
done with that tiring strain!
Being made of the purest and softest Para
rubber, they cannot be felt, even when the
head rests on a pillow. For the same reason,
a blow on the side of the head will not hurt
the ear. In fact, they protect the ear, the
tender inner parts that are so sensitive, from
cold winds, dust, sudden and piercing sounds.
So that you may clearly understand why
these facts are true, and why the Sound Mag-
nifier restores the hearing, we give an illus-
tration of one as it lies in the ear. You will
notice that the Sound Magnifier is shaped
like a trumpet, and so conveys even the
faintest sounds right to the natural ear-
drum. Being of the softest rubber, it cannot
fV
hurt the ear. It fits the orifice of the ear
with precision and comfort. The division
that runs across the mouth is to allow the re-
moval and insertion of the Ear-Drum, and to
brace it. ,
The shape of the Wilson Ear-Drum has
been chosen after years of experience as the
best adapted to carry the faintest sound right
down to the natural ear-drum.
To persons who are nearly deaf, it is not
only trying to have people shout at them,
but it hurts the ear-drum. When age comes
the ear-drum sometimes gets thickened, or
loses its tightness, and shouting does not act
properly on it. It only makes a confused
and tiring noise. But the Sound Magnifiers
concentrate the sound in the best spot for
perfect hearing. , , ~ ,
Of course, if you have lost the hearing-
nerve completely, or were born deaf, we can-
not pretend to cure you. Only a miracle
could do that, in spite of some of the absurd
claims made to-day. But if you can hear the
faintest sound, do not despair. You will
never regret trying the Wilson Ear-Drums.
We have letters to show you from deaf people
in Australia and New Zealand who have been
cured, and you can be cured, too. You may
wear the Ear-Drums day and night, and take
them out whenever you wish. Children can
wear them (we have special sizes for children
from six to twelve). Even a child can insert
and remove them without the least danger.
The price is £1 (the fee for a doctor’s
single visit). This includes a pair of Drums
(they are, only sold in pairs), a fine nickel
remover and also an inserter. After the first
pair is bought you may purchase a single
drum at any time for Bs., but the pair you
get at first will last quite two years. Do not
ask for a trial pair. We do not send them
out on trial, and so they are never second-
hand. Use the Order Form below, and the
Outfit will be sent to you at once, with the
fullest instructions. Don’t delay, but have
the comfort of good hearing as soon as you
can.

The Story of Abdul Kerim.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Bill has been introduced into the Italian Parlia-
ment, and passed with much enthusiasm, declaring
that Tripoli is annexed. — Cable.
ABDUL KERIM lived beside the dark
blue Mediterranean Sea
His clothes were funny, he’d not much
money, but he was happy as he could be;
With hens and goats, and some fishing-
boats, and figs and dates he lived con-
tent ;
He never worried; he rarely hurried, and
didn’t care much how anything went!
Prom dark to light he slept all night, and
rose up earlv and said his prayers ;
Yea! twice a day, in Mahomet’s way, he
sought relief from Shaitan’s snares.
He’d fourteen wives and some horrid knives
—a coffee-pot and a bubble-pipe;
He hated pigs, and civilisation, “Christian
dogs,” and the Time being Ripe !
Though foreign fools had drains, and schools,
and locomotives, and books, and drugs,
Abdul Kerim would have none near him — he
drew the line at tiles and rugs;
His musical ear was bad, I fear; in theatres
he took no delight;
He never “aspired.” He was far too tired.
But when he wanted a change he’d
fight!
Fight! Ah, yes! Old Abdul Kerim leading
his liglit-brown sinewy sons,
Taught the negro tribes to fear him—him
and his long, crook-handled guns.
Fight! He’d dreamed of it in his cradle —
learned it all in his mother’s lap ;
Just as the white man learns to labor, Abdul
Kerim had learned to scrap !
Pietro, he was a restless Dago —also living
beside that sea;
Fond of garlic and fish and sago, macaroni
and eau-de-vie;
Though decadent and rather dirty, Pietro
wasn’t so badly bred —
Centuries back—say, nearly twenty—Pietro’s
fathers the whole world led.
Down the dark blue Mediterranean, cooped-
up Pietro observed afar
Abdul Kerim, lazy, faineant; plainly, his
was a setting star!
“Ecco /” shouted the lively Pietro. “That
old fellow has had his day !
Me —I go—l seize his country ! Evviva!
L’ltalia fard da sd!”
Forth went Pietro, the Happy Pirate; all-
unconscious Abdul dozed ;
Roused by shoutings he sat up, irate. What
was this to his gaze disclosed ?
Pietro? 'Never! Of Abdul’s neighbors least
to be suspect of guile !
Allah! ’twas Pietro, with guns and sabres
waving in most offensive style!
Rage shook Abdul; he started forward,
grabbed liis carvers and cocked his gun;
Called his sons from the desert nor’ward,
and came at Pietro upon the run.
Short and sharp was the dusty trouble; both
were breathless and both were hurt;
Pietro left at a hasty double —Abdul squat-
ted him in the dirt!
Pietro rallied his frightened senses as soon
as ever he reached the sea —
This is the part where the fun commences —
so, at least, it appears to me!
For, with regular Roman humor, slightly
“watered” by lapse of years,
Pietro classed as mere idle rumor all accounts
of his bitten ears !
Further, anxious to let each neighbor know
how mightily he had scored,
Pietro put away all his sabre, saying, “ The
pen, it heats the sword!”
Then he issued a Proclamation —beautiful
language fashioned it!
“Abdul is part l of the Pietro Nation /” —•
Abdul doesn’t worry a bit.
N.S.W.
R. McGEDDON.
A Bottle.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I WONDER how Jim Patterson is?”
said my mate.
“He was pretty bad yesterday,”
said I.
Ted turned out of his stretcher and
yawned.
“I’ll trot up and see if he wants any-
thing,” said he.
“These chops are nearly done now,” said
I. “Give mine an extra touch of fire,” said
Ted. “You had ’em underdone last time.”
Then he put on his boots and went off-
Leaving his chop to sizzle, I set to work
to polish mine off. I was finishing up with
bread and marmalade when he returned.
“Chucked it,” said he.
“No!”
“Yes, uat out.”
I took the cleanest rug we had and went
up the track through the mulga to Patter-
son’s camp, a hessian affair with a bough
shade in front and a fireplace of rough boul-
ders close by. The dead man lay on his
stretcher before the door of his hut. My
mate had covered him with a blanket. He
had been a quiet man. His only comment
on his own illness had been a remark that
he’d either have to raise a sweat or “go out.”
I removed the blanket. Poor old Jim
had not been very'- handsome living, and death
had not improved hifn. I was glad to hide
him again under the rug. Ted came and
we tossed to see who should go to town to
tell the police. Ted won- He usually has
better luck than his mate.
“I’ll ride in on your bike,” he said. “I
wonder did the old boy have anything
worth pinching.” We had a look around.
The first thing we noticed was a bottle on
a ramshackle table.
“Hello!” said Ted. “Whisky?”
But after examination we let it alone. A
few clothes, mining tools, worn and bat-
tered books, an old bpx, pieces of specimen
quartz —that was all the hut contained.
“The heirs won’t fight over it,” said Ted.
“Don’t let anyone get down on that bottle.”
“I’ll need to get a gun, then,” said I.
He went off to the town, which was about
six miles away. I brought up a rifle, a
couple of books, and what tobacco I had-
Presently old Dan Moran came along.
“Is the man dead?” said Dan.
“He is that,” I answered.
The stretcher with the body on it lay
between me and Dan, and I was sprawled
before the door of the hut. Dan approached
the stretcher gingerly, and, pulling back the
rug, revealed the dead face.
“Augh,” he said, staring hard at it.
He seemed to ponder over the correct
thing to say, and was evidently unable to
decide. He pursed his lips, and looked from
the corpse to me, from me to the sky, and
back again at the ground. At last he
turned back the rug :
“Doid dam harrud, I dunnaw?” he haz-
arded in non-committal tones.
I gazed at him doubtfully and he became
uneasy. His eyes wandered past me and
presently his face lit up.
“Isn’t that whisky I see beyant ye there
inside?” he asked.
He was edging around the stretcher-
I put the rifle to my shoulder and sat bolt
upright.
“In the name of the King, stand back, I
commanded, in parade-ground voice.
“Phwat the divil” —-—
“I warn you, Patrick Moran”
“Me name’s not Pathrick!”
“More shame to your spopsors,” I cried,
“and you an Orangeman” -
“Me —me—an Orangeman !”
“An Irishman anyhow,” said I, “but let
me warn you to beware how you look at
that bottle. Beware of the V>ng arm of co-
incidence. Beware of the. might of the
Law!”
Dan peered at me in perplexity.
“Is it mad you ar-r?” stud he, and he
sidled out of the line of fire, “Moind that
gun,” he added, “like a good fella. It
moight explode unbeknownst.”
“There are more things in Heaven and
earth, Dan Moran, than are dreamt of in *
your philosophy 1 .”
“In me what?” said Dan, backing down
the track.
I lowered the rifle and he came a step
or two nearer. I moved the barrel in his
direction. He halted. He was thinking
hard.
“Dick, me b’y,” said he after a pause,
“that’s moy whisky. Lasteways, ’twas me
that gev it to poor ould Patherson.”
“Dan,” said I, “we’ve always been good
friends. Even when you blackguarded me
over the last crushing we did for you and
took your stone to Warty Bob’s mill, I kept
a soft spot in my heart for you. But let
me tell you this. Cer ain words cannot be
recalled. Don’t say them, Dan, don’t say
them.”
Dan removed his old Panama and scratched
amid his iron-grey wool.
“Huh !” he snorted at last, “ould Pather-
son’s defoonct. Didn’t Oi give ’urn the
shtuff when the poor divil couldn’t raise a
shweat, an’ now he’s thrun a siven. Un
what’s the good av me whisky to ’um now.
Dick, me b’y, a dhrap av the cratur at a
wake —a wake”
“You’d better go, Dan,’; said I, your
tongue’s getting you deeper in the bog.
After further argument he went off down
the track, looking back apprehensively every
now and then. I kept: him covered with
the rifle until he disappeared in the mulga.
In about half an hour Fred Martin ap-
peared on the scene. Fred is by calling a
beer destroyer, but he goes prospecting now
and then, by way of change. He had met
Moran and came to the point with brevity.
“I want that bottle o’ whisky I lent Pat-
terson before he died,” said he. .
“Lent — you lent him a botfle of whisky t
said I. ' , , ,
“Yes,” he asserted, “he was very bad, an
havin’ some down at me camp I let him
have it. O’ course he promised to pay me
back when he was able, ’n’ since he’s dead
the best way is for me to take me own
again.”
“Dan Moran said he gave him a bottle of
whisky.”
“Oh, Dan don’t baulk at a lie. Anyway
that bottle in there’s mine.” |
“You think he drank Dan’s.”
“I dunno. I want that bottle.”
“You can’t have it, Fred, not till the
cons come, at any rate.”
“Till the cops come!” he shouted wildly.
“What sort of a bloke are you? What show
will anyone ’ave of getting it from the cops?”
He made a sudden rush for the door, and
I only had time to punch him in the pit of
the stomach with the butt of the rifle. He
curled up in agony, and I dragged him out
into the track s and chased him away blas-
pheming and threatening.
By and by Frank Smith appeared.
“Have you been giving Patterson a bottle,
too?” I asked. “Patterson must have drunk
himself to death.”
“I believe,” said he suavely, “it’s not safe
to approach you. What have you been
doing to Moran and Martin?”
“Didn’t they tell you?” I asked.
“Oh, some tommy rot, of course. You
were quite right not to let them have that
whisky. Anyone’d know that Dan and
Fred’d do anything for drink. You were
quite right to stave ’em off.”
“.Glad you think so.”
“Yes. Now as a matter of simple fact
that whisky in there on the table was mine.
Poor old Patterson!”
He stooped, turned back the rug, and
looked at the face.
“Don’t look too nice, does he?” he re-
marked. “Yes,” he continued, replacing the
rug. “Last night it occurred to me that what
the poor chap wanted was a bottle of the
real stuff. ’Twas my last bottle, and for
reasons of hospitality I hate to be without
it. though, as far as my own tastes it
might lie for ever in the camp. Well, I
brought the bottle around in the moonlight.”
“There was no moon last night,” I re-
marked.
“I mean starlight, of course. He was
lying where he is now. I thought he was
dozing, and slipping gently in without dis-
turbing him put the bottle on the table —
where it is now.”
“It was kind of you,” said I.
“And, after all, the poor old chap never
touched it, it seems. So I might as well
take my bottle home with me. Eh! What?”
Smith likes to give you tlie idea that he
is a gentleman in reduced circumstances.
He also is endowed with a palpitating
thirst.
“Mr. Smith,” I said solemnly, “what poor
Patterson has done with -your whisky I
really don’t know. As for the bottle which
stands inside on the table, I hope—l hope
earnestly—that you have no concern with
it.”
“Come, come,” he said impatiently, but
politely; “whisky is whisky.”

“True,” I replied, “and there are things
that are not whisky—strychnine, for one.”
“Strychnine ! You mean —you can’t”- —--
“Could poor Patterson have an enemy?”
“An enemy?”
“That bottle contains something besides
alcohol.”
“Great Heavens!”
“You saw the poor chap’s face? If a
man were to swallow what I saw in that
bottle”
“Not —not”
“Let me speak plainly.”
I was about to enlarge on the subject
when I was assailed on both flanks. Martin
and Moran took me by surprise. There was
a brief rough-up. I was tumbled headlong
over a boulder. By the time I had disen-
gaged myself the bottle, Moran, Martin and
Frank Smith in that order were speeding
down the track.
“Don’t drink it, you fool,” Smith was
shouting. “Don’t drink it! It’s poison :
it’s poison.”
Moran heeded him no more than if he
were a temperance orator, but holding the
bottle carefully in front of him ran blindly
int > the arms of the policeman whom Ted
had brought from town. The policeman
gripped fast, and Moran struggled furiously
to retain his prize.
“Let me have me whisky; I want me
whisky,” he kept shouting.
I limped down the track to watch the
fight. The policeman overpowered Moran,
and took the bottle.
“What’s all the row about, Dick?” Ted
asked.
“Don’t leave me to watch a corpse again,”
said I. “I’ve had the fright of my life keep-
ing these men from carrying off that bottle.”
“Gimme me whisky,” moaned Moran,
wiping• the sweat from his face.
“’Tain’t his ; it’s” began Fred Mar-
tin. *
“Poison !” said Frank Smith.
The policeman held out the bottle which
by a miracle had not been broken. It con-
tained a fine specimen of mountain devil,
preserved in methylated spirit.

Tom Too-loose.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
r ri HE bare landscape wears a shimmer of
wavy heat, and the only shade cast
comes from the posts of the wire
fence. Every sort of life seems dead except-
ing the flies. They take a fiendish delight
in tormenting a man who is striding up and
down the red dust of the road, cursing a
horse. The horse stands stolidly - in the
shafts of a spring cart. He has his ears
laid back in a very sulky way, and the man
has creeks of perspiration furrowed down his
dusty face. Now and again he rushes at
the horse and kicks him in the ribs, and
then the horse’s ears lie further back and
the whites of his eyes sbr/w wickedly. It
is evident that the man and the horse are
not friendly.
Over the brow of the hill a head shows,
and then a body and legs, and the whole
combination reveals itself out of the waves
of heat as a swagman. When the swag-
man reaches the cart he looks critically at
the horse, and then inquiringly at the man,
and throws his swag down.
“ Jibbin’ ?” he demands.
“What do you think?” asks the driver,
with some show of hostility.
“I’d belt the ’ide off of ’im.”
“S’pose yer’d do more’n that, eh?”
“Well, if he wouldn’t go, I would. I’d
put a pertater under his tail.”
“Anything else yer’d do?”
“I’ve cured a jibbin’ horse by. turnin’ ’im
round and backin’ him up the ’ill. And, if
that won’t work, I’d light a fire under ’im.
Never know’d the fire to fail, only it makes
’em bolt. Still, I’d far rather a bolt than a
jib ennyday. But I’d far rather sell a jib-
bin’ ’orse. Now, I ’ad an ’orse wunst what,
useter jib, and”
The driver suddenly looks furious, andl
steps up to the swagman and plants his;
right shoulder against his, what time his
left fist works convulsively.
“Shut up, or I’ll stoush yer in the jore,”
he says.
The swagman shuts his fists and says:
“Oh, yer would, Would yer?”
“Yes, I would.”
“Oh, you are a lively sort of customer, you
are.”
“Too lively for you by ’alf. For two pins
I’d let yer stop one now.”
“You !”
“Yes, and glad ter do it. I’ve been waitin
two solid hours for a joker like you to come
along with some fool’s advice, and pretendin’
yer know more about ’orses that I do that
’ve driven ’em ever since I could ’old the-
reins. I wuz dyin’ to sail into somebody
just ter relieve me feelin’s.”
The swagman looks thoughtful, and says:
“After all, it’s no use gettin’ excited over a.
jibbin’ ’orse.” Then a bright idea strikes
him, “ ’Ere, try a smoke,” and he thrusts,
a plug of tobacco at the driver.
The driver bites some off instead, and
commences to chew rapidly. After three
spits he says:—
“Now yer begins to act civil like, I don’t
mind tellin’ yer that that ’orse has made a.
different man uv me. I was one of the most;
peaceablest men goin’ till I wuz given him
to drive. At school you couldn’t make me
fight. I’d rather run a mile than fight a
minute, as the sayin’ is, if you’ll understand.
And I growed up like that. Blokes always
said I was too quiet like. I didn’t mind
people gi'vin’ me advice when I first drove
the ’orse; it was the cruelty-to-animals
blokes that started me goin’. When I was
doin’ me best to get the swine goin’, up one
of them blokes would come, and take me
name and address, and call me a monster in
human form, and it fair brbke me up, and
I’d let him ’ave it if it cost me five pounds.
And, to my surprise, I discovered that I was
a bit of a pug, and that a good mill useter
make me feel good, and I always noticed that
old Tom here would start quite willin. after-
wards. It seemed as if he says to himself:
‘Fred isn’t a bad sort Of bloke to fight like
that, and I’ll do him a good turn.’ And I got
into the way of takin’ every opportunity for
a fight.” . ,
“Don’t wonder at it,” says the swngman.
“I’d do it meself if I wuz in your place.
“Oh, I don’t want ter fight. I only wish
there, wuz not the necessity. What
grieves me is the way the ’orse ’as Come to
make trouble in fambly affairs, and come
between me and my missus. I don t like to
talk of domestic matters, if you’ll follow me
meaning; but yer sees I’ve been sucb a dif-
ferent man at home with that ’orse on me
mind that the old girl begins ter think that
I’ve got another bit of skirt. No use tellin’
her it’s Tom Too-loose, but she waits till I
•gets ter sleep to search me pockets for love
letters and all that sort of thing, and exam-
inin’ me clothes to see if she can find enny
lloose hairs. She found one of the hairs of
'Tom’s tail on me, and now nothing will make
.’her believe but there is a girl —and she’s
ginger.” , .
“ ’Ard lines that,” says the sympathetic
;swagman. “Why don’t yer sell the ’orse?”
“The boss can’t. He’s tried. Nobody
'would buy ’im. They say he useter be a race-
’orse wunst till he mauled the stable boys
about so much, and laid down with the
jockeys, till everybody wuz afraid of him.
'They useter call ’im Tom Too-Loose, though I
don’t know why they put the Too-loose
to it. I think they should have called him
Deeming, or Frank Butler. A bloke owed
the boss £27, and, as ,he couldn’t pay it, he
sent the ’orse round. Yer see, the boss ’ad
ter accept ’im, though, if yer asks me, the
boss should ’ave written the debt off and re-
fused the ’orse. It would ’ave paid ’im.
“Everybody knows ’im so. much that he’s
become the joke of the town. We did try
to make a deal with the new par-
son, and it nearly come off, _ only the
parson would drive himself, and insisted on
bavin’ a whip. Well, I didn’t tell yer that
old Tom Too-loose has a derry on whips.
W'on’t stand ’em at enny price. When you
use a whip he just starts to kick, and doesn’t
leave off till there’s no vehicle behind him
ter kick at. So. instead o’ sellin’ the ’orse,
the boss ’ad his best buggy kicked ter pieces,
and, to make matters worse, the parson and
all the women at the church thinks the boss
i is past prayin’ for. try in’ to sell him an
’orse like that. The boss useter go ter
church regular before that, and now he goes
shootin’ with the ungodly every Sunday
mornin’.
“You never see such a derry as that ’orse
’as on whips. We never use a whip, to him.
I always carried a sliprail to belt him with
till tiie cruelty-to-animals blokes sum-
moned me too much, and I ’ad ter give
' :it up. If Tom sees a bloke carryin’ a whip
in his ’and he goes for him like a bull ter-
rier, and a mouth as vicious-lookin’ as a
tiger. The boss’s little Willy is a nice little
kiddy with long flaxen hair, and everybody’s
favorite, and he was the only person Tom
Too-loose seemed to like. The nipper could
crawl all over him. But one day somebody
bought Willie a toy whip with a whistle in
the handle. And Willy went crackin’ his
whip down by the stables, and next thing we
knew was that the horse was carryin’ ’im
round the yard by the ’air. The missus
said the boss would have to get his gun and
shoot ’im, and I never saw the boss so wild
in me life. I didn’t know he knew so many
oaths. He said, ‘l’ll sell that ’orse if I
only get a pound for ’im.”
“But, Lord love yer, yer couldn’t sell ’im
for love of money. We had him in auction,
but the blokes spent all their time a-crackin’
whips at ’im for fun, and it put an end to
the sale. Then we thought we’d let ’im
roam about the streets and get pounded, and
never claim him, but the poundkeeper
wouldn’t go near him. S’pose he was
afraid. Then we put him out to grass
for life. The place was 10 miles
away, and, cornin’ home, I stopped
at the ’Alf-way House for a game of euchre
or two, and, blow me kite high, but if Tom
wasn’t ’ome before me.
“Yer mentioned a little while ago about
backin’ ’im. Well, if you’ll understand me,
Tom won’t back. Nothing will make im
back. If you try to make im back, he
catches you in his mouth and bolts. It s
the only way to make him go forward, but
he won’t go forward till ’e has a good holt on
you, and then he lets yer drop so’s the wheels
’ll go over you. I remember he jibbed once
and stopped all the traffic, and a bloke said
to me. ‘Why don’t you back him? And 1
said, ‘He won’t back, mate.’ And he said,
‘Blime, there’s not an ’orse I cant
make back.’ And he jumps ofiE his lorry and
grabs Tom be the ’ead. I suppose you 11 say
that I shouldn’t ’ave let ’im, knowin the
’orse as well as I do, but I was worked up
that way that it was just what I wanted. I
wanted ter see the wheels go over that man,
so’s the boss would be compelled to send Tom
Too-loose to the boilin’-down works. But me
luck was out. Tom just dropped him an inch
too close in, and the wheels only grazed a bit
of skin off his ’ip.
“I tried lightin’ a fire under him when he
jibbed one day at Sandy C-rik, but do yer
think he’d shift? Not a bit of it. You d
think he had been readin lox s Book ot
Martyrs,’ and thought he was Cranmer at
the stake, and I ’ad ter knock the fire away
to save his life.” . n T)J ,
“Wonder you knocked it away ? I d ave
let im burn.” .
“So would I if I ’ad been a single man, but
who’d ’ave looked after the wife and kiddies
while I was doin’ time?”
The swagman shakes his head wisely, and
says “That’s true.” , , .
“D’ye play euchre?” asks the driver, pull-
ing a pack of cards from his pocket.
“A bit.” .
“Let’s ’ave a game to pass the time away.
They sit down on a small bank and cut for
deal.
“Make it a bob a game to give an in-
terest,” says the driver.
“I’ve not got enny bobs about me, says
the swagman.
The driver gives a mysterious side glance
at Tom Too-loose and whispers: “It’s only a
bit of kid. Don’t look at the ’orse. You see
if he doesn’t go as soon as he sees I’m gettin’
interested. I don’t care whether he goes or
not. D’you pass?”
“Yes.”
“So do I.”
“I’ll make it clubs.”
“It’s your play.”
Tom Too-loose loses his sulky attitude and
pricks up his ears as he watches the game
progress, and they hardly finish the third
hand before he suddenly starts, off at a trot.
The driver bounds after and springs over
the tailboard.
“Yer fergettin’ the cards,” shouts the
swagman.
“It’s all right. I ’aven’t got time to wait,”
shouts the driver, and a minute later the cart
disappears over the _
